Understanding the Basmala: Claims and Terminology
The commentator analyzes the author's opening with basmala and hamdala and defines 'basmala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three claims implied in the phrase 'he began with the basmala then the hamdala' and the definition of the term 'basmala' as a verbal noun and its metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
The basmala is more than a phrase—it carries three logical claims and a rich terminological history.

In this passage, the commentators unpack the author's opening phrase. They reveal that it contains three implicit claims about what was said and in what order. Then they delve into the linguistic origin of the word 'basmala' itself, showing how it evolved from an action to a customary term for the sacred formula. This lesson will walk you through both parts of their analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three claims
Read the passage carefully to find the three claims that the phrase 'he began with the basmala then the hamdala' implies. Notice what is being asserted about the author's action.
The claims are about which words were used, their combination, and their order.
2
Understand the evidence for 'began'
Look at how the commentator explains what it means to 'begin with the basmala' in speech and writing, and what evidence supports each.
One evidence is observation, the other is a general habit.
3
Learn the linguistic definition of 'basmala'
Focus on the etymology of 'basmala' as a verbal noun. See how it is derived from the verb 'yasmal' and what it originally meant.
It is compared to 'dahraja' to show its pattern.
4
Recognize the metaphorical extension
Understand how the word 'basmala' came to be used for the phrase itself rather than the act, and how it became a customary literal term.
This is an important shift in meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم ابتدأ بالبسملة ثم بالحمدلة. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري .
(قوله: ابْتَدَأَ بِالْبَسْمَلَةِ ثُمَّ الْحَمْدَلَةِ اشتمل كلامُهُ على ثلاث دعاوى: الإتيان بهما
1
دون غيرهما كـ سبحان الله والجمع بينهما، وتقديم البسملة على الحمدلة.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: ابْتَدَأَ بِالْبَسْمَلَةِ) أي: نُطقاً وكتابةً، أما الثاني؛ فدليله المشاهدة، وأما الأول؛ فدليله أن من كتب شيئاً تلفظ به غالباً.
والبسملة مصدر قياسي لـ(يَسْمَلَ كَدَخرَجَ دَحْرَجَةً إِذا قال: بسم الله ... إلخ على ما في "الصحاح"، أو إذا كتبها على ما في تهذيب الأزهري، فهو بمعنى القول أو الكتابة، لكن أطلقوها على نفس: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم مجازاً من إطلاق المصدر على المفعول لعلاقة اللزوم، ثم صارت حقيقةً عرفية.
His saying: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, he began with the basmala then the hamdala.' The hashia of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri. (His saying: 'He began with the basmala then the hamdala') His words include three claims: bringing them both and not others like 'Subhanallah', combining them, and preceding the basmala over the hamdala. The taqrirat of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. (His saying: 'He began with the basmala') i.e., verbally and in writing. As for the second, its evidence is observation. As for the first, its evidence is that whoever writes something usually says it. And 'basmala' is a normative verbal noun from 'yasmal' like 'dahraja' when one says 'bismillah...' according to what is in al-Sihah, or when one writes it according to Tahdhib al-Azhari. So it means speech or writing, but they applied it to the phrase 'bismillah al-rahman al-rahim' metaphorically by using the verbal noun for the object due to the relationship of necessity, then it became a customary literal meaning.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's opening. The commentator Ajhuri notes that Bajuri said 'He began with the basmala then the hamdala.' Then Anbabi expands: He says this statement implies three claims: (1) that the author began with both basmala and hamdala (rather than something else like 'Subhanallah'), (2) that he combined them together (not just one), and (3) that he placed the basmala before the hamdala. Then Anbabi explains the meaning of 'began with the basmala' as both verbally and in writing. He gives evidence: for writing, it is observed; for speech, it is that whoever writes something usually says it. Then he defines 'basmala' as a verbal noun derived from 'yasmal' like 'dahraja' is derived from 'dahraja' (to say 'dahraj'). The word originally meant the act of saying or writing 'bismillah', but then it was applied metaphorically to the actual phrase itself, and later became a customary literal term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Basmala means only the phrase itself.
Correction: Originally, 'basmala' referred to the act of saying or writing the phrase; it was later applied to the phrase itself metaphorically, then became customary.

Checkpoint

B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'he began with the basmala then the hamdala' contains three logical claims, and the term 'basmala' has a nuanced linguistic origin as a verbal noun that evolved into a customary name for the formula.

?

Definition and Derivation of Basmala
Bajuri defines 'basmala' as a verbal noun and explains its linguistic origin and usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origin of the term 'basmala', its status as a verbal noun, and how it evolved to refer to the phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim'.

Core Idea
'Basmala' originally means the act of saying 'Bismillah', not the phrase itself.

This lesson unpacks the linguistic history of a key Islamic technical term: 'basmala'. You'll see how a word that originally described an action—saying or writing 'Bismillah'—came to be used as the name of that very phrase. Understanding this helps you appreciate how Islamic scholarly terminology develops.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal English translation. Notice how Bajuri introduces 'basmala' as a 'masdar', or verbal noun, and how he uses analogies and references to authoritative lexicons.
2
Identify the core linguistic point
Notice that 'basmala' is derived from the verb 'yasmilu' and means the act of saying or writing the phrase. This is the original meaning.
Bajuri mentions two sources: al-Sihah and Tahdhib al-Azhari.
3
Understand the metaphorical shift
Consider how the term was then applied metaphorically to the phrase itself (the object of the action) due to the close relationship between the action and its object. Finally, this usage became so common that it is now a 'conventional reality' (haqiqa 'urfiyya).
This illustrates a common process in the development of technical terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبسملة مصدر قياسي لـ(يَسْمَلَ كَدَخرَجَ دَحْرَجَةً إِذا قال: بسم الله ... إلخ على ما في 'الصحاح'، أو إذا كتبها على ما في تهذيب الأزهري، فهو بمعنى القول أو الكتابة، لكن أطلقوها على نفس: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم مجازاً من إطلاق المصدر على المفعول لعلاقة اللزوم، ثم صارت حقيقةً عرفيةً.
And 'basmala' is a regular verbal noun from 'yasmilu' like 'dakhraja dakhrajatan' when he says 'Bismillah...' etc. according to what is in al-Sihah, or when he writes it according to what is in Tahdhib al-Azhari, so it means saying or writing. However, they applied it to the phrase itself 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' metaphorically, by applying the verbal noun to the object (maf'ul) due to the relation of entailment, then it became a conventional reality ('urfiyyah).
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'basmala'. Bajuri explains its linguistic derivation: it is a verbal noun (masdar) from the verb 'yasmilu', analogous to 'dakhraja' giving 'dakhrajah'. Its meaning is either the act of saying the phrase or writing it, depending on the authoritative lexicon. Initially, 'basmala' was used for the action (saying or writing). Then by metaphorical extension (majaz), it was applied to the phrase itself (the object), based on the close association (luzum) between the action and its object. Over time this usage became so common that it is now a conventional reality, i.e., the primary meaning for most speakers.

Key Terms
بسملة
مصدر
حقيقة عرفية
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' itself
B) The act of saying or writing that phrase
C) A type of prayer
D) A chapter of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Basmala linguistically refers to the action of saying 'Bismillah', but through metaphorical extension it became the conventional name for the phrase itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Explanation of 'Abtar' as Tashbih Baligh
Grammatical classification of 'fa-huwa abtar' as tashbih baligh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'fa-huwa abtar' is classified as tashbih baligh and what that means for interpretation.

Core Idea
Tashbih baligh omits both the tool and the point of similarity, keeping words literal.

This commentary clarifies a grammatical feature of the hadith phrase 'fa-huwa abtar'. It tells us that the phrase is not a metaphorical or hyperbolic statement, but an example of eloquent simile (tashbih baligh), where the comparison tool and the aspect of similarity are hidden. Understanding this helps us interpret the hadith correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the attribution to 'the majority' of scholars.
2
Define tashbih baligh
Identify the two elements that are omitted in tashbih baligh: the tool of comparison (like 'ka') and the point of similarity. Understand how this definition applies to the phrase.
The definition is given in the second half of the passage.
3
Consider the consequence
Reflect on what it means that the words 'abtar' and 'aqta'' remain on their original meanings. How does this affect the interpretation of the hadith?
This prevents the reader from taking the phrase as a hyperbolic metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فَهُوَ أَبْتَرُ... إلخ) هو عند الجمهور من باب التشبيه البليغ، وهو ما حذفت منه الأداة ووجه الشبه، وعلى هذا فالأبتر وما بعده باقية على معانيها الأصلية. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
(His saying: 'it is abtar... etc.') according to the majority, it is of the category of tashbih baligh (eloquent simile), which is that from which the tool (of comparison) and the point of similarity are omitted, and on this basis the words 'abtar' and what follow remain on their original meanings. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical commentary on the phrase 'fa-huwa abtar' in the hadith. The commentator states that according to the majority of scholars, this is an example of tashbih baligh (eloquent simile). In this type of simile, the particle of comparison (like 'ka') and the point of similarity are omitted. Therefore, the words 'abtar' (curtailed), 'aqta'' (cut off), and 'ajdham' (maimed) retain their literal meanings without needing a figurative interpretation. This explains that the hadith is not using these words metaphorically but as a direct description of the deficiency of the action.

Key Terms
تشبيه بليغ
أبتر
أداة التشبيه
وجه الشبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith phrase 'fa-huwa abtar' is a literal judgment that someone becomes physically curtailed or cut off.
Correction: The commentary explains it is an example of tashbih baligh, where the comparison is implied and the words retain their original meanings, so it is a figurative description emphasizing the severity of severing kinship ties, not a literal physical alteration.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what kind of simile is 'fa-huwa abtar'?
A) Tashbih mursal (free simile).
B) Tashbih baligh (eloquent simile).
C) Tashbih mufassal (detailed simile).
D) Tashbih mujmal (vague simile).

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'fa-huwa abtar' is an eloquent simile, meaning the tool and point of comparison are omitted, and the words retain their literal meanings to emphasize the severity of severing ties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage emphasizes that 'abtar' retains its literal meaning of being curtailed or cut off. If you truly believe that severing a family tie makes you spiritually curtailed before Allah, what specific relationship are you least willing to mend, and why is your heart resisting that step?
?
The hadith calls the one who breaks kinship ties 'abtar' — and the commentary insists this is not mere exaggeration but a literal description. As you examine your own life, where have you allowed distance or coldness to replace the love Allah commands, and what concrete act of reconciliation will you perform before the end of this week?
?
If the words of Allah's Messenger carry such weight that a single descriptive word like 'abtar' is preserved with its full literal force, how does this recalibrate your urgency to obey his command to uphold family ties, even with those who have wronged you?

Analyzing 'La Yubda' Fihi'
Syntactic and semantic analysis of 'la yubda' fihi' as a second adjective and the function of 'fihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syntactic and semantic analysis of the phrase 'la yubda' fihi' in the hadith, including its role as a second adjective and the causal meaning of 'fihi'.

Core Idea
The phrase 'la yubda' fihi' emphasizes that the important matter itself should motivate beginning with God's name.

This passage delves into the precise wording of the hadith, showing how classical scholars parsed its grammar to extract deeper meaning. The commentary highlights two key points: the grammatical role of 'la yubda' as an adjectival clause and the nuanced meaning of 'fihi' as indicating causation. Understanding these details enriches our appreciation of the hadith's message.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the two main points: the grammatical analysis of 'la yubda' and the explanation of 'fihi'.
The passage is short but dense with grammatical terminology.
2
Understand the first point: second adjective
Identify how 'la yubda' is described as a second adjective (sifa thaniya) that is a clausal adjective (na't bil-jumla). Notice that it comes after a singular adjective, and this order is considered better.
This grammatical analysis clarifies the structure of the phrase.
3
Analyze the word 'fihi'
Understand that 'fihi' here does not mean 'in it' literally. The particle 'fi' indicates causation, so 'fihi' means 'because of it' or 'due to it'. This emphasizes that the important matter is the cause for beginning with basmala.
This semantic nuance is crucial for the intended meaning.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this analysis matters: it shows that the hadith teaches us to begin important matters with basmala because of the importance of the matter itself, not just as a routine habit.
Connects grammar to spiritual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لَا يُبْدَأُ) صفةٌ ثانية لأمر من باب النعت بالجملة بعد النعت بالمفرد، وهو أحسن من عكسه. (قوله فيه) أي: بسببه، وفائدة الإتيان بـ (في) الدالة على السببية: إفادة أن المطلوب: كَوْنُ الأمر ذي البال سبباً باعثاً على التسمية في ابتدائه، لا مطلق وقوع التسمية في ابتدائه ولو بسبب آخر بحيث يكون هو غير منظور إليه عند التسمية.
(His saying: 'one does not begin') is a second adjective for 'matter' (amr) from the category of adjectival clause after the adjective in the singular, and that is better than the opposite. (His saying: 'therein' (fihi)) i.e., because of it, and the benefit of bringing the particle 'fi' indicating causation is to convey that what is required is that the important matter be a cause motivating the naming [i.e., saying basmala] in its beginning, not merely the occurrence of the naming in its beginning even if due to another cause so that [the matter] is not regarded at the time of naming.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and semantic commentary on the hadith wording. First, it analyzes 'la yubda' (one does not begin) as a second adjective (sifa thaniya) for the noun 'amr' (matter). The adjective is a clause that follows a single-word adjective, and this order is considered better than the reverse. Second, it examines the word 'fihi' (therein). The commentator explains that 'fi' here indicates causation (sababiyya), meaning 'because of it' or 'due to it'. The intended meaning is that the important matter itself should be the cause that prompts one to begin with the basmala, not merely that the basmala happens to occur at the start for some other reason, in which case the matter would not be the focus during the naming. This emphasises the direct connection between the matter and the act of beginning with God's name.

Key Terms
صفة
نعت بالجملة
فيه
سببية
ابتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'fihi' simply means 'in it' in a locative sense, referring to the phrase.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that 'fi' here indicates causation, so 'fihi' means 'because of it' (the important matter), emphasizing causality rather than location.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the word 'fi' in 'la yubda' fihi' according to the commentary?
A) It indicates location.
B) It indicates time.
C) It indicates causation.
D) It indicates accompaniment.

Key Takeaway
Careful grammatical analysis of 'la yubda' fihi' reveals that the hadith teaches us to begin important matters with basmala because of the importance of the matter itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly feel that the importance of a task drives you to say 'Bismillah', or do you say it out of habit without connecting it to the matter at hand? What would change if you consciously paused before each task to let its significance prompt you to invoke God?
?
Think of the last time you began something without saying basmala. Was it because you considered it trivial? What does that reveal about your assumption that some matters are too small for God's involvement? How can you train yourself to see every action as important enough to begin with His name?
?
If you were to take this lesson literally—that the matter itself should be the cause (fihi) for basmala—how would it change your daily routine? Will you commit to linking each new task with a deliberate basmala, allowing the task's significance to bring God to mind?

Regular Derivation of Resembling Adjectives
Morphological analysis of three adjectives as ṣifāt mushabbaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three words abtar, aqta', and ajdham follow a standard grammatical pattern for resembling adjectives derived from intransitive verbs with a broken middle radical.

Core Idea
These words are not irregular; they follow a standard pattern.

In this short grammatical note, the commentator clarifies the morphological nature of three adjectives found in a hadith. Many readers might assume these words are irregular or anomalous, but the commentator points out that they are actually regular resembling adjectives (ṣifāt mushabbaha) derived according to a consistent rule. This lesson unpacks that rule and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms used.
Pay attention to the terms 'ṣifa mushabbaha', 'fiʿl lāzim', and 'maksūrat al-ʿayn'.
2
Identify the parts of the explanation
The passage states: (1) these words are resembling adjectives, (2) they are derived from intransitive verbs, (3) those verbs have a broken 'ayn (middle radical), and (4) the pattern 'afʿalu is regular (qiyāsī) for such adjectives.
This is a single coherent claim, not a list of independent points.
3
Understand the grammatical significance
Recognize that the commentator is preempting a potential objection: these forms might appear unusual but are in fact standard. This reflects the precision of Arabic morphology.
The word 'qiyāsī' means the pattern is analogical and predictable.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider how this grammatical detail clarifies the text and shows the commentator’s attention to linguistic accuracy.
This reinforces the reliability of the transmitted wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فَهُوَ أَبْتَرُ... إلخ) أَجْذَمُ وابتر وأقطعُ صِفَات مشبَّهَةٌ مَصُوغَةٌ من أفعال لازمةٍ مكسورة العين؛ ليكون صَوْعُ الصفةِ المشبَّهة التي على أفعل منها قياسياً.
(His saying: 'it is abtar... etc.') 'ajdham', 'abtar', and 'aqta'' are resembling adjectives (sifat mushabbaha) derived from intransitive verbs with a broken 'ayn (middle radical); so that the formation of the resembling adjective on the pattern 'af'alu from them is regular.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a morphological analysis of the three adjectives in the hadith: abtar, aqta', and ajdham. The commentator explains that these are صفات مشبهة (resembling adjectives, a type of adjective in Arabic grammar). They are derived from intransitive verbs that have a kasra (broken) on the middle radical. This derivation pattern (af'alu) is regular and standard for such adjectives. The comment ensures the reader understands that these words are not irregular or strange, but follow a consistent grammatical rule.

Key Terms
صفة مشبهة
أفعل
فعل لازم
مكسورة العين
قياسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The words 'abtar', 'aqta'', and 'ajdham' are irregular or unusual Arabic forms.
Correction: They are actually regular (qiyāsī) resembling adjectives derived from intransitive verbs with a broken middle radical, following the standard 'af'alu pattern.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, from what type of verb are 'abtar', 'aqta'', and 'ajdham' derived?
A) Transitive verbs with fatha on the 'ayn.
B) Intransitive verbs with a broken 'ayn (kasra).
C) Passive verbs with damma on the 'ayn.
D) Verbs with a long vowel.

Key Takeaway
The words 'abtar', 'aqta'', and 'ajdham' are standard resembling adjectives following the regular 'af'alu pattern.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Understanding Dhu Bal: Conditions for Important Acts
Sanusi defines 'dhu bal' and lists its three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'dhu bal' and the three conditions that determine an act's importance in Shari'a.

Core Idea
An act is important in Shari'a only if it is valued by Allah, not inherently sinful, and not trivial.

Before we can apply the practice of beginning important acts with basmala, we must know what makes an act 'important' in the eyes of Shari'a. Sanusi's matn provides a precise definition with clear conditions. In this lesson, we unpack this definition and see how it guides our daily actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the style: a definition followed by a commentator's remark.
The commentator Al-Ajhuri clarifies a nuance.
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage lists three conditions for an act to be considered 'dhu bal'. Find each one. They are: importance by Shari'a, not inherently prohibited/disliked, and not trivial.
The phrase 'لا يكون محرما ولا مكروها لذاتهما' is key.
3
Reflect on the commentator's suggestion
Al-Ajhuri suggests that 'by custom' might better exclude trivial matters. Think about why custom could be more comprehensive than Shari'a in this context.
This shows the interplay between divine law and human custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذي بال صاحب حال يهتم به شرعا بحيث لا يكون محرما ولا مكروها لذاتهما، ولا من سفاسف الأمور - أي الأمور الخسيسة. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: يهتم به شرعا) هذا بيان للحال، والضمير في يهتم به راجع للأمر، وكان الأولى أن يقول: وعرفا؛ ليظهر إخراج ما هو من سفاسف الأمور بقوله: (ذي بال).
Dhu bal: possessor of a situation that is given importance by the Shari'a, such that it is not inherently prohibited (ḥarām) nor inherently disliked (makrūh), and not among trivial matters (safāsif al-umūr) - i.e., base matters. Gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī: (His saying: 'that is given importance by the Shari'a') this is an explanation of the condition, and the pronoun in 'it is given importance' refers to the matter, and it would have been better to say: 'and by custom' to show the exclusion of what is from trivial matters by his saying: (dhū bāl).
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'dhū bāl' (possessor of importance) as a condition for acts that should be started with the basmala. The definition includes three elements: (1) the act must be deemed important by Shari'a (not just custom), (2) it must not be inherently prohibited or disliked (i.e., its prohibition is intrinsic), and (3) it must not be a trivial matter. The commentator notes that the phrase 'by Shari'a' might more appropriately be 'by custom' to better exclude trivial matters, as custom determines what is considered base or unimportant.

Misconception: Some think any act of personal importance qualifies as 'dhu bal' for basmala.
Correction: The definition specifies that the importance must be from Shari'a, not merely personal or customary, and excludes prohibited, disliked, and trivial acts.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for an act to be considered 'dhu bal'?
A) It must be important by Shari'a.
B) It must not be inherently prohibited or disliked.
C) It must be a recommended act (mandub).
D) It must not be among trivial matters.

Key Takeaway
An act is 'dhu bal' only if it is given importance by Shari'a, not inherently prohibited/disliked, and not trivial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson calls you to reevaluate: will you continue to invoke Allah's name on actions that are not worthy of His honor?
?
The lesson teaches that an act must not be inherently prohibited or disliked to be 'dhu bal'. Is there a sin you habitually commit while still saying 'Bismillah' before it? Feel the weight of that contradiction. What will you do to stop using Allah's name on what He has forbidden?
?
Al-Ajhuri noted that custom determines triviality. In a world that glorifies status, wealth, and entertainment, how many 'safāsif al-umūr' have you elevated to importance? This lesson demands you realign your priorities with the Shari'a. What trivial pursuit will you abandon today to make room for what truly matters in the Hereafter?

Defining 'al-Ism' and Its Relation to the Named
Al-Bajuri defines 'ism' and clarifies its relation to the named via two referents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological definition of 'ism' (name), its two possible etymologies, and how the word 'ism' can refer either to the divine names or to the divine essence itself, affecting whether it is considered identical to or other than the named.

Core Idea
The term 'ism' in theology is a mark that elevates its named, and its referent determines identity.

Before we dive into the divine names, we need a clear understanding of what 'ism' (name) means in this theological context. Al-Bajuri distinguishes it from the grammatical term and provides two beautiful etymologies. He then clarifies a potential confusion: when we say 'ism' in the Basmala, what exactly are we referring to? This passage sets the stage for later discussions on the divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'ism'
Read the first sentence where Al-Bajuri defines 'ism' as "that which indicates a named thing" and excludes the grammatical sense. Note the two etymologies that follow.
The definition is broad: any word that indicates a meaning, not just nouns.
2
Understand the two etymologies
Identify the two roots: 'sumuww' (elevation) and 'sima' (mark). Reflect on how each relates to the function of a name.
The name elevates the named by making it known, and it serves as a mark for recognition.
3
Explore the two referents of 'ism' in the Basmala
Read the commentary section starting from 'And from the aforementioned definition it is understood...' Follow the two possibilities: 'ism' as the specific names (Allah, Rahman, etc.) or 'ism' as the essence itself.
This distinction is crucial for later discussions on whether the divine names are identical to or other than the essence.
4
Analyze the implications
Consider how each interpretation affects the relationship between 'ism' and 'musamma'. See how the judgment of identity or difference depends on the referent.
This resolves apparent contradictions when the term 'ism' is used in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الاسم: وهو ما دَلُّ على مسمّى، لا ما قابَلَ الفعل والحرف؛ لأن ذلك اصطلاح نحوي. وهو مشتق من السُّمُو بمعنى: العُلُوّ؛ لأنه يَعْلُو مسمّاه، أو من السَّمَةِ بمعنى: العلامة؛ لأنه علامة عليه. وعلم من التعريف المذكور: حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: يَعْلُو مُسَمَّاهُ) (مسماه) فاعل (يعلو)، ولو قال: يعلو به مسماه؛ لكان أوضح. (قوله: وَعُلِمَ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ... إلخ) حاصل ذلك : أن لفظ (اسم) الواقع في البسملة مثلاً؛ إما أن يراد به الأسماء الدالة على الذات الأقدس بأن يستعمل فيها، وتكون هي مدلولة له، فلفظ (اسم) غير المسمى، وهو الذات باعتبار مدلوله، وهو الأسماء المذكورة، وإما أن يراد بلفظ (اسم) الذات الأقدس، فهو حينئذ عين المسمى باعتبار مدلوله، وهو الذات، فمدلوله على الوجه الأول غير الذات، وذلك الغير هو الأسماء الدالة على تلك الذات، وعلى الثاني نفس الذات، وبهذا علم أن المحكوم عليه بأنه غير المسمى على القول الأول، وأنه عين المسمى على المقابل له مدلول لفظ الاسم.
And the second: al-ism (the name): it is that which indicates a named thing (musamma), not that which contrasts with verb and particle; because that is a grammatical convention. And it is derived from al-sumuww meaning elevation; because it elevates its named thing, or from al-sima meaning the mark; because it is a mark upon it. And from the aforementioned definition it is understood: [Gloss of al-Ajhuri] (His saying: 'it elevates its named thing') ('its named thing') is the subject of 'elevates', and if he had said: 'it elevates by it its named thing', it would be clearer. (His saying: 'And from the definition it is understood...etc.') The summary of that is: the word (ism) occurring in the Basmala, for example, is either intended to mean the names indicating the Most Holy Essence (al-dhat al-aqdas) by being used in them, and they are its reference, so the word (ism) is other than the named, which is the Essence (al-dhat) in terms of its reference, and that other is the mentioned names; or it is intended by the word (ism) to be the Most Holy Essence itself, so it is then identical with the named in terms of its reference, which is the Essence. So its reference in the first aspect is other than the Essence, and that other is the names indicating that Essence, and in the second aspect it is the Essence itself. And by this it is known that what is judged to be other than the named on the first view, and identical with the named on the opposing view, is the reference of the word 'ism' (name).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ism' (name) as used in the context of discussing the divine names, distinguishing it from the grammatical term. Al-Bajuri first gives the definition: 'it is that which indicates a named thing (musamma).' Then he provides two etymologies: from sumuww (elevation) because it elevates the named, or from sima (mark) because it is a mark. The commentary by al-Ajhuri then clarifies the relationship between the word 'ism' and the named (musamma). He explains that when we say 'ism' in the Basmala, it can be understood in two ways: either it refers to the names of Allah (like Allah, Rahman, Rahim) which indicate the Essence, in which case the word 'ism' is other than the Essence; or it refers to the Essence itself, in which case 'ism' is identical with the Essence. The key point is that the judgment of whether 'ism' is identical with or other than the named depends on what is taken as its reference. This explanation resolves potential confusion when the term 'ism' is used in different contexts.

Correction: Al-Bajuri explicitly excludes that grammatical sense; 'ism' here means any word that indicates a meaning, used as a sign for the named.

C) The attribute and the action

Key Takeaway
The theological term 'ism' is a sign that elevates its referent, and whether it is identical to or other than the named depends on what it actually refers to.

The Name is Other Than the Named
Sanusi states the tahqiq that the name is other than the named, and al-Ujhuri explains the reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct theological position that the name (ism) is distinct from the named (musamma) and why this distinction is essential for proper discourse about Allah.

Core Idea
The name is not the same as what it names—this is the verified view in Islamic theology.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational linguistic-theological distinction from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin. The question of whether the name of a thing is identical to the thing itself has implications for how we speak about Allah. Sanusi clarifies that the correct view (al-tahqiq) is that the name is other than the named, and al-Ujhuri explains why this usage aligns with the original linguistic convention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a matn statement followed by a commentary.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key claim
The matn states 'أنه غيرُ المسمى' — that the name is other than the named. This is the core assertion.
The phrase 'وهو التحقيق' marks this as the verified position.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Ujhuri explains that the word 'ism' here refers to the names that denote the divine essence, which are clearly distinct from the essence itself.
This distinction is based on the original linguistic coinage (wad').
4
Reflect on why it is called 'al-tahqiq'
The position is the verification because using 'ism' to mean the names (not the essence) follows its original meaning. The alternative would be a metaphorical usage.
5
Consider the implication
When we say 'Allah' or any divine name, the word is not identical to the divine essence but refers to it. This preserves precise theological language.
This distinction prevents literal identification of created speech with the uncreated essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي أنه غيرُ المسمى، وهو التحقيق. (وقوله: وَهُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ) يعني أن التحقيق أن المراد بلفظ الاسم: الأسماء الدالة على الذات الأقدس، ومعلوم أنها غيرُ الذات، وإنما كان هذا القول التحقيق؛ لأن لفظ الاسم حينئذ يكون مستعملاً فيما وضع له، خلاف ما إذا أريد به الذات.
Text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu 'Abd Allah al-Sanusi: 'That it is other than the named, and that is the verification.' (His saying: 'and that is the verification') means that the verification is that what is intended by the word 'name' is the names which indicate the most holy essence, and it is known that they are other than the essence, and this statement is the verification only because the word 'name' then is used in what it was coined for, contrary to when the essence is intended by it.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the matn (core text) of al-Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin stating the correct position (al-tahqiq) that the name (al-ism) is other than the named (al-musamma). The hashiya by al-Ujhuri then explains that the verification means that the word 'ism' is intended to refer to the names that denote the divine essence, which are clearly distinct from the essence itself. The reason this is the correct view (tahqiq) is that when 'ism' is used in this way, it is used according to its original linguistic convention (wad'), unlike when it is used to mean the essence itself. Thus, the passage establishes the foundational linguistic distinction between the word and the thing it signifies.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
التحقيق
الذات الأقدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name of something is identical to the thing itself (e.g., saying 'Allah' means the word and the essence are the same).
Correction: The tahqiq (verified position) holds that the name is other than the named; the word 'Allah' is a sign referring to the divine essence, not the essence itself.

Checkpoint

A) The name is identical to the named.
B) The name is other than the named.
C) The relationship depends on context.
D) There is no distinction between them.

Key Takeaway
In Sunni theology, the name (ism) is distinct from the named (musamma), ensuring precise language about Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah' in prayer or supplication, do you ever slip into thinking that the sound of the word itself is sacred rather than the Being it points to? How can you train your heart to always mean the Essence, not the utterance?
?
If the name is not the named, then every time you repeat a divine name, you are using a tool to direct your mind to its Owner. Is your remembrance of Allah actually reaching Him, or does it stop at the word? What would it take to make your tongue a true bridge to the Divine?
?
The verified position says that using 'ism' for the names is the original linguistic convention. Do you use the names of Allah carelessly, as if they were ordinary words? How would your speech change if you truly believed that each name is only a signpost, not the destination?

Resolving the Name vs. Named Debate: Al-Ujhuri's Clarification
Al-Ujhuri elaborates on the resolution and concludes that the word 'ism' is other than the essence by agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classical debate over whether the name is identical to the named is resolved by distinguishing the different meanings of the word 'ism', and why the word itself is agreed to be distinct from the essence.

Core Idea
The word 'ism' is not the essence; the debate is about different uses of the term.

In this passage, al-Ujhuri clarifies the resolution of a key theological-linguistic dispute: is the name identical to the named? He shows that the disagreement dissolves once we recognize that 'ism' can refer either to the word (the composite of letters) or to the essence itself. Everyone agrees the word is other than the essence; the debate only arises when 'ism' is used ambiguously.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the key distinction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Ujhuri explains that the word 'ism' can mean either the linguistic expression (the word) or the essence it denotes. This distinction resolves the disagreement.
The passage states: 'إن أريد به الذات، كان محمولاً عليها' (if by it is intended the essence, then it is carried upon it).
2
Understand the agreed point
The passage emphasizes that no rational person thinks the literal word (letters and sounds) is identical to the essence. This is a unanimous position, as the text says 'إذ لا يعقل عاقل أن المركب من الحروف والأصوات يكون متحداً بالذات'.
This agreement shows the debate is only about the meaning of 'ism' in different contexts.
3
Analyze the examples
Look at the examples from al-Ramli and al-Baydawi: 'I wrote Zayd' (word), 'Zayd came' (essence), and the ambiguous 'I saw Zayd'. These illustrate how context determines meaning and why confusion arises.
The ambiguous case shows the need for a context to resolve the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وقوله: نَعَمْ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ الْمَدْلُولُ ... إلخ) بأن أريد بالاسم: الذات، فتحصل من كلامه أن لفظ (اسم) إن أريد به: الأسماء الدالة على الذات، أو استعمل مطلقاً بأن لم يرد به الأسماء ولا الذات؛ كان محمولاً على الأسماء، وإن أريد به الذات؛ كان محمولاً عليها، وكان مدلوله حينئذ : عين المسمى، وإذا علمت أن محل الخلاف هو مدلول (اسم) تعلم أن لفظ اسم غير الذات اتفاقاً؛ إذ لا يعقل عاقل أن المركب من الحروف والأصوات يكون متحداً بالذات، وهذا الخلاف جار في ماصدقات الاسم كلها، ومنها: لفظة اسم التي في البسملة مثلاً كما وجدته بهامش الرملي في الكلام على البسملة. وفي زاده على البيضاوي ما حاصله أن الخلاف في كون الاسم غير المسمى أو عين المسمى مما لا ينبغي أن يقع بين العقلاء، فإنه إن أريد به: اللفظ كما في قولك: كتبت زيداً، فهو غير المسمى ضرورة، وإن أريد به الذات كما في قولك: جاء زيد؛ فهو عين المسمى ضرورة ثم قال والظاهر أن هذا الخلاف مبني على أن الاسم قد يطلق ویراد به اللفظ كما في قولك: كتبت زيداً، وقد يطلق ويراد به: الذات كما في قولك: كتب زيد بالبناء للفاعل، وقد يطلق بلا قرينة ترجح أن المراد به اللفظ أو الذات كما في قولك: رأيت زيداً، فإنه محتمل لأن يكون رأى ذاته ولأن يكون رأى لفظ زيد مرسوماً على حائط مثلاً، فمن قال : إن الاسم غير المسمى؛ يحمله على أن المراد: أنه رأى اللفظ،
(His saying: 'Yes, if by it is meant the denoted meaning... etc.') That by 'name' is intended the essence. So it is obtained from his speech that the word 'name' if by it is intended the names that denote the essence, or if it is used absolutely such that neither the names nor the essence are intended, then it is carried upon the names; and if by it is intended the essence, then it is carried upon it, and its denoted meaning then is identical to the named. And when you know that the place of disagreement is the denoted meaning of 'name', you know that the word 'name' is other than the essence by agreement, for no rational person can conceive that a composite of letters and sounds is identical to the essence. And this disagreement applies to all the instances of 'name', including the word 'name' in the basmalah, for example, as I found in the margin of al-Ramli in his discussion on the basmalah. And in his Zaid [al-Zad] commentary on al-Baydawi, the gist is that the disagreement about whether the name is other than the named or identical to it is something that should not occur among rational people, because if by it is meant the word, as in your saying 'I wrote Zayd', then it is other than the named necessarily; and if by it is meant the essence, as in your saying 'Zayd came', then it is identical to the named necessarily. Then he said: and it is apparent that this disagreement is based on the fact that 'name' may be used and intended to mean the word, as in your saying 'I wrote Zayd', and may be used and intended to mean the essence, as in your saying 'Zayd wrote' in the active voice, and may be used without a context that gives preference to whether the word or the essence is intended, as in your saying 'I saw Zayd', for it is possible that he saw his essence or that he saw the word 'Zayd' written on a wall, for example. So whoever says that the name is other than the named carries it upon the intended meaning that he saw the word.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is al-Ujhuri's commentary (hashiya) on the preceding resolution ('n'am' statement). He explains that the resolution has clarified that the disagreement is really about the denotation of the word 'ism'. If 'ism' is used to mean the essence, then it is identical to the named; if it is used to mean the names indicating the essence, then it is other. Importantly, everyone agrees that the word itself — the composite of letters and sounds — is other than the essence; no rational person would equate them. The passage then cites additional support from al-Ramli and from a gloss on al-Baydawi, which similarly resolves the dispute by distinguishing whether 'ism' refers to the word (lafz) or the essence (dhat). It provides examples: 'I wrote Zayd' uses 'Zayd' as a word, so it is other than the person; 'Zayd came' uses 'Zayd' as the essence. Ambiguity arises when context is lacking, e.g., 'I saw Zayd' could be seeing the person or seeing the written name. Thus, the disagreement reduces to a misunderstanding of the different meanings of 'ism'.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
المدلول
الذات
اللفظ
ماصدقات الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the disagreement is about the ontological relationship between the name and the named (i.e., whether they are identical in reality).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the debate is merely semantic: it hinges on what 'ism' means in a given context—the word or the essence. All rational people agree the word itself is not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ujhuri's commentary, what do all rational people agree on?
A) The name is identical to the essence.
B) The name is other than the essence.
C) The name is sometimes identical and sometimes other.
D) The word 'ism' is composite of letters and sounds and thus other than the essence.

Key Takeaway
The resolution of the ism-musamma debate hinges on distinguishing the intended meaning of 'ism': whether it refers to the linguistic word or the essence itself.

?

The Four Divisions of Praise
Al-Bajuri enumerates the four divisions of praise and justifies God's sole deserving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of praise (الحمد) and why God alone deserves all praise.

Core Idea
Praise belongs to God in all its forms, whether eternal or temporal.

This lesson unpacks the phrase 'الحمد لله' by examining its breadth. The author shows that praise is not limited to our worship; it includes God's self-praise and His praise of prophets, as well as mutual praise among people—all ultimately due to God. Let's explore this rich classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the fourfold division of praise listed in the first sentence.
The division is based on whether the praiser and the praised are eternal (قديم) or temporal (حادث).
2
Identify the four types
List the four types: 1) Eternal praising Eternal (God praising Himself), 2) Eternal praising temporal (God praising prophets), 3) Temporal praising temporal (people praising each other), 4) Temporal praising Eternal (us praising God).
3
Understand why God deserves all
The passage explains that deserving hinges on whether the praised is a description of God or an act from Him. Since all praise either describes God or originates from His actions, He deserves it all.
The gloss on 'مستحق' reinforces this point.
4
Reflect on the broader meaning
Consider how this classification expands your understanding of 'الحمد لله'. It is not merely a phrase but a comprehensive statement about God's worthiness.
You might think praise is only from us, but the passage shows it is much more.

Source Text

His statement: 'All praise is for Allah' means praise in its four divisions – which are: 1) praise of the eternal (قديم) from the eternal: which is Allah's praise of Himself by Himself eternally; 2) praise of the eternal for the temporal (حادث): which is Allah's praise of His prophets and saints; 3) praise of the temporal for the temporal: which is the praise of people for one another; and 4) praise of the temporal for the eternal: which is our praise of Allah. His statement: 'deserving' (مستحق). The aspect of His, exalted, deserving all the divisions is that the one praised is either an attribute of Him, as in His praise of Himself and the praise of temporal beings for Him, or an act from Him, as in His praise of His prophets and saints and the praise of temporal beings for one another. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His statement: 'deserving'. The aspect of His, exalted, deserving all the divisions is that the one praised is either an attribute of Him... [repeated]. Decisions of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: In these contexts, or by His preservation and victory for him when he was restricted, and Ya'qub recited 'word of Allah' (كلمة الله) in the accusative as an appositive to 'word of those who disbelieve', and the nominative is more eloquent because it indicates that the word of Allah is high by itself, and even if it surpasses others, it is for establishing superiority and consideration, and therefore it is in the middle of virtue. His statement: 'meaning praise in its four divisions... etc.' This is apparent with respect to totality and genus, and similarly with respect to the definite article for a known entity; because if the known entity is possessed by Allah or specific to Him or deserving of Him, then other things are likewise by primary implication, so this is from it regarding the fact that the 'al' for a known entity is an explanation of what the matter comes to, as is not hidden. His statement: 'deserving... etc.' The word 'قدر' is the predicate of the preposition and the noun from the meaning of the 'lam', and the more appropriate is to estimate it from the root of 'thubut' as I explained elsewhere.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'praise' (الحمد) in the phrase 'الحمد لله' by dividing it into four categories. The author first lists the four divisions: (1) praise of the Eternal from the Eternal: God's praise of Himself; (2) praise of the Eternal for a temporal being: God's praise of prophets and saints; (3) praise of a temporal being for another temporal being: people praising each other; (4) praise of a temporal being for the Eternal: our praise of God. Then he explains why God deserves all these types of praise: the object of praise is either a description of God (as in His self-praise and creation's praise of Him) or an action emanating from God (as in His praise of prophets and people praising each other). The gloss of al-Ajhuri reiterates this point. The taqrirat of al-Anbabi adds that in certain contexts, God's preservation and victory are reasons for praise, and he comments on the recitation of 'كلمة الله' by Ya'qub (accusative vs. nominative), noting the nominative is more eloquent because it shows the word of Allah is inherently high. Finally, the commentary discusses the meaning of the definite article 'al-' in 'الحمد' and the estimation of the word 'مستحق'.

All praise, whether from God or creatures, eternal or temporal, ultimately belongs to God alone.

?

The Three Types of Lam in 'Lillah': Istihqaq, Ikhtisas, and Milk
Enumeration and definition of three lam types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the lam in 'lillah'—worthiness, special connection, and ownership—and how each applies to the praise of Allah.

Core Idea
The lam in 'lillah' can mean worthiness, connection, or ownership—each with distinct grammatical and theological implications.

The commentator unpacks the preposition that is central to the phrase 'lillah' (for Allah). In Arabic, the lam can carry different shades of meaning, each affecting how we understand that all praise belongs to Allah. This passage clarifies these nuances by examining three categories: istihqaq, ikhtisas, and milk.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source passage and its translation to familiarize yourself with the three types of lam. Notice the examples given for each.
Pay attention to the different contexts: meaning-substance vs. substance-substance.
2
Analyze each definition
For each lam type, examine the definition and the example provided. Understand the grammatical condition: what the lam connects and whether the object is owned.
Lam al-ikhtisas is the most nuanced—note its two sub-cases and the preponderant view that it indicates attachment, not restriction.
3
Reflect on theological relevance
Consider how each meaning applies to 'lillah' in 'al-hamdu lillah'. How does the grammatical analysis support the theological claim that all praise belongs to Allah?
The commentator later builds on this in the context of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاللام الداخلة على اللفظ الشريف؛ إما للاستحقاق، أو للاختصاص، أو للملك. وعلى كل؛ ف (أل) الداخلة على الحمد؛ إما للجنس..... حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (وقوله: مختص المراد بالاختصاص التعلق والارتباط، لا الحصر كما في الصبان والمراد بذلك التعلق أن المحمود به في الأقسام كلها؛ إما وصف له تعالى، أو واقع منه، وتقدم بيانه. (وقوله: أو مملوك المتبادر منه أن تكون المحامد كلها في قبضة القدرة، وعليه ينبني ما يأتي من أن القديم لا يملك، فإن حمل مملوكيته - على معنى أن المحامد كلها راجعة إليه تعالى وأنه محمود بها إما صريحاً كما في حمده نفسه بنفسه وحمد الحوادث له، وإما ضمناً كما في حمده لأنبيائه وأوليائه وحمد الحوادث بعضهم لبعض، فإن قولنا: زيد عالم في قوة أن يقال: الله أعطى زيداً العلم، وثناؤه تعالى على أنبيائه وأوليائه يتضمن ثناءه على نفسه بتفضيلهم على غيرهم -؛ صح جعل اللام للملك على التقادير كلها. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. (قوله: ومملوك ... إلخ) أي: على التفصيل الآتي. :(قوله: إما للاستحقاق... إلخ) لام الاستحقاق هي الواقعة بين معنى وذات، نحو: الحمد لله ) وويل للمطففين بناءً على أن الويل : اسم للعذاب، لا على أنه اسم واد في جهنم. ولام الاختصاص: هي الواقعة بين ذاتين، ومدخولها لا يملك، نحو: الجل للدابة، أو بين ذاتين ومصاحب مدخولها لا يملك، نحو: لزيد ابن ؛ إذ الابن لا يملك، و: أنت لي، و: أنا لك إذا كان كل من المخاطب والمتكلم حراً، والراجح: أن المراد بالاختصاص هنا: التعلق والارتباط لا القصر ولام الملك: هي الواقعة بين ذاتين، ومدخولها يملك، ومصاحب مدخولها يملك، نحو: المال لزيد. وقد يطلقون لام الاختصاص على الأول والثالث أيضاً، كما أنهم قد يطلقون لام الاستغراق على الثاني أيضاً. هذا حاصل ما في الأشموني وحاشية الصبان.
So the lam that enters upon the noble word is either for istihqaq (worthiness), or for ikhtisas (special connection), or for milk (ownership). And in any case, the 'al' that enters upon 'al-hamd' is either for the genus... The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: 'And His saying: "mukhtass" (specially connected) – what is meant by ikhtisas is attachment and connection, not restriction as in al-Sabban. And what is meant by that attachment is that the praised quality in all the divisions is either a description of Him, may He be exalted, or an act originating from Him, and its explanation has preceded. And His saying: "or mamluk" (possessed) – what is understood from it is that all praises are in the grasp of [His] power, and upon this is built what follows, that the eternal cannot be owned. So if the possession is interpreted in the sense that all praises revert to Him, may He be exalted, and that He is praised by them either explicitly, as in His praise of Himself by Himself and the praise of temporal beings for Him, or implicitly, as in His praise of His prophets and saints and the praise of temporal beings for one another, for our saying "Zayd is knowledgeable" is equivalent to saying "Allah gave Zayd knowledge," and His, may He be exalted, praising His prophets and saints implies His praise of Himself for preferring them over others – then the lam can be taken as milk in all cases. The notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: 'His saying: "and mamluk... etc." i.e., according to the detail that follows.' "His saying: 'either for istihqaq... etc.' – lam al-istihqaq is that which occurs between a meaning and a substance, e.g., "al-hamdu lillah" and "waylun lil-mutaffifin" based on the view that "wayl" is a name for punishment, not a valley in Hell. And lam al-ikhtisas is that which occurs between two substances, and its object is not owned, e.g., "al-jullu lil-dabba" (the saddlecloth belongs to the beast), or between two substances where the companion of its object is not owned, e.g., "li-Zaydin ibnun" (Zayd has a son) because a son is not owned, and "anta li" and "ana laka" (you are mine, I am yours) when both are free. The preponderant view is that what is meant by ikhtisas here is attachment and connection, not restriction. And lam al-milk is that which occurs between two substances, and its object is owned, and the companion of its object is owned, e.g., "al-malu li-Zaydin" (the wealth belongs to Zayd). And they sometimes call lam al-ikhtisas the first and third also, just as they sometimes call lam al-istighraq the second as well. This is the gist of what is in al-Ashmuni and the gloss of al-Sabban.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage explains the three possible meanings of the lam in 'lillah' based on Arabic grammar. First, it states that the lam is either for istihqaq (worthiness), ikhtisas (special connection), or milk (ownership). Then it gives detailed definitions and examples from the hashya. Lam al-istihqaq is used when a meaning is attributed to a substance, like 'praise is for Allah' or 'woe to the cheaters.' Lam al-ikhtisas indicates a connection between two entities where the second is not owned, like 'the saddlecloth for the beast.' Lam al-milk indicates ownership where both the object and its companion can be owned, like 'the money for Zayd.' The commentary also discusses the view that ikhtisas here means attachment not restriction, and notes that the classification can overlap. This is a grammatical analysis that underpins the theological claim that all praise belongs to Allah.

Key Terms
لام الاستحقاق
لام الاختصاص
لام الملك
استحقاق
اختصاص
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three meanings of lam are mutually exclusive and cannot overlap.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that lam al-ikhtisas is sometimes used for the first and third categories as well, showing the boundaries are not rigid.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of lam al-istihqaq according to the passage?
A) الجل للدابة
B) المال لزيد
C) الحمد لله
D) أنت لي

Key Takeaway
The lam in 'lillah' is analyzed into three grammatical categories—worthiness, connection, and ownership—which together show that all praise is inherently due to Allah, connected to Him, and owned by Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-hamdu lillah', do you truly feel that Allah is worthy of your praise, or has it become a routine phrase? What would change if you paused to reflect on the type of lam you are using?
?
Consider something you deeply value—your health, family, or talents. Which type of lam best describes your relationship to them: istihqaq (they are inherently yours?), ikhtisas (they are loaned to you?), or milk (you own them)? How does that affect your gratitude to Allah?
?
The lam al-ikhtisas shows that things can be connected to you without being owned. Is there anything in your life that you are attached to but do not truly own? How can this realization free you from possessiveness?

Shukr Istilahi: Definition and Quranic Proof
Bajuri defines shukr istilahi and defends the Quranic proof against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of shukr in kalam and how the Quranic verse is used as evidence despite an apparent objection.

Core Idea
True gratitude means using every blessing in its intended purpose — and that is exceedingly rare.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining shukr in its technical sense as the servant's complete expenditure of Allah's blessings in their proper channels. He then anticipates an objection: the Quranic verse 'And few of My servants are the grateful (al-shakūr)' seems to speak about abundant gratitude, not the basic act. This lesson unpacks the definition, the objection, and the nuanced response that defends the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of shukr istilahi
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note the two key elements: 'spending all blessings' and 'in that for which they were created.'
The phrase 'lā yakādu yūjad' indicates extreme rarity.
2
Identify the Quranic proof and the objection
Find where the verse is cited and understand the objection: 'shakūr' may mean 'greatly grateful,' not simply 'grateful,' so the verse might only prove abundant gratitude is rare.
The objection is crucial for refining the proof.
3
Follow the two responses
First, note the reinterpretation of 'hardly found' as extreme fewness. Second, see the hyperbole argument that spending all at once defines the true shakūr.
These responses show how the verse supports the definition despite the objection.
4
Distinguish shākir from shakūr
Understand the terminological difference: shākir is one who spends blessings over time, while shakūr is one who spends them all in an instant.
This distinction resolves the apparent mismatch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه صَرْفُ العبد جميع ما أنعم الله به عليه فيما خُلِقَ لأجله وهو لا يكاد يُوجَدُ، قال الله تعالى: وَقَلِيلٌ مِنْ عِبَادِيَ الشَّكُورُ . (قوله: قَالَ الله تَعَالَى... إلخ) إنما ينتج إذا أريد بالشكور: الشاكر، فإن أريد به: كثير الشكر كما هو ظاهر؛ لم ينتج ؛ لأن المحكوم عليه بالقلة: كثرة الشكر لا أصله. (قوله: فَإِنَّهُ صَرْفُ... إلخ) ظاهرُهُ : سواء كان في آنٍ أو في آناتٍ. (وقوله: وَهُوَ لَا يَكَادُ يُوجَدُ) يفيد أنه معدوم، والدليل بعد يفيد أنه قليل، فلم يطابق الدليل المدَّعَى. ويُمْكِنُ الجواب بأن المراد من قوله: (لا يكاد يوجد) القلة وعبر عنها بما ذُكِرَ إشارة إلى أنها قلة بمنزلة العَدَم، وأما تأويل القلَّةِ المأخوذة من الآية بالعدم؛ فهو مخالف للواقع. وقد يقال : وَجْهُ الاستدلال: أن الشكور مبالغة في الشاكر اللغوي، والمبالغة حاصلة بصَرْفِ الكلّ الذي هو معنى الشكر اصطلاحاً على ما فيه من البُعْدِ وعدم اختصاص المبالغة بصرف الكل، وعبارة البيضاوي: وَقَلِيلٌ مِنْ عِبَادِيَ الشَّكُورُ : المتوفِّرُ على أداء الشكر بقلبه ولسانه وجوارحه أكثر أوقاته، ومع ذلك لا يُوَفِّي حقه؛ لأن توفيقه للشكر نِعْمَةٌ تستدعي شُكراً آخَرَ لا إلى نهاية، ولذلك قيل : الشَّكُورُ مَنْ يَرَى عَجْزه عن الشكر. وفي كلام بعضهم أن الشخص إن صَرَفَ جميعَ ما أنعم الله به عليه في آناتٍ؛ سُمِّيَ : شاكراً اصطلاحاً، فإن صَرَفَها في آن واحدٍ سمّي شكوراً، وهذا الأخير هو الذي لا يكاد يُوجَدُ كما قال تعالى: (وَقَلِيلٌ مِنْ عِبَادِيَ الشَّكُورُ .
For it is the servant's spending all that Allah has bestowed upon him in that for which it was created, and it is hardly found. Allah Almighty said: 'And few of My servants are the grateful (al-shakūr).' (His saying: 'Allah Almighty said...' etc.) It is only conclusive if by al-shakūr is meant the grateful one (al-shākir); but if it means the one who is greatly grateful (kathīr al-shukr), as is apparent, then it is not conclusive, because what is ruled as few is the abundance of gratitude, not its origin. (His saying: 'For it is the servant's spending...' etc.) Its apparent meaning is: whether it is in an instant or in moments. (His saying: 'And it is hardly found') indicates that it is non-existent, while the later proof indicates that it is few, so the proof does not match the claim. And a response is possible: that what is meant by his saying 'hardly found' is few, and he expressed it with that phrase to indicate that it is a fewness akin to non-existence. But as for interpreting the fewness taken from the verse as non-existence, it contradicts reality. And it may be said: The aspect of the proof is that al-shakūr is a hyperbole for the linguistic shākir, and the hyperbole is achieved by spending all (ṣarf al-kull) which is the meaning of shukr terminologically, despite the distance [in that] and the fact that the hyperbole is not specific to spending all. And the expression of al-Bayḍāwī: 'And few of My servants are the grateful (al-shakūr)': the one who devotes himself to performing gratitude with his heart, tongue, and limbs most of his times, and yet he does not fulfill its right, because his being granted success to gratitude is a blessing that calls for another gratitude ad infinitum. Therefore it is said: al-shakūr is he who sees his inability to give thanks. And in the speech of some, that a person, if he spends all that Allah has bestowed upon him in moments, he is called a shākir terminologically, and if he spends them in a single instant, he is called a shakūr. This latter is the one who is hardly found, as the Almighty said: 'And few of My servants are the grateful (al-shakūr).'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines shukr istilahi as the servant's expenditure of all blessings in their intended purpose, and notes its rarity. It then presents an objection regarding the Quranic proof: the verse says 'few are the shakūr' (superlative), which implies abundance of gratitude, not just minimal gratitude, so the proof that shukr (basic gratitude) is rare is invalid. The response distinguishes two interpretations: 'hardly found' means very few, almost non-existent, but not literally non-existent. A further explanation from al-Bayḍāwī and others explains that the hyperbole is achieved by spending all, and the one who spends all at once is the true shakūr, which is extremely rare. The passage thus defends the proof by reinterpreting the verse and the terms.

Key Terms
شكر
شكور
شاكر
صرف
نعمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'And few of My servants are the grateful' proves that basic shukr (gratitude) is rarely found in anyone.
Correction: The verse uses 'shakūr,' which is a hyperbole meaning 'greatly grateful' or 'one who gives perfect gratitude.' It only proves that the highest level of gratitude (spending all blessings at once) is rare, not the minimal act of shukr.

Checkpoint
What is the initial objection raised against using the verse 'Wa qalilun min 'ibadiya al-shakur' as proof that shukr istilahi is rare?
A) The verse talks about shakur (greatly grateful) not shakir (grateful).
B) The verse says qalil (few) which is not the same as 'adam (non-existence).
C) The definition of shukr is not clear.
D) The verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The definition of shukr istilahi is comprehensive and rare; the Quranic verse, upon careful interpretation, supports this rarity by referring to the superlative degree of gratitude.

The Six Relations between Shukr and Hamd
Bajuri enumerates the six relations between linguistic and terminological shukr and hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six distinct logical relationships between the terms shukr and hamd in their linguistic and terminological senses, and how they are categorised into absolute general-specific, synonymy, and general-specific from one perspective.

Core Idea
The six relations between shukr and hamd reveal a precise logical grid for Islamic theological vocabulary.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary on Umm al-Barahin explains how four key terms—shukr istilahi, shukr lughawi, hamd istilahi, and hamd lughawi—relate to one another. Understanding these relationships is essential for precise theological reasoning. Bajuri enumerates six relations, showing that the words are not simply interchangeable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own classification come through before you analyse.
Notice the four terms: شكر اصطلاحي, شكر لغوي, حمد اصطلاحي, حمد لغوي.
2
Identify the first three relations
Bajuri states that terminological shukr has an absolute general-specific relation with each of the other three terms. That gives three relations.
Absolute general-specific means one term is fully included in the other, not vice versa.
3
Understand the fourth relation
Linguistic shukr and terminological hamd are synonyms (tarāduf). That is the fourth relation.
This was previously indicated in the text.
4
Grasp the last two relations
Linguistic hamd has a one-sided general-specific relation (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ wajhī) with each of terminological hamd and linguistic shukr. That gives two more relations.
One-sided means each is more general in some respects and more specific in others.
5
Sum up to six
Add the three from step 2, the one from step 3, and the two from step 4 to get six total relations, as al-Ajhūrī indicated.
This enumeration shows the precision of Islamic terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النسبة بين الشكر الاصطلاحي وبين كل الشكر الاصطلاحي وبين كل من الحمد اللغوي والاصطلاحي والشكر اللغوي عموم وخصوص مطلق، فالشكرُ الاصطلاحي أخصُّ من الجميع، فهذه نِسَب ثلاث. والنسبة بين الشكر اللغوي والحمد الاصطلاحي الترادف كما تقدَّمت الإشارة إليه. والنسبة بين الحمد اللغوي وكلّ من الحمد الاصطلاحي والشكر اللغوي: العمومُ والخصوص الوجهي، فهاتان نسبتان فإذا ضَمَمْتَهُما للتي قبلهما مع السابقة كانت الجملةُ سِتَّةَ كما أشار إلى ذلك سيدي على الأجهوري بقوله:
And know that the relationship between terminological shukr and each of terminological shukr and each of linguistic hamd, terminological hamd, and linguistic shukr is absolute general-specific (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq). So terminological shukr is more specific than all of them. So these are three relations. The relationship between linguistic shukr and terminological hamd is synonymy (tarāduf), as was previously indicated. And the relationship between linguistic hamd and each of terminological hamd and linguistic shukr is general-specific from one perspective (al-ʿumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-wajhī). So these are two relations. Then when you add them to the previous three with the earlier one, the total becomes six, as my master ʿAlī al-Ajhūrī indicated in his saying:
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage classifies the relationships between the four terms: shukr istilahi, shukr lughawi, hamd istilahi, hamd lughawi. It states that shukr istilahi has an absolute general-specific relationship with each of the other three (i.e., it is more specific, and they are more general). Then it lists three relations from that. Then it says shukr lughawi and hamd istilahi are synonymous. Then it says hamd lughawi has a one-sided general-specific relationship with both hamd istilahi and shukr lughawi (meaning each is more general in some respects and more specific in others). That gives two more relations. Adding the three from before, plus the one earlier (the synonymy), the total becomes six, as Al-Ajhuri indicated.

The Three Ratios Between Shukr and Hamd
Sanusi defines the three ratios between shukr and hamd and derives six ratios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three basic ratios between shukr and hamd (terminological and linguistic) and how they combine to give six distinct relationships.

Core Idea
The terms shukr and hamd have three core relationships that expand into six.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the exact relationships between the terms shukr (thankfulness) and hamd (praise) when considered in both their linguistic and terminological senses. This precision is essential for aqeedah because these terms are used in theological discussions about gratitude to Allah. The author begins by identifying three fundamental ratios and then shows how combining them yields six total relationships, as illustrated by a poetic summary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three basic ratios
Read the passage and locate the three fundamental relationships: terminological shukr is most specific, linguistic shukr equals terminological hamd, and linguistic hamd relates to the other two by general-specific in some respects (umum wa khusus wajhi).
These are the building blocks for the six ratios.
2
Follow the derivation to six ratios
Notice how the third ratio yields two relationships, which when added to the previous two give a total of six. The author quotes Ali al-Ajhuri's poetic summary to reinforce this.
The poem encodes all six ratios compactly.
3
Reflect on the commentary about bestowal
The commentator explains that terminological shukr's specificity depends on considering the concept of divine bestowal (in'am), which is implied in the definition even if not explicitly stated.
This shows how technical terms in kalam often carry implicit conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالشكرُ الاصطلاحي أخصُّ من الجميع، فهذه نِسَب ثلاث. والنسبة بين الشكر اللغوي والحمد الاصطلاحي الترادف كما تقدَّمت الإشارة إليه. والنسبة بين الحمد اللغوي وكلّ من الحمد الاصطلاحي والشكر اللغوي: العمومُ والخصوص الوجهي، فهاتان نسبتان فإذا ضَمَمْتَهُما للتي قبلهما مع السابقة كانت الجملةُ سِتَّةً كما أشار إلى ذلك سيدي على الأجهوري بقوله: إِذَا نُسِبَا لِلْحَمْدِ وَالشُّكْرِ رُمَّتَهَا بِوَجْهِ لَهُ عَقْلُ اللَّبِيبِ يُوَالِفُ فَشُكْرِي لَدَى عُرْفِ أَخَصُّ جَمِيعَهَا وَفِي لُغَةِ الْحَمْد عُرْفاً يُرادِفُ عُمُومٌ لِوَجْهِ فِي سِوَاهُنَّ نِسْبَةٌ فَذِي نِسَبٌ سِتٌ لِمَنْ هُوَ عَارِفُ (قوله: فَالشَّكْرُ الْاِصْطِلاحِي أَخَصُّ مِنَ الْجَمِيعِ هذا يتوقف على اعتبار الإنعام في مفهومه مع أنهم لم يذكروه فيه إلا أن يُقَال - كما قاله الصَّبَّانُ فيما كتبه على مقدمة "جمع الجوامع" -: إن اعتبار الإنعام في المفهوم قد أشير له بقولهم: أَنْعَمَ الله به عليه فيما خُلِقَ لأجله؛ وإن كان لا يتقيَّدُ بالإنعام بالشيء المصروف كما لا يخفى.
So terminological shukr (thankfulness) is more specific than all of them, so these are three ratios. And the ratio between linguistic shukr and terminological hamd (praise) is synonymy as was previously indicated. And the ratio between linguistic hamd and each of terminological hamd and linguistic shukr is general-specific in some respects, so these are two ratios. If you add them to the two before them along with the preceding one, the total becomes six, as my master Ali al-Ajhuri indicated by his saying: When hamd and shukr are compared, you intend them in a way that the mind of the intelligent agrees. So my shukr in terminology is more specific than all of them, and in the language of hamd in terminology it is synonymous. General-specific in some respects in other than them is a ratio, so these are six ratios for whoever is knowledgeable. (His saying: 'So terminological shukr is more specific than all of them') This depends on considering bestowal in its concept, even though they did not mention it in it, except that it is said—as al-Sabban said in his gloss on the introduction of Jam' al-Jawami'—that consideration of bestowal in the concept is indicated by their saying: 'God bestowed it upon him for the purpose for which it was created'; even if it is not restricted to bestowal of the thing expended, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the ratios between the terms shukr (thankfulness) and hamd (praise) in both their linguistic (lughawi) and terminological (istilahi) senses. The author begins by stating that terminological shukr is more specific than all others—meaning it is the narrowest concept because it requires expending blessings in their proper purpose. Three fundamental ratios are identified: (1) terminological shukr is more specific than everything else, (2) linguistic shukr and terminological hamd are synonyms, and (3) linguistic hamd with both terminological hamd and linguistic shukr have a general-specific in some respects relationship (umum wa khusus wajhi). Adding these two ratios to the previous ones, six total ratios emerge. To summarize, the author quotes the poetic lines of Ali al-Ajhuri, which encode these relationships: terminological shukr is most specific; linguistic hamd and terminological hamd are synonymous; the remaining relationships are general-specific in some respects. Finally, a commentator (likely al-Bajuri) remarks that defining terminological shukr as 'more specific' depends on including the idea of divine bestowal (in'am) in its meaning, a point that, though not explicit, is implied in standard definitions.
