The Five Pillars of Praise (Arkan al-Hamd)
Sanusi enumerates the five pillars of praise with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five essential components (arkān) of praise (al-ḥamd) as defined by Sanusi, and how each component is identified in a concrete example.

Core Idea
Praise has five necessary pillars: the praiser, the praised, the attribute, the reason, and the expression.

Sanusi opens a seemingly simple concept—praise—and shows that it has a precise analytical structure. In this lesson, we examine the five pillars (arkān) of praise, which every Muslim should understand to properly appreciate the meanings of al-ḥamd in the Qur'an and worship. The passage provides a clear enumeration and an illustrative example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the list of five terms and how the example unpacks each one.
2
Identify each pillar in the example
Work through the example of praising Zayd for his generosity. Identify which element corresponds to each pillar: praiser, praised, attribute, reason, and expression.
3
Distinguish the third and fourth pillars
Pay attention to the distinction between maḥmūd bihī (the attribute) and maḥmūd ʻalayhi (the reason). They may be the same in essence but differ in consideration.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this analysis matters for understanding theological concepts like praising Allah, where His attributes and reasons for praise are often one in essence but distinguished by consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأركانُ الحمد خمسة: حامد و محمود، و محمود به، و محمود عليه، وصيغة. فإذا حَمِدْتَ زيداً لكونه أكرمك مثلاً، كأن قلت زيد عالم؛ فأنت حامد، وزيد محمود، وثبوت العلم محمود به والإكرام محمود عليه، وقولك: زيد عالم صيغة. ثم إن المحمود به والمحمود عليه في هذا المثال اختلفا ذاتاً واعتباراً، وقد يتحدان ذاتاً ويختلفان اعتباراً، كأن يكون كلّ منهما الكرمُ، لكن من حيث كونه مدلول الصيغة يقال له : محمود به ومن حيث كونه باعثاً على الحمد يقال له: محمود عليه.
And the pillars of praise (al-ḥamd) are five: a praiser (ḥāmid), a praised (maḥmūd), that by which he is praised (maḥmūd bihī), that for which he is praised (maḥmūd ʻalayhi), and a linguistic form (ṣīgha). So if you praise Zayd because he honored you, for example, as if you say "Zayd is knowledgeable," then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised, the affirmation of knowledge is that by which he is praised, the honor is that for which he is praised, and your statement "Zayd is knowledgeable" is the linguistic form. Then the maḥmūd bihī and maḥmūd ʻalayhi in this example differed in essence and consideration, and they may be identical in essence but differ in consideration, such as each of them being generosity, but from the perspective of its being the meaning of the formula it is called maḥmūd bihī, and from the perspective of its being the motive for praise it is called maḥmūd ʻalayhi.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the five pillars (arkān) of al-ḥamd (praise). The author lists them: 1) the praiser (al-ḥāmid), 2) the praised (al-maḥmūd), 3) that by which the praised is praised (al-maḥmūd bihī) – i.e., the attribute or quality, 4) that for which the praised is praised (al-maḥmūd ʻalayhi) – i.e., the reason or motive, and 5) the linguistic form or expression (al-ṣīgha). He then illustrates with an example: praising Zayd for his generosity by saying "Zayd is knowledgeable." In this example, the praiser is you, the praised is Zayd, the attribute of knowledge is the maḥmūd bihī, the generosity is the maḥmūd ʻalayhi, and the statement "Zayd is knowledgeable" is the expression. He further explains that the maḥmūd bihī and maḥmūd ʻalayhi may differ in essence (dhāt) and consideration (iʻtibār), or may be identical in essence but differ in consideration, e.g., both being generosity but considered from different angles: as the meaning of the formula it is maḥmūd bihī, as the cause of praise it is maḥmūd ʻalayhi.

Key Terms

الحامد
المحمود
المحمود به
المحمود عليه
الصيغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five pillars are only relevant for praising humans, not for praising Allah.
Correction: The passage presents these pillars as the general structure of any praise (ḥamd), including praise of Allah. The example using Zayd is just an illustration; the same analysis applies to praising Allah, where the attributes (maḥmūd bihī) and reasons (maḥmūd ʻalayhi) are often the same in essence (e.g., His mercy) but distinguished by consideration.

Checkpoint

A) Praiser, praised, attribute, reason, and expression
B) Intention, statement, action, belief, and sincerity
C) Heart, tongue, limbs, intention, and sincerity
D) Praise, gratitude, glorification, sanctification, and exaltation

Key Takeaway
Praise (al-ḥamd) is not a single act but an act composed of five necessary elements: the praiser, the praised, the attribute praised by, the reason praised for, and the linguistic expression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Alhamdulillah', do you consciously consider all five pillars—who you are as the praiser, who Allah is as the praised, the attribute you are praising Him for, the reason that motivated that praise, and the words you use? How will this lesson change your next utterance of praise?

Think of a time you praised someone insincerely. Which pillar exposes that insincerity most—the reason (maḥmūd ʻalayhi) or the expression (ṣīgha)? What does that reveal about your heart?
?
If the attribute and the reason can be identical in essence yet differ in consideration, how does that affect the way you reflect on Allah's names and attributes? Are you praising Him for His mercy only as an attribute, or also as the reason for your gratitude?

Four Associations Between Hamd and Shukr
Al-Ujhuri explains the four types of association between hamd and shukr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific associations between hamd and shukr and the nature of the remaining two relationships.

Core Idea
The relationship between hamd and shukr depends on which definition is used.

Al-Ujhuri unpacks a dense statement about the relationships between praise and gratitude. He identifies four associations that specifically involve shukr (technical or linguistic) with various forms of hamd, and then explains that the remaining two associations are only partially overlapping. This lesson breaks down that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms.
The passage is a hashya, so it refers to a previous statement.
2
Identify the four associations
The passage lists four associations: technical shukr with each of the three types of hamd, and linguistic shukr with customary hamd. List them in your own words.
The 'three' are likely technical, linguistic, and customary hamd.
3
Understand the remaining two
The remaining two associations involve linguistic hamd with customary hamd and linguistic hamd with linguistic shukr. Their relationship is 'general and specific in one respect' (umum wa khusus wajhi). Ask yourself what partial overlap means.
This shows that not all pairs are fully identical or separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فِي سِوَاهُنَّ أي سوى الاجتماعات الأربعة المشتملة على النسب الأربعة، وهي اجتماع الشكر الاصطلاحي مع كل واحد من الثلاثة، واجتماع الشكر اللغوي مع الحمد العرفي، وسواها هو اجتماع اجتماع الحمد اللغوي مع الحمد العرفي والشكر اللغوي، والمعنى: إن النسبة في سوى الاجتماعات الأربعة، وهو الاجتماعان المتقدم بيانهما، هي العموم والخصوص الوجهي
(His saying: 'in other than them' means other than the four associations that comprise the four relationships, which are: the association of technical shukr with each of the three, and the association of linguistic shukr with customary hamd. And other than them is the association of linguistic hamd with customary hamd and linguistic shukr. The meaning: the relationship in other than the four associations, which are the two associations previously explained, is general and specific in one respect (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-wajhī).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a hashya by al-Ujhuri clarifying a preceding statement about the types of association between al-ḥamd (praise) and al-shukr (gratitude). He explains that the expression 'other than them' refers to the four specific associations that involve the various technical and linguistic definitions of ḥamd and shukr. Those four are: 1) technical shukr with each of the three (presumably the three types of ḥamd?), and 2) linguistic shukr with customary ḥamd. The 'other than them' refers to two further associations: linguistic ḥamd with customary ḥamd and linguistic ḥamd with linguistic shukr. The relationship in these remaining two associations is that of general and specific in one respect (al-ʻumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-wajhī), meaning they overlap partially but not completely.

الشكر
الاجتماع
النسب
العام والخاص الوجهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four associations are between hamd and shukr in general, without considering their definitions.
Correction: The associations are specifically between technical and linguistic definitions. Technical shukr, for example, is not the same as linguistic shukr.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Meanings of Salat and Definition of Homonymy
Al-Bajuri enumerates the meanings of salat and defines homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of salat (mercy, supplication) and how the term can be considered a homonym with a common meaning.

Core Idea
Salat has different meanings for Allah and others, leading to the concept of homonymy.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri lays out the foundational meanings of the word 'salat' and introduces the concept of homonymy. He distinguishes between its application to Allah (mercy) and to others (supplication), and then defines what it means for a word to be a homonym.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the discussion. Notice the key terms: 'tadarru'', 'du'a', 'rahma', 'ishtirak lafzi'.
The passage uses technical terms that will be explained.
2
Identify the meanings of salat
Identify the three meanings mentioned: for Allah (mercy), for angels (seeking forgiveness), for others (supplication).
3
Understand the definition of homonymy
Al-Bajuri defines homonymy as one word with multiple meanings, using the example of 'ayn. Grasp this definition.
This is a key methodological concept.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for theology and how it avoids attributing improper meanings to Allah.

وبالنسبة لغيرهم - ولو شَجَراً أو حَجَراً أو مَدَراً - : التضرُّعُ والدعاء لثبوت صلاتها على النبي كما رواه الحَلَبِيُّ في "السيرة "(۱) ؛ وإن اشتهر أنها سَلَّمَتْ عليه فقط. وإن شئتَ قلتَ ـ وهو الأخصرُ - بالنسبة الله الرحمة، وبالنسبة لغيره من الملائكة وغيرهم الدعاءُ، وحينئذ يكون شاملاً للاستغفار وغيره. واختار ابنُ هِشَامٍ في "مغنيه (۲): أنه العطفُ - بفتح العين ، وهو بالنسبة الله الرحمةُ إلخ. ، ويترتب على هذا الخلاف أنها من قبيل المشترك اللفظي على الأول، وضابطه: أن يتَّحِدَ اللفظ ويتعدد المعنى، كما في لفظ: عين، فإنه واحد ومعناه تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي . (قوله: التَّضَرُّعُ) هو السؤالُ بخُشُوع وذِلَّةٍ، فعَطْفُ الدعاء عليه عطف عام على خاص. وفيه: أن جَعْلَ الدعاء معنى مشتركاً ينافي قوله فيما تقدم: (معنى لغوي فقط وهو الدعاء). والجواب بأن المشترك : إنما هو الدعاء بالنسبة لغير الله، بخلاف الخاص بأهل اللغة، فإنه الدعاء سواءً كان بالنسبة الله أو لغيره ; مردودٌ بأنه لا يُتَصَوَّرُ الدعاء من الله؛ إذ ليس هنالك أَعْلَى منه حتى يَطْلُبَ منه، فالمعنى اللغوي فقط إنما هو بالنسبة لغير الله، كما فقط كذلك. وفي "دقائق المنهاج: أن المعنى المشترك: هو الرحمة فقط، أن الشرعي وعلى هذا لا إشكال. وقال بعضهم: ليس للصلاة إلا معنيان فقط: الدعاء، والأقوال والأفعال المخصوصة، الأوّلُ لُغَوِيّ، والثاني شرعي، وأما إطلاقها على الرحمة بالنسبة الله؛ فهو مجاز؛ لأن كل شيء اسْتَحَالَ على الله باعتبار مبدئه؛ جَازَ إطلاقه عليه تعالى باعتبار غايته. (قوله: وَهُوَ الْأَخْصَرُ) أي: والأولى أيضاً؛ لأنه ربما يُتَوَهَّمُ من التعبير في جانب الملائكة بالاستغفار، وفي جانب غيرهم بالدعاء؛ أنْ دُعَاءَ الملائكة بصيغة المغفرة فقط، وليس كذلك.
And with respect to others - even a tree or a stone or a clod - it is supplication and prayer, due to the establishment of their prayer upon the Prophet as related by al-Halabi in 'al-Sira'; although it is famous that she only greeted him. And if you wish, you may say - and it is the shortest - with respect to Allah it is mercy, and with respect to other than Him from angels and others it is prayer, and then it includes seeking forgiveness and other things. And Ibn Hisham chose in his 'Mughni' that it is 'al-'atf' (with fatha on 'ayn), and with respect to Allah it is mercy, etc. And upon this difference follows that it is of the type of lafzi homonym on the first view, and its definition is that the word is one and the meaning is multiple, as in the word 'ayn, for it is one and its meaning... (reports of al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi). (His saying: 'al-tadarru'') is asking with humility and lowliness, so the conjunction of 'du'a' upon it is a conjunction of general to specific. And in it: that making 'du'a' a shared meaning contradicts his previous saying: 'linguistic meaning only is du'a'. And the answer that the shared meaning is only du'a with respect to other than Allah, contrary to the specific according to the people of language, for it is du'a whether with respect to Allah or other than Him, is refuted because du'a from Allah is inconceivable, since there is no one higher than Him to ask from. So the linguistic meaning only is with respect to other than Allah, as it is only that. And in 'Daqa'iq al-Minhaj': that the shared meaning is only mercy, that the shar'i meaning, and upon this there is no problem. And some said: There are only two meanings for salat: du'a and the specific words and actions; the first is linguistic, the second is shar'i. As for its application to mercy with respect to Allah, it is metaphorical; because everything that is impossible for Allah in consideration of its beginning, it is permissible to apply to Him in consideration of its end. (His saying: 'and it is the shortest') i.e., and also the best; because perhaps it might be imagined from the expression on the side of angels as seeking forgiveness, and on the side of others as prayer, that the prayer of angels is only in the form of forgiveness, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines the various meanings of 'salat' and addresses the issue of homonymy. Al-Bajuri begins by stating that for others (non-God), salat means supplication and prayer, giving the example of trees and stones greeting the Prophet. He then presents the concise view: for Allah it is mercy, for others it is prayer, which includes seeking forgiveness. Ibn Hisham's view is mentioned that 'al-'atf' (inclination?) is mercy for Allah. Then he explains that the difference of opinion leads to classifying 'salat' as lafzi homonym (one word, multiple meanings) on the first view. He critiques the claim that 'du'a' is a shared meaning, because du'a from Allah is impossible. He cites 'Daqa'iq al-Minhaj' saying the shared meaning is only mercy, resolving the issue. He then mentions another opinion that salat has only two meanings: linguistic (du'a) and shar'i (specific acts). Applying it to Allah as mercy is metaphorical, as it is permissible to apply things to Allah based on their end. He concludes that the concise expression is preferable to avoid misunderstanding that angels only pray for forgiveness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'mushtarak lafzi'?
A) One word with one meaning
B) One word with multiple meanings
C) Multiple words with one meaning
D) A word that is used metaphorically

Key Takeaway
The term 'salat' is applied differently to Allah (mercy) and others (supplication), and this difference makes it a homonym with a shared core of meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'salat', do you truly feel it is a moment of deep humility and supplication before Allah, or has it become just a routine movement? What would change if you reminded yourself that your salat is an act of tadarru'—begging before your Lord?
?
The word 'salat' is a homonym: for Allah it is mercy, for you it is supplication. Have you ever assumed Allah's mercy is just like human mercy, limited and conditional? How might this lesson correct your understanding of Allah's unbound mercy every time you pray?
?
If the very word for your prayer is a homonym that shifts meaning with its subject, are you careful with other words about Allah? Do you sometimes attribute to Him human qualities because of word confusion? What one word about Allah can you clarify today based on this lesson?

Critique of Du'a as Shared Meaning and Metaphorical Application
Al-Bajuri critiques the shared meaning of du'a and explains the metaphorical nature of salat for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'du'a' cannot be a shared meaning for Allah and others, and how 'salat' is applied metaphorically to Allah as mercy.

Core Idea
Du'a from Allah is inconceivable; salat is mercy for Allah, metaphorically.

Having established the meanings, Al-Bajuri now critiques the idea that 'du'a' is a shared meaning. He argues that du'a cannot apply to Allah, so the correct shared meaning is mercy. He also explains that applying 'salat' to Allah as mercy is metaphorical, not literal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Pay attention to the argument: why making 'du'a' a shared meaning is problematic.
Look for the phrase 'la yutakhayyalu al-du'a min Allah'.
2
Understand the refutation
Al-Bajuri refutes the claim that du'a is the shared meaning by pointing out that Allah cannot supplicate.
3
Learn the correct view from Daqa'iq al-Minhaj
The correct view is that the shared meaning is only mercy, which resolves the issue.
4
Grasp the metaphorical application
Some scholars say salat has only two meanings (du'a and specific acts). Applying to Allah as mercy is metaphorical, based on the end (ghaya) rather than the beginning.
This is an important methodological principle: what is impossible in its origin can be attributed to Allah considering its end.

Lafzi and Ma'nawi Homonymy in Kalam
Al-Bajuri defines and exemplifies lafzi and ma'nawi homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of homonymy in Islamic theology: lafzi (verbal) and ma'nawi (semantic), and how they differ.

Core Idea
Homonymy can be one word with many meanings or one meaning with many instances.

Al-Bajuri introduces a crucial distinction in theological terminology: the difference between words that have multiple unrelated meanings and words that have a single meaning but many individual referents. This passage clarifies these two types with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage begins mid-sentence; it is part of a larger discussion on homonymy.
2
Identify the two types of homonymy
The passage discusses two types: lafzi (verbal) homonymy and ma'nawi (semantic) homonymy. Find where each is defined or exemplified.
Lafzi is illustrated with 'ayn; ma'nawi with 'asad.
3
Understand the examples
For lafzi, 'ayn is coined for different meanings (eye, running water, gold, silver). For ma'nawi, 'asad (lion) has one meaning that applies to many individual lions.
Notice that lafzi involves multiple coinages, while ma'nawi involves a single coinage with multiple instances.
4
Note the verified view
Al-Bajuri states that the verified view is the second (ma'nawi) for the term under discussion, contrary to those who chose the first (lafzi).
This indicates a scholarly preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متعدّد؛ لأنه وُضِعَ للباصرة بوضع وللجارية بوضع، وللذهب والفضة بوضع إلى غير ذلك. وأنها من قبيل المشترك المعنوي على الثاني، وضابطه: أن يتَّجِدَ كلُّ من اللفظ والمعنى، لكن يكون لذلك المعنى أفراد مشتركة فيه، كما في لفظ : أَسَدٍ، فإنه واحد ومعناه واحد، ولكن لمعناه أفراد مشتركة فيه والتحقيق الثاني خلافاً لمن اختار الأول.
Multiple; because it was coined for the seeing eye by one coinage and for the running water by another, and for gold and silver by another, and so on. And that it is of the type of ma'nawi homonym on the second view, and its definition is that both the word and the meaning are one, but that meaning has multiple instances that share in it, as in the word 'asad' (lion), for it is one and its meaning is one, but its meaning has multiple instances that share in it. And the verified view is the second, contrary to those who chose the first.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage classifies homonymy (ishtirak) into two types. First, lafzi homonymy: one word coined for multiple distinct meanings, like 'ayn (eye/water source/gold etc.). Second, ma'nawi homonymy: one word with a single meaning that applies to multiple individuals, like 'asad' (lion) which refers to any lion. The author states that the verified view is the second (ma'nawi) for the term under discussion, contrary to those who chose the first (lafzi).

Key Terms
اشتراك لفظي
اشتراك معنوي
عين
أسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think homonymy only means one word with unrelated meanings (lafzi).
Correction: Homonymy includes ma'nawi, where one word has a single meaning but many individual referents.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of ma'nawi homonymy according to this passage?
A) One word, multiple unrelated meanings
B) One word and one meaning, but multiple instances of that meaning
C) Multiple words with one meaning
D) One word coined for different things by different coinages

Key Takeaway
Homonymy in theological terms includes both words with multiple meanings (lafzi) and words with a single meaning that has many instances (ma'nawi).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a word in the Qur'an that could have multiple meanings (like 'ayn), do you dismiss it as confusing or do you actively seek out which meaning is intended? How does your answer reveal your attitude toward divine speech?
?
This lesson teaches that one word can refer to many individual lions (ma'nawi homonymy). In your daily life, do you treat categories of people (e.g., 'believers', 'sinners') as abstract labels, or do you recognize each individual as a distinct instance of that meaning? What would change if you lived by the latter?
?
The verified view (al-tahqiq) in this lesson is the second, against those who chose the first. Are you willing to abandon a comfortable preconception about homonymy when the evidence points otherwise? What is one belief about religious terms that you hold without scrutiny, and are you ready to test it?

Lexical vs. Conceptual Common Words
Commentator defines and distinguishes between lexical and conceptual common words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between lexical common (mushtarak lafẓī) and conceptual common (mushtarak ma‘nawī) words, and identify which category words like 'ayn fall into according to the correct view.

Core Idea
Conceptual common words share one meaning with many individuals; lexical common words have multiple unrelated meanings.

In Arabic, some words can refer to multiple things, but the reason for this multiplicity differs. The commentator distinguishes between two types: lexical common, where the word has multiple separate meanings, and conceptual common, where one meaning applies to many individuals. This passage defines the latter and clarifies the correct position on a debated example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice how the commentator contrasts two types of common words and then gives the correct view.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types
The passage refers to two types: the first is lexical common (multiple distinct coinages) and the second is conceptual common. Identify the definition given for conceptual common.
The example of 'asad' (lion) illustrates the conceptual type.
3
Understand the debate
The commentator mentions that there is a disagreement: some consider words like 'ayn to be lexical common, while others consider them conceptual common. Determine which view is declared correct.
The phrase 'wa-l-taḥqīq al-thānī' indicates the correct view.
4
Apply the definition
Consider the word 'ayn: does it have one meaning with many individuals, or multiple separate meanings? According to the correct view, it is conceptual common. Reflect on why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متعدّد؛ لأنه وُضِعَ للباصرة بوضع وللجارية بوضع، وللذهب والفضة بوضع إلى غير ذلك. وأنها من قبيل المشترك المعنوي على الثاني، وضابطه: أن يتَّجِدَ كلُّ من اللفظ والمعنى، لكن يكون لذلك المعنى أفراد مشتركة فيه، كما في لفظ : أَسَدٍ، فإنه واحد ومعناه واحد، ولكن لمعناه أفراد مشتركة فيه والتحقيق الثاني خلافاً لمن اختار الأول.
Multiple; because it was coined for the eye (bāṣira) by one coinage, for the spring (jāriya) by another coinage, and for gold and silver by yet another coinage, etc. And they (these words) are of the type of common meaning (mushtarak ma‘nawī) according to the second opinion. Its definition is that both the word and the meaning are single, but that meaning has individuals shared in it, as in the word 'asad' (lion), for it is one and its meaning is one, but its meaning has individuals shared in it. And the correct view (taḥqīq) is the second, contrary to those who chose the first.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines two types of common words (muqaddamāt) in Arabic: lexical common (mushtarak lafẓī) and conceptual common (mushtarak ma‘nawī). The commentator begins by referring to the view that words like 'ayn are multiple common lexical words because they were coined separately for different meanings (eye, spring, gold, silver, etc.). He then states that according to the second opinion (likely that of al-Sanūsī or his school), these are actually conceptual common words. The definition of conceptual common is given: the word is one, the meaning is one, but that meaning applies to multiple individuals equally—like 'asad' (lion), which refers to every individual lion without being specific. The commentator declares that the correct view (taḥqīq) is the second (that it is conceptual common), opposing those who chose the first (lexical common).

Key Terms
المشترك اللفظي
المشترك المعنوي
اللفظ
المعنى
الأفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think words like 'ayn are lexical common (multiple unrelated meanings) because they can refer to different things.
Correction: The correct view is that they are conceptual common: the underlying meaning is a general concept that applies to many individuals, not separate unrelated meanings.

Checkpoint
What is the correct view (taḥqīq) regarding words like 'ayn that have multiple referents?
A) They are lexical common (mushtarak lafẓī).
B) They are conceptual common (mushtarak ma‘nawī).
C) They are not common at all.
D) The correct view is the first opinion.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual common words have one meaning that applies to many individuals, and the correct view classifies words like 'ayn under this type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that words like 'iman' (faith) can be conceptual common: one core meaning with many degrees and instances. Reflect on your own iman: do you see each moment of belief and action as a manifestation of the same single faith, or do you treat them as disconnected? How does this insight change how you will nurture your iman today?
?
The correct view in the passage classifies words like 'ayn as conceptual common. Apply this to your acts of worship: is your salah (prayer) one single act of devotion repeated many times, or do you lose the unity of meaning? Will you now approach each prayer as a fresh instance of the same submission, and what will you do to ensure that?

The commentator distinguishes between lexical common (fragmented meanings) and conceptual common (unified meaning in many individuals). Examine your life: are your deeds—charity, fasting, dhikr—unified under the single concept of obedience to Allah, or are they scattered? Synonymy of 'Ilm and Ma'rifa and Their Use for Allah
Sanusi discusses the synonymy of 'ilm and ma'rifa and the restriction of 'arif for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological and linguistic nuance regarding the synonymy of 'ilm and ma'rifa and why Allah is called 'alim but not 'arif.

Core Idea
Knowledge and acquaintance are synonymous, but Allah is only called Knower due to prior ignorance implied by 'acquaintance'.

This lesson examines a subtle point in Islamic theology: the relationship between the words 'ilm (knowledge) and ma'rifa (acquaintance) when attributed to Allah. While they are synonymous in Arabic, tradition restricts 'arif for Allah because it suggests prior ignorance. The passage presents the verification and a scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the main text and identify the claim
Read the main text (first sentence) that states 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonymous, but Allah is called 'alim not 'arif because ma'rifa requires prior ignorance.
Note the exception and the reason given.
2
Understand the distinction between usage and literal meaning
The first gloss explains that the requirement of prior ignorance is in usage (isti'malan) not literal definition (wad'an), so synonymy is not contradicted.
This is a key linguistic nuance.
3
Consider the objection and the response
The second gloss raises an objection: if ma'rifa requires prior ignorance and 'ilm does not, how can they be synonyms? The gloss offers a forced reconciliation. The third gloss clarifies that Zakariya rejected the requirement of prior ignorance, allowing both terms for Allah.
This shows scholarly difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق: أن العِلْمَ والمعرفة مترادفان إلا أنه يُطلق عليه تعالى عالم دون عارِف؛ لأن المعرفةَ تَسْتَدْعِي سَبْقَ الجهل، ومَنَعَ ذلك شيخ الإسلام زكريا، واختار أنه يُطلق عليه تعالى كلّ من عالم وعارف؛. (قوله: تَسْتَدْعِي سَبْقَ الْجَهْلِ :أي: استعمالاً لا وضعاً؛ لأنه غلب استعمالها في الإدراك الحاصل بعد الجهل، فلا ينافي ترادفهما كما تقدم. (قوله: لِأَنَّ الْمَعْرِفَةَ تَسْتَدْعِي... إلخ) فيه: أنها إذا اسْتَدْعَتْ ذلك واستلزمته دون العلم؛ كيف تكون مرادِفَةً له؟ إنما هذا رَأي من يخصها بعِلم مسبوق بجهل، وهو مقابل الترادف، إلا أن يقال: إن المراد: الترادف من حيث شُمول كل للمركبات و الكليات و مقابليهما، لكنه تكلف. (قوله: وَمَنَعَ ذَلِكَ) أي: استدعاءَها سَبْقَ الجهل.
And the verification is that knowledge ('ilm) and acquaintance (ma'rifa) are synonymous except that He Most High is called 'alim (knowing) not 'arif (acquainted), because ma'rifa requires prior ignorance. And Shaykh al-Islam Zakariya prevented that (i.e., the requirement of prior ignorance) and chose that He Most High is called both 'alim and 'arif. (His saying: 'requires prior ignorance' means: in usage not in literal meaning, because its usage predominantly refers to cognition obtained after ignorance, so this does not contradict their synonymy as previously stated. (His saying: 'because ma'rifa requires... etc.') in it: if it requires that and entails it without 'ilm, how can it be synonymous with it? This is only the opinion of one who specifies it with knowledge preceded by ignorance, and it is the opposite of synonymy, unless it is said: what is meant is synonymy in terms of each including composite and universal things and their opposites, but that is forced. (His saying: 'and he prevented that') means: its requiring prior ignorance.)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses a theological and linguistic issue: whether 'ilm (knowledge) and ma'rifa (acquaintance) are synonymous, especially in reference to Allah. The main text states that as a matter of verification (tahqiq), they are synonymous. However, Allah is described as 'alim but not 'arif because ma'rifa etymologically implies prior ignorance, which is impossible for Allah. Shaykh al-Islam Zakariya al-Ansari disagreed with this restriction and allowed both terms for Allah. The accompanying glosses elaborate: the requirement of prior ignorance is by customary usage (isti'malan), not by lexical definition (wad'an), so it does not negate synonymy. Another gloss raises an objection: if ma'rifa requires prior ignorance and 'ilm does not, how can they be synonymous? This objection reflects the view of those who restrict ma'rifa to knowledge after ignorance, which contradicts synonymy. A possible counter is that synonymy is intended in terms of both covering composite and universal concepts, but this is considered forced (takalluf). The final gloss clarifies that Zakariya prevented the requirement of prior ignorance, allowing both terms for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that 'arif is never used for Allah under any circumstances.
Correction: The passage shows that while the main position restricts it, Shaykh al-Islam Zakariya allowed calling Allah 'arif.

B) Because ma'rifa implies prior ignorance
C) Because 'ilm is more comprehensive
D) Because Allah does not know particulars

Key Takeaway
'Ilm and ma'rifa are synonymous in Arabic, but due to customary usage implying prior ignorance, ma'rifa is not applied to Allah in standard theological language, though some scholars permit it.

Synonymity of Ilm and Ma'rifa and the Divine Name 'Alim
Sanusi argues for synonymity with a usage-based exception for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author holds that 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonymous yet restricts the divine name to 'Alim, and how objections to this position are resolved.

Core Idea
Synonymity does not require identical usage; divine perfection bars any term implying prior ignorance.

In this passage, Sanusi investigates a subtle lexical and theological issue: are 'ilm and ma'rifa synonyms? He affirms they are, but notes that Allah is only called 'Alim, not 'Arif, because ma'rifa in common usage presupposes prior ignorance—impossible for Allah. This raises objections about how true synonymity can coexist with a usage difference. The commentary and footnotes address these concerns, grounding the conclusion in Quranic usage and scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and exception
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Sanusi first states the investigation (tahqiq) that 'ilm and ma'rifa are synonyms, then immediately adds an exception: Allah is called 'Alim, not 'Arif. Understand the reason given: because ma'rifa necessitates prior ignorance.
This exception is key to the whole discussion.
2
Understand the objection about synonymity
The text presents a logical objection: if ma'rifa entails prior ignorance, how can it be synonymous with 'ilm, which does not? This objection challenges the possibility of synonymity. Examine how the commentator distinguishes between root meaning (wad') and actual usage (isti'mal).
Pay attention to the terms 'wad'an' and 'isti'malan'.
3
See how the author defends using 'I'lam'
The passage then answers a potential question: why did the author (Sanusi) use the phrase 'i'lam' (Know) rather than 'i'rif' (Recognize) if they are synonyms? The answer is straightforward: because 'i'lam' is the wording of the Quran. This shows that even with synonymity, Quranic precedent guides theological language.
This reinforces the earlier distinction between root meaning and usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله: تستدعي سبق الجهل: أي: استعمالا لا وضعا؛ لأنه غلب استعمالها في الإدراك الحاصل بعد الجهل، فلا ينافي ترادفهما كما تقدم. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
(قوله: لأن المعرفة تستدعي... إلخ) فيه: أنها إذا استدعت ذلك واستلزمته دون العلم؛ كيف تكون مرادفة له؟ إنما هذا رأي من يخصها بعلم مسبوق بجهل، وهو مقابل الترادف، إلا أن يقال: إن المراد: الترادف من حيث شمول كل للمركبات والكليات ومقابليهما، لكنه تكلف.
(قوله: ومنع ذلك) أي: استدعاءها سبق الجهل.
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية
لورود ذلك) لا يقال: إذا كان التحقيق: أن العلم والمعرفة مترادفان، فلم عبر المصنف بـ (اعلم) دون اعرف؟ لأنا نقول: عبر بـ (اعلم)؛ لأنه لفظ القرآن، قال تعالى: فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ.

(1) لعل المحشي يشير إلى الحديث: «طوبى لكل غني تقي، ولكل فقير خفي، يعرفه الله ولا يعرفه الناس». أو إلى الحديث الآخر: «تعرف إلى الله في الرخاء يعرفك في الشدة» إذ فيهما نسبة المعرفة إلى الله تعالى. أما الحديث الأول فسنده ضعيف، وأخرجه العسكري في "الأمثال"، والثاني أخرجه الطبراني في "الكبير" (١١٥٦٠)، والحاكم (٦٣٣/٣). وأخرج ابن أبي شيبة (۱۹۷۰۲)، والبيهقي في "الكبرى" (٤٦/٩) أن عمر بن الخطاب لما أخبر يقتل النعمان بن مقرن، وقيل: أصيب فلان وفلان وآخرون لا نعرفهم، قال: لكن الله يعرفهم. قلنا: إذا سلمنا أن المعرفة يسبقها جهل، والعلم لا يسبقه جهل، ألا يمكن أن نقول: إننا نثبت معرفة تليق به، كما أن له علما يليق بجلاله... وفي "الفروق اللغوية" في الفرق بين المعرفة والعلم (۲۰۳۳) كلام مؤداه أن الأمر في استعمال عبارة (عرف الله) يسير. والله أعلم.
And the investigation is that knowledge (al-ilm) and recognition (al-ma'rifa) are synonymous, except that He, exalted, is called 'Alim (Knowing) and not 'Arif (Recognizer), because recognition necessitates prior ignorance. And Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya prevented that, and he chose that He, exalted, is called both 'Alim and 'Arif.
(His saying: 'necessitates prior ignorance': meaning in usage, not in root meaning; because its usage has prevailed in perception after ignorance, so it does not contradict their synonymity, as previously mentioned. By the Taqrirat of al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi.)
(His saying: 'because recognition necessitates... etc.') In it: That if it necessitates that and entails it without knowledge, how can it be synonymous with it? This is only the view of one who restricts it to knowledge preceded by ignorance, and it is opposed to synonymity, unless it is said: The intended meaning is synonymity in terms of the comprehensiveness of each for compounds and universals and their opposites, but that is strained.
(His saying: 'And he prevented that' meaning: its necessitation of prior ignorance.)
Hashiya of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya
(For the occurrence that) It cannot be said: If the investigation is that knowledge and recognition are synonymous, why did the author express with 'I'lam' (Know) and not 'I'rif' (Recognize)? For we say: He expressed with 'I'lam' because it is the wording of the Quran. He, exalted, said: 'So know that there is no god except Allah.'

(1) Perhaps the commentator points to the hadith: 'Blessed is every wealthy pious person, and every poor hidden person, whom Allah recognizes and people do not recognize him.' Or to the other hadith: 'Recognize Allah in prosperity, He will recognize you in hardship.' For in both, recognition is attributed to Allah. As for the first hadith, its chain is weak, and it was narrated by al-Askari in 'al-Amthal'. The second was narrated by al-Tabarani in 'al-Kabir' (11560), and al-Hakim (3/633). And Ibn Abi Shayba (19702) and al-Bayhaqi in 'al-Kubra' (9/46) narrated that Umar ibn al-Khattab, when informed of the killing of al-Nu'man ibn Muqarrin and it was said: 'So-and-so and so-and-so were struck, and others we do not recognize,' he said: 'But Allah recognizes them.' We say: If we grant that recognition is preceded by ignorance, and knowledge is not preceded by ignorance, can we not say that we affirm a recognition befitting Him, just as He has knowledge befitting His majesty? And in 'al-Furuq al-Lughawiyya' on the difference between recognition and knowledge (2033), there is discussion whose gist is that the matter in using the phrase 'He recognized Allah' is easy. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an investigation (tahqiq) into the relationship between 'ilm and ma'rifa. Sanusi asserts that they are synonyms in lexical meaning, but in theological usage, Allah is described only as 'Alim, not 'Arif, because ma'rifa in common usage implies prior ignorance, which is impossible for Allah. Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya disagreed and allowed both. The commentary then addresses a logical objection: if ma'rifa necessitates prior ignorance, how can it be synonymous? It replies that the synonymity is in root meaning, not usage. The commentator al-Bajuri answers why the author used 'i'lam' rather than 'i'rif': because it is the Quranic wording. The footnote provides hadith evidence and linguistic discussion to support that ma'rifa can be attributed to Allah in a befitting manner, though the author's position is cautious.

معرفة
ترادف
عالم
عارف
سبق الجهل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That synonymity in root meaning prevents any difference in usage; here the distinction is purely based on conventional usage, not lexical meaning.
Correction: Synonymity in root meaning (wad') does not preclude differences in actual usage (isti'mal). The author uses this distinction to explain why Allah is called 'Alim rather than 'Arif even though the terms are lexically synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Ilm and ma'rifa are synonymous in root meaning, but in divine attribution, only 'Alim is used because ma'rifa implies prior ignorance, and the Quran uses 'I'lam'.

Definition of al-Ḥukm al-Sharʿī and Its Nature
Sanusi defines hukm sharʿī; al-Ajhuri answers an objection; al-Anbabi clarifies taʿalluq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ḥukm al-sharʿī as divine speech relating to a person's act in terms of taklīf or waḍʿ, the objection that it is not an attribution and the two answers, the meaning of its components, and the nature of its relation to acts as one of indication, not effect.

Core Idea
The sharʿī ruling is divine speech itself, not a human judgment, and its relation to acts is one of indication, not causation.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barāhīn begins by defining the third type of judgment: the sharʿī ruling (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī). Sanusi's definition is concise, but its implications are far-reaching. The commentator al-Ajhūrī raises a critical objection: how can divine speech be called a 'ruling' when a ruling normally attributes one thing to another? Two answers are provided, one based on necessary consequence and the other on disciplinary terminology. Then the meaning of key terms like taklīf and waḍʿ is unpacked, and al-Anbābī clarifies that the relation (taʿalluq) is one of indication, not effect, leading to a debate about whether the ruling is eternal or originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and ask: what makes it a ruling?
Carefully read Sanusi's definition: 'the speech of Allah that relates to the act of a person in terms of obligation or prescription.' Consider why this is called a 'ruling' (ḥukm) and how it differs from everyday rulings.
The definition is very compact; each word carries technical meaning.
2
Examine the objection and the two answers
Al-Ajhūrī raises an objection: divine speech does not itself establish one thing for another. Work through the two answers—the first based on necessary consequence, the second on disciplinary terminology.
The two answers show different ways to resolve the objection.
3
Analyze the meanings of taklīf and waḍʿ
Study the explanations of 'obligation' (taklīf) and 'prescription' (waḍʿ). Note that taklīf includes demand with its four divisions and permission, while waḍʿ includes making something a cause, condition, or preventative.
These are technical terms; their precise meaning is essential.
4
Understand the nature of taʿalluq and its implications
Al-Anbābī clarifies that the relation (taʿalluq) of the ruling to the act is one of indication (dalāla), not effect or disclosure. This relation occurs when the demand is made, but the ruling itself remains eternal.
This leads to a dispute with al-Maḥallī about whether the ruling is eternal or originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : الحكم الشرعى وهو كلامُ الله المتعلق بفعل الشخص من حيث التكليف أو الوضع له.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَهُوَ كَلَامُ الله ... إلخ) إن قيل: إن كلام الله ليس إثبات أمر لأمر... إلخ؛ فالجواب بأحد أمرين: الأول: أنه إثبات باعتبار لازمه ؛ لأن كلام الله الذي اقتضى الطلب على وجه الجزم يتضمن إثباتنا الوجوب .وهكذا الثاني: أن تعريف الحكم الشرعي بما ذکر اصطلاح للأصوليين، واصطلاح الفقهاء أن الحكم الشرعي: إثبات أمر لأمر أو نفيه عنه بواسطة الدليل الشرعي.
(قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقُ بِفِعْل الشَّخْصِ) أي: الدال عليه.
(قوله: مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّكْلِيف :أي لا من حيث إنه مخلوق أو ممكن مثلاً، ومراده بالتكليف ما يعم الطلب بأقسامه الأربعة والإباحة.
(وقوله: أوِ الْوَضْع لَهُ أي للتكليف الشامل للطلب بأقسامه الأربعة وللإباحة ومعنى الوضع لذلك: جعل الشارع لذلك سبباً أو شرطاً أو مانعاً وإثباته الصحة إن استوفى الأمور المعتبرة فيه والفساد إن لم يستوفها، فالطلب بأقسامه الأربعة والإباحة والوضع بالمعنى المتقدم مدلولة للكلام كما يعلم من "حاشية الهدهدي" وإن كان الكلام نفسه طلباً وإباحة ووضعاً باعتبار دلالته عليها كما يعلم أيضاً من الحاشية المذكورة، والظاهر أن إطلاق الطلب والإباحة والوضع على الكلام باعتبار تلك الدلالة مجازي من إطلاق اسم المدلول على الدال إلا أن يكونوا اصطلحوا على ذلك الإطلاق، فيكون إطلاقاً حقيقياً.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
(قوله: (الْمُتَعَلَّقُ) أي: تعلّق دِلالةٍ، لا تأثير ولا انكشاف، والمراد: تعلّقاً تنجيزياً حادثاً عند توجه الطلب، ولا يلزم من حدوث التعلق الذي هو صِفَةُ الكلام حدوث الحُكْمِ المفسر بالكلام المذكور؛ لأن التعلُّق المذكور ليس صِفَةً حقيقة، بل هو نِسْبَةٌ واعتبار، فلا يلزم من حدوثها حدوث موصوفها، فالحكم قديم لا حادث، وذهب العلامة المَحَلِّي إلى حدوثه.
And the third: the sharʿī ruling (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī), which is the speech of Allah (kalām Allāh) that relates to the act of a person in terms of obligation (taklīf) or prescription (waḍʿ) for it.
Gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī:
(His saying: AND IT IS THE SPEECH OF ALLAH ... etc.) If it is said: The speech of Allah is not an establishing of one thing for another ... etc.; then the answer is by one of two things: The first: that it is an establishing in consideration of its necessary consequence; because the speech of Allah that necessitated demand in a decisive manner entails our establishing obligation. And likewise the second: that the definition of sharʿī ruling by what was mentioned is a technical term of the uṣūliyyūn, and the technical term of the jurists is that the sharʿī ruling is: the establishing of a thing for a thing or its negation from it by means of a sharʿī proof.
(His saying: THAT RELATES TO THE ACT OF THE PERSON) i.e., indicating it.
(His saying: IN TERMS OF OBLIGATION) i.e., not in terms of it being created or possible, for example, and his intent by obligation (taklīf) is what includes demand (ṭalab) with its four divisions and permissibility (ibāḥa).
(And his saying: OR PRESCRIPTION FOR IT) i.e., for obligation that encompasses demand with its four divisions and permissibility; and the meaning of prescription (waḍʿ) for that: the Lawgiver's making for that a cause (sabab), or a condition (sharṭ), or a preventative (māniʿ), and establishing its validity (ṣiḥḥa) if it fulfills the considerations in it, and its corruption (fasād) if it does not fulfill them. So demand with its four divisions, and permissibility, and prescription in the aforementioned sense are what is indicated by the speech, as is known from the Gloss of al-Hudhayfī, even though the speech itself is demand, permissibility, and prescription in consideration of its indication of them, as is also known from the aforementioned gloss. And the apparent is that applying the terms demand, permissibility, and prescription to speech in consideration of that indication is metaphorical, from applying the name of the signified to the signifier, unless they have made a technical convention for that application, in which case it is a real application.
Annotations of the scholar Muḥammad al-Anbābī.
(His saying: THAT RELATES) i.e., a relation of indication (taʿalluq dalāla), not of effect (taʾthīr) nor of disclosure (inkishāf). And the intended meaning is an actualized relation that occurs at the time of the demand, and it does not follow from the occurrence of the relation which is a quality (ṣifa) of speech the occurrence of the ruling that is explained by the aforementioned speech; because the mentioned relation is not a real quality, but rather a relation and consideration, so its occurrence does not entail the occurrence of its qualified entity (mawṣūf). So the ruling is eternal (qadīm), not originated (ḥādith), and the scholar al-Maḥallī held it to be originated.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage begins with Sanusi's definition of the third type of judgment: the sharʿī ruling (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī), which is divine speech relating to a person's act in terms of either obligation (taklīf) or prescription (waḍʿ). Al-Ajhūrī then raises an objection: how can this be a 'ruling' since it is not the attribution of one thing to another (e.g., 'prayer is obligatory') but rather the divine speech itself? The answer is twofold. First, the divine speech that demands an act necessarily implies the attribution of obligation—so the ruling is established by way of its necessary consequence. Second, the definition varies by discipline: in uṣūl al-fiqh, it is defined as divine speech relating to the act; in fiqh, as the attribution or negation of a thing by a sharʿī proof. Al-Ajhūrī then clarifies key terms: 'relating to the act' means indicating it; 'in terms of obligation' excludes other aspects like creation; 'prescription' includes making something a cause, condition, or preventative, and affirming validity or corruption. He notes that demand, permissibility, and prescription are properly what the speech indicates, though the speech itself can be called by these names metaphorically. Finally, al-Anbābī comments on 'relation' (taʿalluq): it is a relation of indication, not of effect or disclosure, and it is an originated relation that occurs when the demand is addressed. This relation is not a real attribute of speech but a relative consideration, so its origination does not imply that the ruling (the eternal divine speech) is originated. Al-Maḥallī disagreed and held that the ruling itself is originated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the sharʿī ruling is the same as the jurist's act of attributing something to an act (e.g., 'prayer is obligatory').
Correction: The sharʿī ruling is divine speech itself; the attribution is either a necessary consequence or a separate definition used by jurists.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhūrī's answer to the objection, what is the first way to resolve the claim that ḥukm sharʿī is not an attribution?
A) The definition is a technical term of the jurists only.
B) The ḥukm is an attribution by way of the necessary consequence of the speech.
C) The ḥukm is actually the act of the mujtahid, not the speech.
D) The speech itself is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
The sharʿī ruling is eternal divine speech; its relation to acts is a relation of indication that occurs when the act is performed, but the speech itself remains uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you truly believe that the ruling concerning it is eternal divine speech that has always existed, or do you unconsciously treat it as something that only came into being when you acted? How can you cultivate a deeper awe for Allah's eternal command in your daily obedience?
?
The lesson distinguishes between divine speech itself and our human process of establishing obligation. Are you prone to equating your own deductions or understandings of what Allah requires with His eternal speech itself? In what areas of your life might you be unknowingly elevating your own judgments above the divine word? What step will you take today to humble yourself before the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Al-Anbābī says the relation of the ruling to your act is one of indication, not causation. This means your action does not affect Allah's speech; rather, the speech indicates whether your action is valid or not. How does this reality challenge any sense you might have that your deeds 'create' or 'earn' a divine response? Will you live today with the awareness that every action is simply the place where eternal speech meets your choice, and you are being addressed by the One who never changes?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
5 min
The Two Kinds of Sharʿī Ruling and the Five Divine Addresses of Obligation
Sanusi divides hukm sharʿī into two kinds and enumerates the five subdivisions of taklīf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary categories of divine address (taklīf and waḍʿ) and the specific rulings within the category of taklīf.

Core Idea
Divine speech about human actions is divided into commanding and prescribing.

Sanusi begins by establishing the fundamental division of all sharʿī rulings. Before exploring the details of each category, he first defines the two overarching types: address of obligation (khitāb taklīf) and address of prescription (khitāb waḍʿ). In this lesson we focus on the first type and its five specific rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's own definitions come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, definitional style.
2
Distinguish the two categories
Identify the two overarching categories: khitāb taklīf and khitāb waḍʿ. Note how each is defined in relation to divine speech and human action.
The key difference lies in 'from the perspective of obligation' vs. 'from the perspective of prescription'.
3
Learn the five subdivisions of taklīf
Memorize the five rulings: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), dislike (karāha), and permissibility (ibāḥa). For each, note the definition in terms of demand decisiveness.
Pay attention to the contrast between decisive and non-decisive demands.
4
Reflect on the definitions
Ask yourself: Why is permissibility defined as giving choice rather than a demand? How does this fit with the idea of obligation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينحصر في قسمين: خطاب تكليف وهو كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلّق بفعل الشخص من حيث التكليف، وخطاب وضع وهو كلام الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل الشخص من حيث الوضع.
وللأول خمسة أقسام الإيجاب وهو كلام الله تعالى المتعلّق بطلب فِعْل الشيء طَلَباً جازماً، والندب: وهو كلام الله تعالى المتعلّق بطلب فعل الشيء طلباً غير جازم، والتحريم: وهو كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلّق بطلب تزك الشيء طلباً جازماً، والكراهة - ولو خفيفةً : وهي كلامُ الله المتعلّق بطلب ترك الشيء طلباً غير جازم، والإباحة: وهي كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلّق بالتخيير بين فِعْل الشيء وتَرْكِه. وللثاني خمسة أقسام أيضاً: وهي كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلق بكون الشيء سبباً، أو شرطاً، أو مانعاً، أو صحيحاً، أو فاسداً.
وإذا نظرت لكون هذه الخمسة تجري مع كلّ واحدٍ من الخمسة السابقة؛ كانت الجملةُ: خَمْسَةٌ ،وعشرين قائمةً من ضَرْبِ خمسة في مِثْلِها.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: بِكَوْنِ الشَّيْءِ سَبَباً) أي بجعله سبباً، وهذا الجعل المعبر عنه فيما تقدم بالوضع (قوله: وَإِذَا نَظَرْتَ.... إلخ) لكن الصحة والفساد لا يجريان في المحرم إلا إذا كان
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
(قوله: وَالْإِيجَابُ وَهُوَ كَلَامُ اللهِ... إلخ) أي: فالإيجابُ والتحريم والكراهة والنَّدْبُ والإباحة أسماء للكلام القديم، وجَعْلُ الندب والكراهة من الأحكام التكليفية ظاهر على القول بأن التكليف طَلَبُ ما فيه كُلْفَةٌ، أما على أنه: إلزام ما فيه كُلْفَةٌ؛ فلا بُدَّ من اعتبار التغليب، وكذا لا بُدَّ من اعتباره بالنسبة للإباحة عليهما.
(قوله: كَانَتِ الْجُمْلَةُ خَمْسَةٌ وَعِشْرِينَ أمثلة ذلك: وجوب البيع، سَبَبُهُ: اضطرار المشتري، وشَرْطُهُ التكليف ومانعه اضطرار البائع وصحة البيع باستكمال الشروط، وفساده بانتفائه.
And it is confined to two kinds: address of obligation (khitāb taklīf) which is the speech of Allah Most High relating to the act of a person in terms of obligation (taklīf), and address of prescription (khitāb waḍʿ) which is the speech of Allah Most High relating to the act of a person in terms of prescription (waḍʿ).
For the first there are five divisions: obligation (ījāb), which is the speech of Allah Most High relating to demanding the doing of a thing with a decisive demand; recommendation (nadb), which is the speech of Allah Most High relating to demanding the doing of a thing with a non-decisive demand; prohibition (taḥrīm), which is the speech of Allah Most High relating to demanding the abandonment of a thing with a decisive demand; dislike (karāha) – even if slight – which is the speech of Allah relating to demanding the abandonment of a thing with a non-decisive demand; and permissibility (ibāḥa), which is the speech of Allah Most High relating to giving choice between the doing of a thing and its abandonment.
For the second there are also five divisions: they are the speech of Allah Most High relating to a thing being a cause (sabab), or a condition (sharṭ), or a preventative (māniʿ), or valid (ṣaḥīḥ), or corrupt (fāsid).
And if you consider that these five run alongside each one of the previous five, the total becomes twenty-five, resulting from multiplying five by its like.
Gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī:
(His saying: A THING BEING A CAUSE) i.e., by His making it a cause, and this making is what was previously expressed as prescription (waḍʿ). (His saying: AND IF YOU CONSIDER ... etc.) But validity and corruption do not apply in the case of the prohibited except if ...
Annotations of the scholar Muḥammad al-Anbābī:
(His saying: AND OBLIGATION, WHICH IS THE SPEECH OF ALLAH ... etc.) i.e., obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, and permissibility are names for the eternal speech. And making recommendation and dislike from the prescriptive rulings is apparent according to the view that obligation is a demand of what involves difficulty; as for the view that it is an imposition of what involves difficulty, then consideration of preponderance is necessary, and likewise must be considered with respect to permissibility for both.
(His saying: THE TOTAL BECOMES TWENTY-FIVE) Examples of that: the obligation of a sale – its cause: the purchaser's necessity; its condition: obligation; its preventative: the seller's necessity; and the validity of the sale by fulfillment of conditions, and its corruption by their absence.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification. Sanusi states that the sharʿī ruling is confined to two kinds: (1) address of obligation (khitāb taklīf) – divine speech relating to a person's act in terms of obligation, and (2) address of prescription (khitāb waḍʿ) – divine speech relating to the act in terms of prescription (making something a cause, condition, etc.). He then details the five divisions of the first kind: obligation (ījāb) – decisive demand to do; recommendation (nadb) – non-decisive demand to do; prohibition (taḥrīm) – decisive demand to abandon; dislike (karāha) – non-decisive demand to abandon; and permissibility (ibāḥa) – giving choice. For the second kind, also five: speech relating to a thing being a cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), preventative (māniʿ), valid (ṣaḥīḥ), or corrupt (fāsid). Sanusi then notes that if you consider the five of the second kind running alongside each of the five of the first kind, the total combinations are twenty-five (5 × 5). Al-Ajhūrī comments that 'being a cause' means His making it a cause, which is the same as 'prescription'. He also notes that validity and corruption do not apply in the prohibited case. Al-Anbābī explains that the names (obligation, etc.) are names of the eternal speech; he clarifies that including recommendation and dislike as prescriptive rulings is straightforward on the view that taklīf is a demand of what involves difficulty, but if taklīf is an imposition of what involves difficulty, then preponderance must be considered; similarly for permissibility. He then gives an example: the obligation of a sale has its cause (purchaser's necessity), condition (legal competence), preventative (seller's necessity), and then validity or corruption depending on fulfillment of conditions.

Key Terms
خطاب التكليف
خطاب الوضع
الإيجاب
الندب
التحريم
الكراهة
الإباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that prescriptive rulings (cause, condition, etc.) are not part of divine speech.
Correction: The passage affirms that both khitāb taklīf and khitāb waḍʿ are divine speech (kalam Allah). The waḍʿ rulings are also divine speech relating to the act.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes khitāb taklīf from khitāb waḍʿ according to Sanusi?
A) The first is about commands, the second about prohibitions.
B) The first relates to obligation, the second to making something a cause, condition, etc.
C) The first is for individuals, the second for communities.
D) The first is eternal, the second is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The sharʿī ruling is confined to two kinds: address of obligation and address of prescription; the five subdivisions of obligation are obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, and permissibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the five types of divine address do you most often encounter in your daily life? Are you responding to it with the appropriate level of urgency or are you treating a decisive demand as optional?
?
Consider a recommendation (nadb) that you have been neglecting. Why is it not sinful to avoid it, yet you may be missing a greater reward? What is one specific recommendation you will act upon today?
?
When you face a permissible action (ibāḥa), do you pause to thank Allah for the choice, or do you take it for granted? How can you transform a permissible act into an act of worship by intention?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
The Five Prescriptive Rulings and the Total Combinations
Sanusi enumerates the five divisions of khitāb waḍʿ and calculates the total combinations with khitāb taklīf, with glosses clarifying points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of prescriptive rulings (cause, condition, preventative, valid, corrupt) and how they combine with the five obligation rulings to produce twenty-five possible combinations.

Core Idea
Every prescriptive ruling can combine with every obligation ruling, creating twenty-five possible divine addresses.

Having learned the five rulings of divine command, Sanusi now presents the five rulings that establish the framework for causes, conditions, and validity. He then reveals a mathematical truth: any act can be assessed through both lenses simultaneously. The accompanying glosses clarify key points about these categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage that lists the five waḍʿ rulings and the calculation of the total. Then read the translation.
Notice the phrase 'kullu wāḥidin min al-khamsati s-sābiqati' meaning each one of the previous five.
2
Learn the five waḍʿ terms
Memorize the five prescriptive rulings: cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), preventative (māniʿ), valid (ṣaḥīḥ), and corrupt (fāsid). Understand each as a type of divine speech.
These are not demands but designations.
3
Understand the combination calculation
Sanusi states that each of the five waḍʿ rulings can apply to each of the five taklīf rulings, resulting in 5 × 5 = 25. Consider examples like the obligation of a sale having a cause, condition, etc.
This shows the systematic nature of Islamic legal theory.
4
Study the glosses
Read the glosses by al-Ajhūrī and al-Anbābī. Al-Ajhūrī clarifies that 'being a cause' is by divine making (waḍʿ). Al-Anbābī explains that the taklīf names are eternal speech, and discusses the inclusion of nadb and karāha under taklīf.
These glosses resolve potential ambiguities.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification. Sanusi states that the sharʿī ruling is confined to two kinds: (1) address of obligation (khitāb taklīf) – divine speech relating to a person's act in terms of obligation, and (2) address of prescription (khitāb waḍʿ) – divine speech relating to the act in terms of prescription (making something a cause, condition, etc.). He then details the five divisions of the first kind: obligation (ījāb) – decisive demand to do; recommendation (nadb) – non-decisive demand to do; prohibition (taḥrīm) – decisive demand to abandon; dislike (karāha) – non-decisive demand to abandon; and permissibility (ibāḥa) – giving choice. For the second kind, also five: speech relating to a thing being a cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), preventative (māniʿ), valid (ṣaḥīḥ), or corrupt (fāsid). Sanusi then notes that if you consider the five of the second kind running alongside each of the five of the first kind, the total combinations are twenty-five (5 × 5). Al-Ajhūrī comments that 'being a cause' means His making it a cause, which is the same as 'prescription'. He also notes that validity and corruption do not apply in the prohibited case. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you take has a divine cause, condition, and potential for validity or corruption, how would you re-evaluate a recent decision you made without considering Allah's speech? Can you trace its shariah components now?
?
Knowing that Allah's address to you includes not only commands but also these prescriptive dimensions, does it change how you view your daily habits? What is one habit you now see as having a divine condition you previously ignored?
?
The combination of prescriptions yields 25 possible divine addresses. How many of these do you consciously consider in your life? Are you content with ignoring some, or does this call you to a more comprehensive awareness of Allah's speech?

The Two Types of Divine Address
Sanusi defines khitab taklif and khitab wad`.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of divine address: address of obligation (khitab taklif) and address of positing (khitab wad`).

Core Idea
Divine speech is analyzed as either commanding or assigning status.

Sanusi begins by dividing divine address into two fundamental types. Understanding this division is essential because all subsequent rulings in theology and law depend on whether an address imposes an obligation or establishes a legal status. This lesson introduces the definitions and the distinction between them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English carefully.
2
Identify the two types
Notice the definitions and the key phrases 'min hayth al-taklif' and 'min hayth al-wad'.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about how obligation (taklif) differs from positing (wad`) in terms of divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينحصر في قسمين: خطاب تكليف وهو كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلّق بفعل الشخص من حيث التكليف، وخطاب وضع وهو كلام الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل الشخص من حيث الوضع. وللأول خمسة أقسام الإيجاب وهو كلام الله تعالى المتعلّق بطلب فِعْل الشيء طَلَباً جازماً، والندب: وهو كلام الله تعالى المتعلّق بطلب فعل الشيء طلباً غير جازم، والتحريم: وهو كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلّق بطلب ترك الشيء طلباً جازماً، والكراهة - ولو خفيفةً : وهي كلامُ الله المتعلّق بطلب ترك الشيء طلباً غير جازم، والإباحة: وهي كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلّق بالتخيير بين فِعْل الشيء وتَرْكِه. وللثاني خمسة أقسام أيضاً: وهي كلامُ الله تعالى المتعلق بكون الشيء سبباً، أو شرطاً، أو مانعاً، أو صحيحاً، أو فاسداً. وإذا نظرت لكون هذه الخمسة تجري مع كلّ واحدٍ من الخمسة السابقة؛ كانت الجملةُ: خَمْسَةٌ وعشرين قائمةً من ضَرْبِ خمسة في مِثْلِها.
And it is confined to two divisions: Khitab al-Taklif (address of obligation), which is the speech of Allah Most High related to the action of the person with respect to obligation; and Khitab al-Wad' (address of positing), which is the speech of Allah Most High related to the action of the person with respect to positing. The first has five categories: al-Ijab (obligation) which is the speech of Allah Most High related to requesting the doing of something as a definitive request; al-Nadb (recommendation) which is the speech of Allah Most High related to requesting the doing of something without definitiveness; al-Tahrim (prohibition) which is the speech of Allah Most High related to requesting the abandonment of something as a definitive request; al-Karahah (dislike) which is the speech of Allah Most High related to requesting the abandonment of something without definitiveness; and al-Ibahah (permissibility) which is the speech of Allah Most High related to giving the choice between doing something and leaving it. The second also has five categories: they are the speech of Allah Most High related to a thing being a cause (sabab), condition (shart), impediment (mani'), sound (sahih), or corrupt (fasid). And if you consider that these five apply together with each one of the preceding five, then the total becomes twenty-five derived from multiplying five by its like.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from the matn defines the two types of divine address (khitab) that form the basis of Islamic legal theory. It first distinguishes between khitab al-taklif (address of obligation) and khitab al-wad' (address of positing). The former concerns commands that obligate, recommend, prohibit, discourage, or permit an action. The latter concerns the legal status of actions as causes, conditions, impediments, valid, or invalid. The passage then enumerates the five categories of each. For taklif: obligation (ijab), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (tahrim), dislike (karahah), and permission (ibahah). For wad': cause, condition, impediment, soundness, and corruption. Finally, it points out that these two sets interact: each of the five wad' categories can apply to each of the five taklif categories, yielding twenty-five possible combinations. This framework is foundational for understanding how divine commands are analyzed in Sunni theology and law.

Key Terms
خطاب تكليف
خطاب وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between khitab taklif and khitab wad' according to Sanusi?
A) Khitab taklif is about obligation, khitab wad' is about prohibition.
B) Khitab taklif is about obligation, khitab wad' is about legal status.
C) Khitab taklif is about creation, khitab wad' is about command.
D) Both are the same.

Key Takeaway
Divine address is divided into two types: one that commands actions and one that declares their legal status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse from the Qur'an, do you instinctively assume it is a command (taklif) or a declaration of status (wad‘)? How might this assumption distort your understanding of Allah's speech if you always choose one over the other? Commit to checking which category a verse belongs to before acting on it.
?
Think of a specific trial you faced recently. Did you perceive it as a direct command to be patient (taklif), or as a status declared by Allah (wad‘) that made it a cause for expiation? How does your perception change your attitude and reliance on Allah? What would change if you viewed it as the other type?
?
In your daily life, you constantly encounter things that are causes (like sunrise for prayer time) and conditions (like purity for prayer). Do you ever thank Allah for these wad‘i signs as much as you strive to obey His commands? What might be missing in your gratitude if you only focus on taklif?

The Five Categories of Taklif
Sanusi lists the five types of khitab taklif.

Core Idea
The five taklif categories cover all divine commands and permissions.

The Five Categories of Wad'
Sanusi lists the five types of khitab wad'.

Learning Objective

Khitab wad' assigns legal attributes to actions.

Just as khitab taklif has five categories, khitab wad' also has five. These categories describe the legal status of actions as being causes, conditions, impediments, valid, or invalid. This lesson presents them.

The 25 Combinations of Taklif and Wad'
Sanusi calculates the total combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the five taklif categories combine with the five wad' categories to produce 25 possible divine addresses.

Core Idea
Each wad' category can apply to each taklif category, yielding 25 types.

Sanusi concludes by noting that the two sets of five categories interact. Every wad' attribute (cause, condition, etc.) can be applied to every taklif command (obligation, etc.), resulting in 25 distinct combinations. This lesson explains that multiplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the final sentence about multiplication.
2
Understand the interaction
Consider how a cause can be attached to an obligation, a recommendation, etc.
3
Calculate the total
Multiply 5 by 5 to get 25, as the passage states.

The Five Taklif Categories as Names of Eternal Speech and Taghlib
Al-Ajhuri explains the ontological status of the five categories and the role of taghlib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the five categories of taklif are names of God's eternal speech and why taghlib (predominance) must be considered under certain definitions of taklif.

Core Idea
The five rulings are not just labels; they are names of God's eternal speech.

In this passage, al-Ajhuri clarifies a foundational point in Islamic theology: the five legal categories (obligation, prohibition, etc.) are not merely human constructs but are names of God's eternal speech. He then addresses a subtle nuance regarding how the definition of taklif affects the inclusion of certain categories, introducing the principle of taghlib. This helps us appreciate the depth of kalam reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories and their relation to God's speech
Read the passage and note that al-Ajhuri explicitly states that al-Ijab, al-Tahrim, al-Karahah, al-Nadb, and al-Ibahah are names of the eternal speech (kalam qadim). This is a key ontological claim.
2
Understand the two definitions of taklif
The passage compares two definitions: taklif as 'requesting what involves difficulty' (talab ma fihi kulfa) and as 'imposing what involves difficulty' (ilzam ma fihi kulfa). See how each affects nadb and karahah.
The first definition includes them directly; the second requires taghlib.
3
Apply taghlib to ibahah
Notice that ibahah (permission) requires taghlib under both definitions because it does not involve any request or imposition of difficulty.
This shows the careful reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْإِيجَابُ وَهُوَ كَلَامُ اللهِ... إلخ) أي: فالإيجابُ والتحريم والكراهة والنَّدْبُ والإباحة أسماء للكلام القديم، وجَعْلُ الندب والكراهة من الأحكام التكليفية ظاهر على القول بأن التكليف طَلَبُ ما فيه كُلْفَةٌ، أما على أنه: إلزام ما فيه كُلْفَةٌ؛ فلا بُدَّ من اعتبار التغليب، وكذا لا بُدَّ من اعتباره بالنسبة للإباحة عليهما.
(His saying: 'And al-Ijab and it is the speech of Allah... etc.') Meaning: So al-Ijab, al-Tahrim, al-Karahah, al-Nadb, and al-Ibahah are names of the eternal speech. And making nadb and karahah from the obligational rulings is apparent according to the saying that taklif is 'requesting that which involves difficulty.' However, according to the definition that it is 'imposing that which involves difficulty,' then taghlib (predominance) must be considered. Likewise, it must be considered with respect to ibahah with regard to both definitions.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a gloss by al-Ajhuri explaining a key theological point: the five categories of taklif (obligation, prohibition, etc.) are not merely legal constructs but are names of God's eternal speech (kalam qadim). The gloss then addresses a subtle issue concerning the definition of taklif. If taklif is defined as 'requesting something that involves difficulty' (talab ma fihi kulfa), then nadb (recommendation) and karahah (dislike) clearly fall under it. But if taklif is defined as 'imposing something that involves difficulty' (ilzam ma fihi kulfa), then nadb and karahah are not strictly imposing, so the term taklif is applied to them by taghlib (predominance or analogical extension). Similarly, ibahah (permission) does not involve requesting at all, so it requires taghlib under both definitions. This shows the careful theological reasoning about the scope of divine address.

Key Terms
الكلام القديم
التغليب
التكليف
الطلب
الإلزام
الكلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five taklif categories are just legal labels with no relation to God's eternal speech.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri clarifies that al-Ijab, al-Tahrim, al-Karahah, al-Nadb, and al-Ibahah are names of the eternal speech, meaning they are ontologically rooted in God's attribute of speech.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, under the definition of taklif as 'ilzam ma fihi kulfa', what must be considered for nadb and karahah?
A) They are directly included
B) They require taghlib
C) They are not part of taklif
D) They are part of wad'

Key Takeaway
The five taklif categories are names of God's eternal speech, and their inclusion under taklif may require taghlib depending on the definition of taklif.

How would your heart respond differently if you truly believed every command is a direct address from the Eternal One? ?
