مبني على القول بثبوت الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء أربعة أقسام موجودات وهي ما تَصِحُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، ومعدومات: ما لا تُبُوتَ له. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَمَعْدُومَاتٌ : وَهِيَ مَا لَا تُبُوتَ (لَهُ أي ليس له تحقق في نفسه، أو له تحقق لكن مفهومه عدميّ، فشَمِلَتِ المعدوماتُ : المعدوماتِ المَحْضَةَ، كابن زيد في حالة عدمه، والعدميات كبقاء الله ،وقدّمه وإلا وَرَدَ عليه أن الأشياء خمسةٌ بزيادة العدميات تأمل. حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية وأحوال: وهي الوَاسِطَةُ بين الموجودات والمعدومات، وأمور اعتبارية: وهي ما له ثبوت لكنه لم يَرْتَقِ إلى درجة الأحوال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوَاسِطَةُ... إلخ) بأن لم تفسر بالعدم لتخرج المعدومات، ولم تمكن رؤيتها لتخرج الصفات الوجودية. (قوله: وَهِيَ مَا لَهُ ثُبوت) أي في الواقع ونفس الأمر كقيام القدرة بذاته تعالى، فإنه ثابت في الواقع بقطع النظر عن الذهن؛ لأنه عين ثبوت القدرة للذات، وهو حاصل قطعاً، بدليل استدلالهم عليه بقولهم : لو انتفت القدرة عنه تعالى إلى آخر البرهان الآتي، فإن الاستدلال عليه بذلك يدل على حصوله في الواقع؛ إذ لا يستدلّ إلا على الأمور الواقعية، ويدلُّ على حصول الأمر الاعتباري في الواقع ونفس الأمر بقطع النظر عن الذهني تقسيمهم له إلى قديم وحادث، ولو كان ذهنياً فقط؛ لبطل هذا التقسيم؛ لأن الذهني يكون إلا حادثاً، ووجه كون الحال أرقى منه على هذا أن الحال قار للذات - أي: وصف. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي - لا (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوَاسِطَةُ ... إلخ) أي: بأن كان له ثبوت في نفسه أرقى من ثبوت الاعتبار إلا أنه لم ينته إلى درجة الوجود، والحالُ تنقسم قسمين: نفسية ومعنوية، وعبارة المصنف في "شرح الكبرى" والقائلون بثبوت الحال كالقاضي وإمام الحرمين يَقْسِمُون الصفاتِ ثلاثةَ أقسام: نفسية ومعنوية ومعاني. ووجه الحصر : أن المتحقق؛ إما أن يتحقق باعتبار نفسه، أو باعتبار غيره الأول الموجود، والثاني الحال، وهو إما أن يكون الغيرُ الذي تحقق به ذاتاً موصوفة، أو معنى يَقُومُ بموصوف، الأول الحالُ النفسية، والثاني الحال المعنوية اهـ. قال اليوسي: ما ذُكِرَ من التقسيم إلى ثلاثة أقسام هو باعتبار الصفة الثبوتية. (قوله: وَأُمُورٌ اعْتِبَارِيَّةً وَهِيَ مَا لَهُ ثُبُوتٌ أي في نفسه على الخلاف في ذلك. (وقوله: لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَرْتَقِ إِلَى دَرَجَةِ الْأَحْوَالِ بأن كان ثبوته أقل من ثبوتها، ومثلوا الاعتبار بالإمكان والوجود وغير ذلك، وللحال بالكون قادراً والكون مريداً وغير ذلك.
Based on the affirmation of states (ahwal) based on the method that says things are four divisions: existents, which are what can be seen; and non-existents: what has no subsistence. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'And non-existents: they are what has no subsistence' meaning it has no realization in itself, or it has realization but its concept is negative, so the non-existents include pure non-existents like Ibn Zayd in the state of his non-existence, and negative things like the everlastingness of God, and he presented it, otherwise it would be objected that things become five with the addition of negative things, reflect. The gloss of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusiyya: And states (ahwal): they are the intermediary between existents and non-existents, and conceptual things (umur i'tibariyya): they are what have subsistence but have not risen to the degree of states. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'And they are the intermediary... etc.' meaning they are not interpreted by non-existence to exclude non-existents, nor can they be seen to exclude existential attributes. His saying: 'And they are what have subsistence' meaning in reality and actual fact, like the subsistence of power in His essence, Exalted, for it is established in reality regardless of the mind because it is identical to the subsistence of power for the essence, and it is definitely actual, as evidenced by their argument: if power were negated from Him, Exalted, to the end of the proof that follows, for the argument for it indicates its occurrence in reality, since one only argues for real things. And it indicates the occurrence of the conceptual thing in reality and actual fact regardless of the mind, their division of it into pre-eternal and originated; if it were only mental, this division would be invalid because the mental is only originated. And the reason the state is higher than it is that the state is inherent in the essence – i.e., a description. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: No, his saying: 'And they are the intermediary... etc.' means that it has subsistence in itself higher than the subsistence of the conceptual, but it has not reached the degree of existence. And the state (hal) is divided into two types: nafsiyya (essential) and ma'nawiyya (mental). And the wording of the author in 'Sharh al-Kubra': Those who affirm the state, like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, divide attributes into three types: nafsiyya, ma'nawiyya, and ma'ani. The reason for the limitation is that what is realized either is realized by virtue of itself or by virtue of another; the first is existent, the second is the state, and it is either that the other by which it is realized is a described essence or a meaning subsisting in the described; the first is the essential state (hal nafsiyya), the second is the mental state (hal ma'nawiyya). End. Al-Yusi said: What was mentioned of the division into three types concerns positive attributive attributes. His saying: 'And conceptual things (umur i'tibariyya) they are what have subsistence' i.e., in itself, with disagreement on that. And his saying: 'But it has not risen to the degree of states' meaning that its subsistence is less than their subsistence. And they gave as examples of the conceptual: possibility, existence, and others, and of the state: being powerful and being willing, and others.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the fourfold classification of things according to the theory of ahwal: existents (things that can be seen), non-existents (what have no subsistence), states (intermediary between existents and non-existents, having a subsistence that is not fully existential), and conceptual things (having subsistence but lower than states). The glosses clarify each category: non-existents include pure non-existents (like Ibn Zayd before existence) and negative things (like God's everlastingness). States are defined as having a subsistence that is not interpretable by non-existence and not visible, thus excluding non-existents and existential attributes. Conceptual things are shown to have real subsistence (e.g., the subsistence of power in God) as evidenced by arguments and their division into pre-eternal and originated. The passage then subdivides states into nafsiyya (essential) and ma'nawiyya (mental), citing authorities like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn who divide attributes into nafsiyya, ma'nawiyya, and ma'ani. The reason for this division is that what is realized is either by itself (existent) or by another (state), and that other is either a described essence (essential state) or a meaning subsisting in the described (mental state). Examples of conceptual things are possibility and existence; examples of states are being powerful and being willing.

أمور اعتبارية
ثبوت
قار للذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think conceptual things are purely mental constructs.
Correction: The passage provides evidence that they have real subsistence, such as the argument from division into pre-eternal and originated.

Checkpoint
Why did scholars argue that conceptual things have real subsistence?
A) Because they can be seen.
B) Because they can be divided into pre-eternal and originated.
C) Because they are non-existent.
D) Because they are mental constructs.

Key Takeaway
States and conceptual things have real subsistence, with states being higher because they are inherent in the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God's attributes are real but unseen. How does the concept of states—intermediary between non-existence and attributes—challenge you to affirm His attributes with certainty even when reason cannot perceive them? Will you commit to trusting what reason and revelation establish, even if your heart craves sensory proof?
?
The proof distinguishes what is merely mental from what is real. Examine one belief you hold about God that might be just a mental construct, not truly subsisting in your conviction. What would it take for that belief to become a real attribute of your faith? Decide today to seek clarity.

Subdivision of States and Attributes
Divides states into two types and attributes into three, with examples and reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how states are subdivided into essential (nafsiyya) and mental (ma'nawiyya) states, and how attributes are divided into three types: nafsiyya, ma'nawiyya, and ma'ani.

Core Idea
States are of two types: essential and mental; attributes are three types: essential, mental, and meanings.

After understanding the nature of states and conceptual things, we now examine their subdivisions. This lesson details the two types of states (nafsiyya and ma'nawiyya) and the threefold division of attributes as held by proponents of ahwal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the division of states
The passage first divides states into nafsiyya (essential) and ma'nawiyya (mental). Then it discusses the threefold division of attributes: nafsiyya, ma'nawiyya, and ma'ani.
Note the reasoning: what is realized either by itself (existent) or by another (state), and that other is either a described essence (nafsiyya) or a meaning (ma'nawiyya).
2
Understand the threefold division of attributes
This is presented on the authority of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. The three types are essential attributes (like existence), mental attributes (like being powerful), and meanings (the ground of the mental attribute).
Also note al-Yusi's remark that this division concerns positive attributes (sifat thubutiyya).
3
Connect to conceptual things
The passage ends with examples of conceptual things (existence, possibility) and states (being powerful, being willing). This reinforces the hierarchy.
Reflect on how this relates to the earlier discussions.

?

Definition of States and Mental Considerations
Sanusi defines states and mental considerations; Al-Ajhurī proves their reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of states and mental considerations as given by Sanusi, and how the commentators provide evidence for the extra-mental reality of mental considerations.

Core Idea
States and mental considerations are real but not fully existent; they differ in what they qualify.

Sanusi introduces two key ontological categories in Ashʿarī metaphysics: states (aḥwāl) and mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya). These are not fully existent nor non-existent. In this lesson, we will explore their definitions and the evidence that they have reality beyond the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definitions
Read the matn and commentary carefully. Notice how Sanusi defines states as 'al-wāsiṭa bayna al-mawjūdāt wa-l-maʿdūmāt' (intermediary between existents and non-existents).
2
Understand the intermediary concept
The state is neither a full existent nor a non-existent. The commentator explains it is not interpreted as non-existence (to exclude non-existents) nor as perceptible (to exclude positive attributes).
This sets the stage for a unique ontological category.
3
Examine the proof for mental considerations
Al-Ajhurī provides evidence that mental considerations have real subsistence. First, the division into eternal and originated shows they are not merely mental (since mental things are only originated). Second, the theological inference about power in God proves real subsistence because inferences are only made from real matters.
These proofs are crucial for affirming the reality of these categories.
4
Distinguish states from mental considerations
The key difference is that a state (ḥāl) is 'qār li-l-dhāt' (subsistent for the essence) while a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī) is not; it has a lower degree of subsistence.
This distinction will be explored further in the next lesson.

Source Text

وأحوال: وهي الواسطة بين الموجودات والمعدومات، وأمور اعتبارية: وهي ما له ثبوت لكنه لم يرتق إلى درجة الأحوال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوَاسِطَةُ... إلخ) بأن لم تفسر بالعدم لتخرج المعدومات، ولم تمكن رؤيتها لتخرج الصفات الوجودية. (قوله: وَهِيَ مَا لَهُ ثُبوت) أي في الواقع ونفس الأمر كقيام القدرة بذاته تعالى، فإنه ثابت في الواقع بقطع النظر عن الذهن؛ لأنه عين ثبوت القدرة للذات، وهو حاصل قطعاً، بدليل استدلالهم عليه بقولهم: لو انتفت القدرة عنه تعالى إلى آخر البرهان الآتي، فإن الاستدلال عليه بذلك يدل على حصوله في الواقع؛ إذ لا يستدلّ إلا على الأمور الواقعية، ويدلُّ على حصول الأمر الاعتباري في الواقع ونفس الأمر بقطع النظر عن الذهني تقسيمهم له إلى قديم وحادث، ولو كان ذهنياً فقط؛ لبطل هذا التقسيم؛ لأن الذهني يكون إلا حادثاً، ووجه كون الحال أرقى منه على هذا أن الحال قار للذات. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي: (قوله: وَهِيَ الْوَاسِطَةُ ... إلخ) أي: بأن كان له ثبوت في نفسه أرقى من ثبوت الاعتبار إلا أنه لم ينته إلى درجة الوجود، والحالُ تنقسم قسمين: نفسية ومعنوية، وعبارة المصنف في 'شرح الكبرى' والقائلون بثبوت الحال كالقاضي وإمام الحرمين يَقْسِمُون الصفاتِ ثلاثةَ أقسام: نفسية ومعنوية ومعاني. ووجه الحصر: أن المتحقق؛ إما أن يتحقق باعتبار نفسه، أو باعتبار غيره الأول الموجود، والثاني الحال، وهو إما أن يكون الغيرُ الذي تحقق به ذاتاً موصوفة، أو معنى يَقُومُ بموصوف، الأول الحالُ النفسية، والثاني الحال المعنوية اهـ. قال اليوسي: ما ذُكِرَ من التقسيم إلى ثلاثة أقسام هو باعتبار الصفة الثبوتية. (قوله: وَأُمُورٌ اعْتِبَارِيَّةً وَهِيَ مَا لَهُ ثُبُوتٌ أي في نفسه على الخلاف في ذلك. (وقوله: لَكِنَّهُ لَمْ يَرْتَقِ إِلَى دَرَجَةِ الْأَحْوَالِ بأن كان ثبوته أقل من ثبوتها، ومثلوا الاعتبار بالإمكان والوجود وغير ذلك، وللحال بالكون قادراً والكون مريداً وغير ذلك. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: لها ، والأمر الاعتباري قار للصفات، فإن قيام القدرة بالذات الأقدس وصف للقدرة، وقيام البياض بزيدٍ وصف للبياض، وما كان قاراً للذات أقوى مما كان قاراً للصفة، ولا يرد ومن تبعه جعلوا الوجود أمراً اعتبارياً مع أنه قار للذات؛ لأن التفرقة المذكورة إنما هي على القول بإثبات الأحوال. والظاهر أن السعد ومن تبعه لا يقولون بثبوت الأحوال، وما ذكر من أن الأمر الاعتباري له ثبوت في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن أحد قولين، وهو الظاهرُ الذي ترضاه العقول، وثانيهما: أنه لا ثبوت له إلا في الذهن، فالثابت في الواقع ذات الله تعالى وقدرته، وأما قيامها بالذات؛ فأمر ذهني فقط لا تحقق له في الواقع. وكلامُ الشيخ الشرقاوي في حاشية 'الهدهدي' مختلف فتارةً قرّرَ أن الأمر الاعتباري ذهني فقط، ومثله بقيام قدرته بذاته تعالى، وتارة قرّر أن له حصولاً في نفسه بقطع النظر عن الذهن وهما قولان في الأمر الاعتباري الانتزاعي كقيام القدرة بالذات وقيام البياض بزيد، أما الاعتباري الاختراعي كبحر من زئبق؛ فهو ذهني اتفاقاً، وما ذكر من أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف أمرٌ اعتباري لا حال هو بالنسبة إلى الذات، وأما بالنسبة إلى الصفة نفسها؛ فهو حال نفسي لها؛ لأنه عبارة عن وجودها في الموصوف، ووجود الشيء سواء كان ذاتاً أو صفةً حال نفسي له كما يعلم ذلك من 'حاشية الهدهدي' و'حاشية الدسوقي على المصنف'. ففيهما التصريح بأن المراد بالذات في قولهم الحال الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات الشيء ذاتاً كان أو صفة وبأن قيام العرض بالذات حال نفسي للعرض، فتلخص أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف حال نفسي بالنسبة إلى الصفة لكونه قاراً لها، وأمر اعتباري بالنسبة إلى الذات لكونه ليس قاراً لها، بل لصفتها، وإنما أطلنا الكلام في هذا المقام لصعوبته.
And states (aḥwāl): they are the intermediary between existing things and non-existing things. And mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya): they are what have subsistence but did not rise to the degree of states. The gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhurī: (His saying: 'and they are the intermediary... etc.') means that it is not interpreted as non-existence to exclude non-existents, and it is not possible to perceive them to exclude positive attributes. (His saying: 'and they are what have subsistence') i.e., in reality and the matter itself, like the subsistence of power by His essence, Exalted is He. For it is established in reality, regardless of the mind, because it is the very subsistence of power for the essence, and it is definitely obtained, evidenced by their inference upon it by their saying: 'If power were negated from Him, Exalted is He, to the end of the proof to come.' For the inference upon it by that indicates its occurrence in reality, since one infers only from real matters. And it indicates the occurrence of the mental consideration in reality and the matter itself, regardless of the mind, their division of it into eternal and originated. If it were only mental, this division would be invalid because the mental is only originated. And the aspect of the state being higher than it on this basis is that the state is subsistent for the essence. The taqrīrāt of the scholar Muḥammad al-Anbābī: (His saying: 'and they are the intermediary... etc.') i.e., in that it has in itself a subsistence higher than the subsistence of the consideration, except that it did not reach the degree of existence. The state (ḥāl) is divided into two divisions: nafsī (essential) and maʿnawī (meaningful). And the statement of the author in 'Sharḥ al-Kubrā', and those who affirm the state such as al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn, divide attributes into three divisions: nafsī, maʿnawī, and maʿānī (meanings). The reason for the enumeration is that what is actualized either is actualized with respect to itself or with respect to another. The first is the existent, the second is the state. And the state is either that the other by which it is actualized is a qualified essence, or a meaning that subsists in a qualified thing. The first is the nafsī state, the second is the maʿnawī state. Al-Yūsī said: What was mentioned of the division into three divisions is with respect to the positive attribute. (His saying: 'and mental considerations, and they are what have subsistence' i.e., in itself, based on disagreement regarding that. (And His saying: 'but it did not rise to the degree of states') meaning that its subsistence is less than their subsistence. And they exemplified the consideration with possibility and existence and other than that, and the state with being able and being willing and other than that. The gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhurī: And the mental consideration subsists for attributes. For the subsistence of power by the most holy essence is a description of power, and the subsistence of whiteness by Zayd is a description of whiteness. And what subsists for the essence is stronger than what subsists for the attribute. And it is not refuted by [the fact that] those who followed him made existence a mental consideration even though it subsists for the essence, because the mentioned distinction is only based on the doctrine of affirming states. And the apparent is that al-Saʿd and those who followed him do not affirm states. And what was mentioned that the mental consideration has subsistence in itself regardless of the mind is one of two opinions, and it is the apparent that intellects are satisfied with. And the second is that it has no subsistence except in the mind. So what is established in reality is the essence of God Most High and His power, and as for their subsistence by the essence, it is only a mental matter, having no actuality in reality. And the speech of al-Shaykh al-Sharqāwī in the gloss of al-Hudhudī differs. Sometimes he affirmed that the mental consideration is only mental, and exemplified it by the subsistence of His power by His essence, Exalted is He. And sometimes he affirmed that it has obtained in itself regardless of the mind. These are two opinions regarding the iʿtibārī intizāʿī (abstractive mental consideration) like the subsistence of power by the essence and the subsistence of whiteness by Zayd. As for the iʿtibārī ikhtirāʿī (inventive mental consideration) like a sea of mercury, it is only mental by agreement. And what was mentioned that the subsistence of the attribute by the subject is a mental consideration, not a state, is with respect to the essence. And as for with respect to the attribute itself, it is a nafsī state for it, because it is an expression of its existence in the subject. And the existence of a thing, whether it is an essence or an attribute, is a nafsī state for it, as is known from 'Hāshiyat al-Hudhudī' and 'Hāshiyat al-Dasūqī ʿalā al-Muṣannif'. For in them it is explicitly stated that what is meant by essence (dhāt) in their saying 'the state necessarily pertaining to the essence as long as the essence is the thing, whether it is an essence or an attribute,' and that the subsistence of an accident by the essence is a nafsī state for the accident. Thus it is concluded that the subsistence of the attribute by the subject is a nafsī state with respect to the attribute, because it is subsistent for it, and a mental consideration with respect to the essence, because it is not subsistent for it, but for its attribute. And we have elaborated speech in this place due to its difficulty.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines and explains two key ontological categories in Ashʿarī metaphysics: states (aḥwāl) and mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya). The main text (matn) from Sanusi defines 'states' as intermediaries between existents and non-existents, and 'mental considerations' as having subsistence (thubūt) but not reaching the degree of states. The commentators then expand on these definitions.

Al-Ajhurī explains that 'intermediary' means the state is neither non-existent nor fully existent like attributes; it has a unique status. For the mental consideration, he gives the example of the subsistence of power in God's essence: this is real, not merely mental, because it is the exact subsistence of power for the essence. He offers a proof: the theologians' inference that if power were removed from God, other attributes could not follow shows that this subsistence is real (since inference is only from real things). Additionally, the fact that mental considerations are divided into eternal and originated shows they have extra-mental reality; if they were only mental, they would all be originated, invalidating the division. A state is higher because it subsists for the essence, while a mental consideration subsists for the attribute.

Al-Anbābī adds that a state has a higher subsistence than a consideration but not full existence. He then divides states into two types: nafsī (essential, e.g., existence itself) and maʿnawī (meaningful, e.g., being powerful). He cites al-Sanūsī's 'Sharḥ al-Kubrā' and others who divide attributes into three: nafsī, maʿnawī, and maʿānī (meanings). The reasoning is that something actualized is either through itself (an existent) or through another (a state). That other is either a qualified essence (giving a nafsī state) or a meaning subsisting in a qualified thing (giving a maʿnawī state). Al-Yūsī notes this division is only for positive attributes. Then examples are given: considerations include possibility and existence; states include being able and being willing.

Al-Ajhurī returns to explain that a mental consideration subsists for attributes (not for the essence directly). For instance, power's subsistence in God describes power, not God's essence. What subsists for the essence is stronger. He addresses an objection: if existence is a mental consideration, it subsists for the essence, contradicting the rule. He responds by noting that the distinction between states and considerations is only valid for those who affirm states (like al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn). Al-Saʿd (al-Taftāzānī) and his followers do not affirm states, so for them existence is indeed a consideration without issue.

Then he presents the two opinions on the reality of mental considerations: 1) they have subsistence regardless of the mind (the apparently more reasonable view), or 2) they exist only in the mind. On the second view, only God's essence and power are real; their relation (subsistence) is mental. Al-Sharqāwī is cited as vacillating between these two, but they apply only to 'abstractive' mental considerations (like subsistence of power in essence), not 'inventive' ones (like a sea of mercury), which are only mental by consensus.

Finally, a crucial nuance: the subsistence of an attribute in its subject is an iʿtibārī matter relative to the subject's essence, but a nafsī state relative to the attribute itself, because the attribute's existence in the subject is its own fact. This is supported from other commentaries. Thus the overall conclusion: consider two perspectives—for the attribute, it's a state; for the essence, it's a consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'amr iʿtibārī' is purely imaginary with no reality.
Correction: The passage proves it has extra-mental subsistence through the division into eternal/originated and the inference argument.

Checkpoint
Which proof does al-Ajhurī use to show mental considerations have real subsistence?
A) They are perceptible by the senses.
B) They are divided into eternal and originated.
C) They are synonymous with attributes.
D) They are only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
States and mental considerations are real categories in Ashʿarī metaphysics, with states being higher because they subsist for the essence, proven by arguments from inference and division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your intentions and beliefs are, in a sense, 'mental considerations'—they are real yet not fully material. Have you ever treated your own inner sincerity as less real than your outward actions? How does knowing that these inner realities have extra-mental subsistence change the way you value and nurture them? What will you do today to give more weight to your inner states?
?
The passage tells us that states are higher than mental considerations because they 'subsist for the essence.' Consider your own spiritual states—like the love of God or certainty of faith. Do these truly 'subsist' in your soul, or are they merely intellectual considerations you hold in your mind? What would it take for them to move from being mental acknowledgments to states that define your very essence?
?
Al-Ajhurī uses inference as proof that mental considerations are real. Your faith itself is built on inferences from creation to Creator. Do you treat that inference as proof of something as real as the power of existence, or do you dismiss it as mere mental exercise? Will you allow that inference to transform from a mental consideration to a state that subsists in your heart, and how will you act on it today?

Logic and Technical Terms

8 min
Divisions of States and the Reality of Mental Considerations
Al-Anbābī and al-Ajhurī divide states and analyze subsistence relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how states are divided into nafsī and maʿnawī, and how the subsistence of an attribute relates differently to the essence and the attribute itself, along with the two opinions on the reality of mental considerations.

Core Idea
A state subsists for the essence; a mental consideration subsists for the attribute.

After establishing the basic definitions, the commentators delve deeper into the classification of states and the nuanced relationship between attributes and their subjects. This lesson unpacks the division of states and the important perspective that the same relationship can be considered a state for the attribute and a mental consideration for the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the division of states
Al-Anbābī divides states into two: nafsī (essential) and maʿnawī (meaningful). Understand the reasoning: what is actualized either by itself (existent) or by another (state). The other is either a qualified essence (nafsī) or a meaning (maʿnawī).
2
Understand the threefold attribute division
Those who affirm states divide attributes into nafsī, maʿnawī, and maʿānī. Al-Yūsī notes this is only for positive attributes.
3
Analyze the nuance of subsistence
Al-Ajhurī explains that a mental consideration subsists for the attribute, not the essence. For example, power's subsistence in God describes power, not essence. He also addresses the objection about existence.
This is a key point: the same relationship can be viewed from two perspectives.
4
Consider the two opinions on mental considerations
Two opinions are presented: (1) mental considerations have subsistence regardless of the mind (apparently reasonable), (2) they exist only in the mind. This applies to 'abstractive' considerations, not 'inventive' ones (which are mental by consensus).
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The subsistence of an attribute in its subject is a nafsī state for the attribute (because it subsists for it) and a mental consideration for the essence (because it does not subsist for the essence but for the attribute).
Thus, perspective matters in classification.

And states (aḥwāl): they are the intermediary between existing things and non-existing things. And mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya): they are what have subsistence but did not rise to the degree of states. The gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhurī: (His saying: 'and they are the intermediary... etc.') means that it is not interpreted as non-existence to exclude non-existents, and it is not possible to perceive them to exclude positive attributes. (His saying: 'and they are what have subsistence') i.e., in reality and the matter itself, like the subsistence of power by His essence, Exalted is He. For it is established in reality, regardless of the mind, because it is the very subsistence of power for the essence, and it is definitely obtained, evidenced by their inference upon it by their saying: 'If power were negated from Him, Exalted is He, to the end of the proof to come.' For the inference upon it by that indicates its occurrence in reality, since one infers only from real matters. And it indicates the occurrence of the mental consideration in reality and the matter itself, regardless of the mind, their division of it into eternal and originated. If it were only mental, this division would be invalid because the mental is only originated. And the aspect of the state being higher than it on this basis is that the state is subsistent for the essence. The taqrīrāt of the scholar Muḥammad al-Anbābī: (His saying: 'and they are the intermediary... etc.') i.e., in that it has in itself a subsistence higher than the subsistence of the consideration, except that it did not reach the degree of existence. The state (ḥāl) is divided into two divisions: nafsī (essential) and maʿnawī (meaningful). And the statement of the author in 'Sharḥ al-Kubrā', and those who affirm the state such as al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn, divide attributes into three divisions: nafsī, maʿnawī, and maʿānī (meanings). The reason for the enumeration is that what is actualized either is actualized with respect to itself or with respect to another. The first is the existent, the second is the state. And the state is either that the other by which it is actualized is a qualified essence, or a meaning that subsists in a qualified thing. The first is the nafsī state, the second is the maʿnawī state. Al-Yūsī said: What was mentioned of the division into three divisions is with respect to the positive attribute. (His saying: 'and mental considerations, and they are what have subsistence' i.e., in itself, based on disagreement regarding that. (And His saying: 'but it did not rise to the degree of states') meaning that its subsistence is less than their subsistence. And they exemplified the consideration with possibility and existence and other than that, and the state with being able and being willing and other than that. The gloss of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhurī: And the mental consideration subsists for attributes. For the subsistence of power by the most holy essence is a description of power, and the subsistence of whiteness by Zayd is a description of whiteness. And what subsists for the essence is stronger than what subsists for the attribute. And it is not refuted by [the fact that] those who followed him made existence a mental consideration even though it subsists for the essence, because the mentioned distinction is only based on the doctrine of affirming states. And the apparent is that al-Saʿd and those who followed him do not affirm states. And what was mentioned that the mental consideration has subsistence in itself regardless of the mind is one of two opinions, and it is the apparent that intellects are satisfied with. And the second is that it has no subsistence except in the mind. So what is established in reality is the essence of God Most High and His power, and as for their subsistence by the essence, it is only a mental matter, having no actuality in reality. And the speech of al-Shaykh al-Sharqāwī in the gloss of al-Hudhudī differs. Sometimes he affirmed that the mental consideration is only mental, and exemplified it by the subsistence of His power by His essence, Exalted is He. And sometimes he affirmed that it has obtained in itself regardless of the mind. These are two opinions regarding the iʿtibārī intizāʿī (abstractive mental consideration) like the subsistence of power by the essence and the subsistence of whiteness by Zayd. As for the iʿtibārī ikhtirāʿī (inventive mental consideration) like a sea of mercury, it is only mental by agreement. And what was mentioned that the subsistence of the attribute by the subject is a mental consideration, not a state, is with respect to the essence. And as for with respect to the attribute itself, it is a nafsī state for it, because it is an expression of its existence in the subject. And the existence of a thing, whether it is an essence or an attribute, is a nafsī state for it, as is known from 'Hāshiyat al-Hudhudī' and 'Hāshiyat al-Dasūqī ʿalā al-Muṣannif'. For in them it is explicitly stated that what is meant by essence (dhāt) in their saying 'the state necessarily pertaining to the essence as long as the essence is the thing, whether it is an essence or an attribute,' and that the subsistence of an accident by the essence is a nafsī state for the accident. Thus it is concluded that the subsistence of the attribute by the subject is a nafsī state with respect to the attribute, because it is subsistent for it, and a mental consideration with respect to the essence, because it is not subsistent for it, but for its attribute. And we have elaborated speech in this place due to its difficulty.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines and explains two key ontological categories in Ashʿarī metaphysics: states (aḥwāl) and mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya). The main text (matn) from Sanusi defines 'states' as intermediaries between existents and non-existents, and 'mental considerations' as having subsistence (thubūt) but not reaching the degree of states. The commentators then expand on these definitions.

Al-Ajhurī explains that 'intermediary' means the state is neither non-existent nor fully existent like attributes; it has a unique status. For the mental consideration, he gives the example of the subsistence of power in God's essence: this is real, not merely mental, because it is the exact subsistence of power for the essence. He offers a proof: the theologians' inference that if power were removed from God, other attributes could not follow shows that this subsistence is real (since inference is only from real things). Additionally, the fact that mental considerations are divided into eternal and originated shows they have extra-mental reality; if they were only mental, they would all be originated, invalidating the division. A state is higher because it subsists for the essence, while a mental consideration subsists for the attribute.

Al-Anbābī adds that a state has a higher subsistence than a consideration but not full existence. He then divides states into two types: nafsī (essential, e.g., existence itself) and maʿnawī (meaningful, e.g., being powerful). He cites al-Sanūsī's 'Sharḥ al-Kubrā' and others who divide attributes into three: nafsī, maʿnawī, and maʿānī (meanings). The reasoning is that something actualized is either through itself (an existent) or through another (a state). That other is either a qualified essence (giving a nafsī state) or a meaning subsisting in a qualified thing (giving a maʿnawī state). Al-Yūsī notes this division is only for positive attributes. Then examples are given: considerations include possibility and existence; states include being able and being willing.

Al-Ajhurī returns to explain that a mental consideration subsists for attributes (not for the essence directly). For instance, power's subsistence in God describes power, not God's essence. What subsists for the essence is stronger. He addresses an objection: if existence is a mental consideration, it subsists for the essence, contradicting the rule. He responds by noting that the distinction between states and considerations is only valid for those who affirm states (like al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn). Al-Saʿd (al-Taftāzānī) and his followers do not affirm states, so for them existence is indeed a consideration without issue.

Then he presents the two opinions on the reality of mental considerations: 1) they have subsistence regardless of the mind (the apparently more reasonable view), or 2) they exist only in the mind. On the second view, only God's essence and power are real; their relation (subsistence) is mental. Al-Sharqāwī is cited as vacillating between these two, but they apply only to 'abstractive' mental considerations (like subsistence of power in essence), not 'inventive' ones (like a sea of mercury), which are only mental by consensus.

Finally, a crucial nuance: the subsistence of an attribute in its subject is an iʿtibārī matter relative to the subject's essence, but a nafsī state relative to the attribute itself, because the attribute's existence in the subject is its own fact. This is supported from other commentaries. Thus the overall conclusion: consider two perspectives—for the attribute, it's a state; for the essence, it's a consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subsistence of an attribute in its subject is simply a state for both essence and attribute.
Correction: It is a state only for the attribute; for the essence, it is a mental consideration because it does not subsist for the essence but for the attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of 'subsistence of an attribute in its subject' with respect to the attribute?
A) It is a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī).
B) It is a nafsī state (ḥāl nafsiyya).
C) It is a maʿnawī state.
D) It is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
States are divided into nafsī and maʿnawī, and the subsistence of an attribute is a state for the attribute but a mental consideration for the essence, illustrating the importance of perspective in ontology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the subsistence of God's power in His essence is a mental consideration relative to the essence but a nafsī state for the attribute itself. In your heart, when you think of God's power, do you unwittingly imagine it as something separate from God? How can this distinction help you affirm both God's unity and the reality of His attributes? Write one concrete way you will correct your imagination.
?
The passage presents two opinions on whether mental considerations have extra-mental reality. Which opinion do you find yourself naturally drawn to, and why? Does your choice reveal a deeper inclination to either over-emphasize or under-emphasize the reality of God's attributes? Pray and honestly assess where your heart leans, then commit to the view that most safeguards tawhid.

Definition of Qadim and Azali and Their Relationship
Al-Bajuri defines qadim and azali and explains their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly views on the relationship between the terms qadim and azali, and the inclusion of non-existent attributes in eternity.

Core Idea
Qadim and azali are either synonymous or related as general to specific, with azali being broader.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies two key kalam terms: qadim and azali. While the first view equates them, a second view distinguishes them by scope. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how classical scholars discuss divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to get an overview of the two views presented.
Pay attention to the flow: first the verified view, then the alternative view, then the clarification of 'non-existent'.
2
Identify the first view (verified)
The first view states that qadim and azali are synonymous, meaning 'that which has no beginning'. Note that this applies to both existent and non-existent entities.
This is the al-tahqeeq (verified) position.
3
Identify the second view (alternative)
The second view distinguishes qadim (specific to existent) from azali (more general). Understand the relation of 'absolute generality and specificity' (al-umoom wa al-khusoos bi al-itlaq) between them.
Azali includes both existent and non-existent, while qadim is only for existent.
4
Understand the meaning of 'non-existent'
The commentator explains that 'non-existent' here refers to negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) and relational matters (umoor i'tibariyya) that are considered eternal, like the subsistence of power in the divine essence.
This clarifies how attributes like 'everlastingness' and 'incomparability' are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق: أن القديم والأزلي بمعنى واحد وهو ما لا أَوَّلَ له، وجودياً كان أو عَدَميّاً. وقيل: القديمُ خاص بالوجودي، والأزليُّ أَعَمُ منه، وعليه يكون بينهما العموم والخصوص بإطلاق؛ لأنهما يجتمعان في الوجودي كذاته تعالى وقدرته وينفرِدُ الأزليُّ في العَدَمِيّ كالبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث. (قوله: أَوْ عَدَمِيّاً) المراد بالعدمي: ما قابل الوجودي، فيشمل العدمي حقيقة، وهي الصفات السلبية والأمور الاعتبارية القديمة كقيام القدرة بالذات الأقدس والأحوال القديمة على القول بثبوت الأحوال.
And the verified view is that al-qadim (the eternal) and al-azali (the pre-eternal) are synonymous, meaning that which has no beginning, whether it be existent or non-existent. And it is said: al-qadim is specific to the existent, and al-azali is more general than it, and accordingly there is absolute generality and specificity between them because they converge in the existent like His Essence and His Power, and al-azali applies uniquely to the non-existent like [the attributes of] everlastingness (al-baqa) and incomparability to originated things (al-mukhalafah lil hawadith). (His saying: 'or non-existent') What is meant by 'non-existent' is that which is opposite of existent, so it includes the truly non-existent, which are the negative attributes and the relational matters that are eternal, such as the subsistence of power by the Most Sacred Essence and the eternal states according to the view affirming states.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines and clarifies the relationship between two key terms in kalam: qadim and azali. The commentator begins by stating the 'verified view' (al-tahqeeq) that the two terms are synonymous, both meaning 'that which has no beginning' (ma la awwala lahu). This applies whether the entity is existent (wujoodi) like God's essence, or non-existent (adami) like negative attributes. Then he presents the alternative view: that qadim is specific to existent things, while azali is more general, covering both existent and non-existent. He explains that according to this view, there is a relation of absolute generality and specificity (al-umoom wa al-khusoos bi al-itlaq) between the two: they overlap in existent things (e.g., God's essence and power are both qadim and azali), but azali uniquely applies to non-existent attributes such as baqa (everlastingness) and mukhalafah (incomparability). The commentator then glosses the phrase 'or non-existent' from the source text, explaining that 'non-existent' here means what is opposite of existent, which includes truly non-existent things like negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) and relational matters (umoor i'tibariyya) that are considered eternal, such as the subsistence of power by the divine essence, and also the eternal states (ahwal) according to those who affirm their reality.

عموم وخصوص
صفات سلبية
أمور اعتبارية
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadim and azali are always interchangeable.
Correction: According to one valid view, qadim applies only to existent entities while azali is more general, covering both existent and non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the verified view, what is the relationship between qadim and azali?
A) They are synonymous
B) Qadim is more general
C) Azali is specific to existent
D) They are opposite

Key Takeaway
In kalam, the terms qadim and azali are often used interchangeably, but a nuanced view distinguishes them by scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage mentions that some of Allah's attributes are 'non-existent' (adami) like His incomparability. Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah with human limitations or forms? What practical steps can you take today to purify your understanding of Allah and worship Him as He truly is, beyond your imagination?
?
If the term azali includes attributes like everlastingness (baqa), do you truly live as if you will meet Allah and dwell with Him eternally? What is one attachment in your life that proves you are more focused on this world than on the everlasting? Will you cut it today?

The Pronoun in Two Attributes and the Addition of 'Ta'ala'
Al-Bajuri explains the use of the pronoun in two particular attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author used a pronoun in the attributes 'mukhalif' and 'qa'im bi nafsihi' and the significance of adding 'ta'ala'.

Core Idea
The pronoun signals that these attributes belong to Allah alone in their ultimate sense.

The author Sanusi uses a pronoun in two specific divine attributes, but not in the ones before. This might seem like a small stylistic choice, but it carries deep theological meaning. In this lesson, we will explore the reasons behind this choice and how it leads to affirming Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage gives two reasons for the pronoun.
2
Understand the two reasons
Identify the first reason (stylistic variation) and the second (specification of meaning). The second reason is especially important because these attributes can be applied to creatures, so the pronoun differentiates the divine sense.
3
Reflect on the connection to 'ta'ala'
The passage links the pronoun to the addition of 'ta'ala' (exalted) as a reminder of transcendence. Consider why mentioning God should always be accompanied by a recognition of His incomparability.
This shows the etiquette of speaking about Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما أتى المصنف بالضمير في هذه الصفة والتي بعدها دون ما قبلها للتفنن، أو لأن كُلّاً منهما يَصِحُ اتصاف غیره تعالی به فیقال زيد مخالف لغيره في كذا، وقائم بنفسه بمعنى أنه لا يحتاج إلى غيره في أمورِ معايشه، وفي الإتيان بالضمير تنصيص على أنَّ المراد المخالفة والقيام بالنفس المناسِبَان له تعالى.
ولما أتى بالضمير العائد للمولى جل وعز؛ ناسَبَ أن يأتي بقوله (تعالى) الدال على التنزيه؛ لأنه يُطْلَبُ من العبد أنه متى ذُكِرَ المولى أتى بما يدل على تنزيهه عما لا يليق به.
فإن قيل: الحوادثُ لا تَشْمَلُ المعدومات بل تختص بالموجودات، والمولى كما هو مخالف للموجودات مخالف للمعدومات، فهلا عبر المصنف بالممكنات الشاملة لكلّ من الموجودات والمعدومات؟
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
:(قوله: مُخَالِفٌ لِلْمَعْدُومَاتِ إِن أريد بمخالفته لها عدم الموافقة من غير تفصيل إلى قولنا: (في الجرمية (والعرضية إلى آخر ما تقدم؛ كانت مخالفته لها ظاهرة، وإن أريد بمخالفته لها عدم موافقته لها على التفصيل إلى الجرمية وما بعدها؛ كان المراد بمخالفته للمعدومات أنها لو وجدت كان مخالفاً لها في الجرمية وما بعدها.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: أَوْ لِأَنَّ كُلّاً مِنْهُمَا يَصِحُ... إلخ) قد يقال: إن الصفات الثلاث المذكورة أوّلاً كذلك، فالأولى أن يقال: إن الإتيان بالضمير في هذه الصفة والتي بعدها للتوصل إلى التنزيه بقوله تعالى ردّاً على من قال: إنه جسم، أو في جهةٍ، أو صفة قائمة بذات عيسى، بخلاف بقية الصفات، فإنه لم يُصَرّخ أحدٌ من العقلاء بنقائضها ما عدا الوحدانية.
ولا يقال: كان يأتي بالضمير في الوحدانية ردّاً على الثَّانَوِيَّة الذين صرحوا بالتعدد؛ لأنا نقول: إن رَدَّ قول الثانوية وارِد في الكتاب والسنة بكثرة، فلذلك لم يَكْتَرِثْ بكلامهم
حتى يَرُدَّ عليهم تأمل.
أجيب بأن الموجودات هي التي تُتَوَهَّمُ فيها المماثلة لكونها مشارِكَةً للمولى في الوجود؛ وإن كان لا يجوز أن يُقَال: المولى مُماثل للحوادث في الوجود، بخلاف المعدومات، فلا تُتَوَهَّمُ فيها المماثَلَةُ لعدم كونها مشاركة له تعالى في ذلك.
And the author only used the pronoun in this attribute and the one after it, unlike those before it, for stylistic variation, or because each of them can be validly attributed to other than Him, so it is said: Zayd is different (mukhalif) from others in such-and-such, and self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi) meaning he does not need others in his means of livelihood. And in using the pronoun there is a specification that what is intended is the opposition and self-subsistence that are appropriate to Him, exalted. And when he used the pronoun referring to the Master, glorified and majestic, it was appropriate for him to say 'exalted (ta'ala)' indicating transcendence, because it is required of the servant that whenever the Master is mentioned, he says something indicating His transcendence above what is not befitting Him. If it is said: 'al-hawadith' (contingent events) do not include non-existent things but are specific to existent things, and the Master is as He is different from existent things, He is different from non-existent things, so why did the author not use 'al-mumkinat' (possible things) which include both existent and non-existent? (Margin of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: His statement: 'different from non-existent things' — if what is meant by His difference from them is non-correspondence without specification to our statement: 'in corporeality and accidentality' up to what preceded, then His difference from them is apparent, and if what is meant by His difference from them is His non-correspondence to them in detail to corporeality and beyond, then what is meant by His difference from non-existent things is that if they existed, He would be different from them in corporeality and beyond. (Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His statement: 'or because each of them can be valid... etc.' It may be said: The three attributes mentioned first are likewise, so it is better to say: The use of the pronoun in this attribute and the one after it is to arrive at transcendence by His statement 'ta'ala' as a refutation of whoever said He is a body, or in a direction, or a attribute subsisting in the essence of Jesus, unlike the rest of the attributes, for no rational person has explicitly stated their opposites except for unicity. And it is not said: He could have used the pronoun in unicity as a refutation of the dualists who explicitly stated multiplicity, because we say: The refutation of the dualists' statement is abundant in the Book and the Sunnah, therefore he did not concern himself with their words to refute them — reflect.) It was answered that existent things are those in which likeness is conceived because they participate with the Master in existence, although it is not permissible to say the Master is like the contingent events in existence, unlike non-existent things, so likeness is not conceived in them because they do not participate with Him, exalted, in that.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary and an objection-answer unit. The commentator begins by explaining why the author (Sanusi) used a pronoun (i.e., 'his' as in 'mukhalifuhu') specifically for the attributes 'different' (mukhalif) and 'self-subsisting' (qa'im bi nafsihi) and not for earlier attributes. Two reasons are given: first, for stylistic variation; second, because these two attributes can be applied to created beings in a limited sense (e.g., Zayd is different from others or self-reliant), so the pronoun signals that here they are meant in a way befitting Allah alone. Then he notes that since the pronoun refers to Allah, it is appropriate to add 'ta'ala' (exalted) to affirm His transcendence. An objection follows: 'al-hawadith' (contingent events) only applies to existent things, but Allah is also different from non-existent things, so why did the author not use 'al-mumkinat' (possible things) which includes both existent and non-existent? The commentator provides two responses: the first from al-Ujhuri's gloss, explaining that if 'different from non-existent things' means simply non-identity, it is obvious; if it means detailed difference (e.g., in corporeality), it means that if non-existents existed, Allah would still differ from them in those aspects. The second response comes from al-Anbabi's annotation, which offers an alternative explanation: the pronoun is used here to enable the refutation of false beliefs (that Allah is a body, in a direction, or an attribute in Jesus) through the word 'ta'ala', while unicity did not require such a refutation because it is already established in revelation. Finally, the direct answer to the objection is given: likeness is only conceivable for existent things because they share existence with Allah (though not in a mithliyya sense), whereas non-existent things do not share existence, so no likeness is imagined.

مخالف
قائم بنفسه
تعالى

C) To refute the claim that Allah has a body
D) Because the other attributes do not need pronouns

Key Takeaway
The pronoun in these two attributes signals that they are meant in a sense unique to Allah, and the addition of 'ta'ala' reinforces transcendence.

What does that neglect reveal about the reverence—or lack of it—in your heart? Starting now, will you make it a habit to consciously affirm Allah's transcendence when you speak of Him?

These two attributes can be applied to creatures in a limited sense. In what areas of your life are you tempted to rely on others as if they were self-sufficient or to think of them as truly 'different' in a way that competes with Allah? How does the lesson's clarification of the divine meaning free you from misplaced trust? What practical step will you take today to redirect that trust to Allah alone?

Objection: Does 'Hawadith' Include Non-Existent Things?
Al-Bajuri presents an objection and multiple responses regarding the scope of 'al-hawadith'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that 'al-hawadith' excludes non-existent things and how Al-Bajuri and others answer it.

Core Idea
'Hawadith' only covers existent things, but Allah's difference applies to non-existents as well, so why didn't the author use a broader term?

In the previous lesson, we saw that the author used the term 'mukhalif lil-hawadith' (different from contingent events). But alert readers noticed a problem: 'hawadith' only includes existent things, while Allah is also different from non-existent things. So why didn't he say 'mukhalif lil-mumkinat' (different from possible things) which covers both? This lesson examines the objection and the insightful responses from the commentators.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Pay attention to the claim that 'hawadith' does not include non-existents and the suggestion to use 'mumkinat' instead.
This is a common point of confusion in kalam terminology.
2
Study al-Ujhuri's response
Al-Ujhuri gives two interpretations: simple non-identity, or conditional detailed difference. Understand both.
3
Study al-Anbabi's response
Al-Anbabi offers a different angle: the pronoun was used to introduce 'ta'ala' to refute specific false beliefs, not to cover non-existents.
His response references refutations of anthropomorphism.
4
Understand the final answer
The final answer explains that likeness is only conceivable for existent things that share existence with Allah, so non-existents are irrelevant.
This resolves the objection theologically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما أتى المصنف بالضمير في هذه الصفة والتي بعدها دون ما قبلها للتفنن، أو لأن كُلّاً منهما يَصِحُ اتصاف غیره تعالی به فیقال زيد مخالف لغيره في كذا، وقائم بنفسه بمعنى أنه لا يحتاج إلى غيره في أمورِ معايشه، وفي الإتيان بالضمير تنصيص على أنَّ المراد المخالفة والقيام بالنفس المناسِبَان له تعالى.
ولما أتى بالضمير العائد للمولى جل وعز؛ ناسَبَ أن يأتي بقوله (تعالى) الدال على التنزيه؛ لأنه يُطْلَبُ من العبد أنه متى ذُكِرَ المولى أتى بما يدل على تنزيهه عما لا يليق به.
فإن قيل: الحوادثُ لا تَشْمَلُ المعدومات بل تختص بالموجودات، والمولى كما هو مخالف للموجودات مخالف للمعدومات، فهلا عبر المصنف بالممكنات الشاملة لكلّ من الموجودات والمعدومات؟
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
:(قوله: مُخَالِفٌ لِلْمَعْدُومَاتِ إِن أريد بمخالفته لها عدم الموافقة من غير تفصيل إلى قولنا: (في الجرمية (والعرضية إلى آخر ما تقدم؛ كانت مخالفته لها ظاهرة، وإن أريد بمخالفته لها عدم موافقته لها على التفصيل إلى الجرمية وما بعدها؛ كان المراد بمخالفته للمعدومات أنها لو وجدت كان مخالفاً لها في الجرمية وما بعدها.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: أَوْ لِأَنَّ كُلّاً مِنْهُمَا يَصِحُ... إلخ) قد يقال: إن الصفات الثلاث المذكورة أوّلاً كذلك، فالأولى أن يقال: إن الإتيان بالضمير في هذه الصفة والتي بعدها للتوصل إلى التنزيه بقوله تعالى ردّاً على من قال: إنه جسم، أو في جهةٍ، أو صفة قائمة بذات عيسى، بخلاف بقية الصفات، فإنه لم يُصَرّخ أحدٌ من العقلاء بنقائضها ما عدا الوحدانية.
ولا يقال: كان يأتي بالضمير في الوحدانية ردّاً على الثَّانَوِيَّة الذين صرحوا بالتعدد؛ لأنا نقول: إن رَدَّ قول الثانوية وارِد في الكتاب والسنة بكثرة، فلذلك لم يَكْتَرِثْ بكلامهم
حتى يَرُدَّ عليهم تأمل.
أجيب بأن الموجودات هي التي تُتَوَهَّمُ فيها المماثلة لكونها مشارِكَةً للمولى في الوجود؛ وإن كان لا يجوز أن يُقَال: المولى مُماثل للحوادث في الوجود، بخلاف المعدومات، فلا تُتَوَهَّمُ فيها المماثَلَةُ لعدم كونها مشاركة له تعالى في ذلك.
And the author only used the pronoun in this attribute and the one after it, unlike those before it, for stylistic variation, or because each of them can be validly attributed to other than Him, so it is said: Zayd is different (mukhalif) from others in such-and-such, and self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi) meaning he does not need others in his means of livelihood. And in using the pronoun there is a specification that what is intended is the opposition and self-subsistence that are appropriate to Him, exalted. And when he used the pronoun referring to the Master, glorified and majestic, it was appropriate for him to say 'exalted (ta'ala)' indicating transcendence, because it is required of the servant that whenever the Master is mentioned, he says something indicating His transcendence above what is not befitting Him. If it is said: 'al-hawadith' (contingent events) do not include non-existent things but are specific to existent things, and the Master is as He is different from existent things, He is different from non-existent things, so why did the author not use 'al-mumkinat' (possible things) which include both existent and non-existent? (Margin of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: His statement: 'different from non-existent things' — if what is meant by His difference from them is non-correspondence without specification to our statement: 'in corporeality and accidentality' up to what preceded, then His difference from them is apparent, and if what is meant by His difference from them is His non-correspondence to them in detail to corporeality and beyond, then what is meant by His difference from non-existent things is that if they existed, He would be different from them in corporeality and beyond. (Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His statement: 'or because each of them can be valid... etc.' It may be said: The three attributes mentioned first are likewise, so it is better to say: The use of the pronoun in this attribute and the one after it is to arrive at transcendence by His statement 'ta'ala' as a refutation of whoever said He is a body, or in a direction, or a attribute subsisting in the essence of Jesus, unlike the rest of the attributes, for no rational person has explicitly stated their opposites except for unicity. And it is not said: He could have used the pronoun in unicity as a refutation of the dualists who explicitly stated multiplicity, because we say: The refutation of the dualists' statement is abundant in the Book and the Sunnah, therefore he did not concern himself with their words to refute them — reflect.) It was answered that existent things are those in which likeness is conceived because they participate with the Master in existence, although it is not permissible to say the Master is like the contingent events in existence, unlike non-existent things, so likeness is not conceived in them because they do not participate with Him, exalted, in that.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary and an objection-answer unit. The commentator begins by explaining why the author (Sanusi) used a pronoun (i.e., 'his' as in 'mukhalifuhu') specifically for the attributes 'different' (mukhalif) and 'self-subsisting' (qa'im bi nafsihi) and not for earlier attributes. Two reasons are given: first, for stylistic variation; second, because these two attributes can be applied to created beings in a limited sense (e.g., Zayd is different from others or self-reliant), so the pronoun signals that here they are meant in a way befitting Allah alone. Then he notes that since the pronoun refers to Allah, it is appropriate to add 'ta'ala' (exalted) to affirm His transcendence. An objection follows: 'al-hawadith' (contingent events) only applies to existent things, but Allah is also different from non-existent things, so why did the author not use 'al-mumkinat' (possible things) which includes both existent and non-existent? The commentator provides two responses: the first from al-Ujhuri's gloss, explaining that if 'different from non-existent things' means simply non-identity, it is obvious; if it means detailed difference (e.g., in corporeality), it means that if non-existents existed, Allah would still differ from them in those aspects. The second response comes from al-Anbabi's annotation, which offers an alternative explanation: the pronoun is used here to enable the refutation of false beliefs (that Allah is a body, in a direction, or an attribute in Jesus) through the word 'ta'ala', while unicity did not require such a refutation because it is already established in revelation. Finally, the direct answer to the objection is given: likeness is only conceivable for existent things because they share existence with Allah (though not in a mithliyya sense), whereas non-existent things do not share existence, so no likeness is imagined.

Key Terms
الحوادث
المعدومات
الممكنات
الجرمية
العرضية
المماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'al-hawadith' logically includes non-existent things or that the author made an oversight.
Correction: In kalam, 'hawadith' exclusively refers to existent contingent events. The objection is valid, but the responses show the author's choice is deliberate and theologically sound.

Checkpoint
What was the main objection raised regarding the term 'al-hawadith'?
A) It is too broad and includes non-existents
B) It is too narrow and excludes non-existents, while Allah is different from both
C) It implies that Allah is similar to events
D) It contradicts the attributes of perfection

Key Takeaway
The objection that 'hawadith' excludes non-existents is answered by showing that likeness is only conceivable for existents, so the term is appropriate for the context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine your relationship with Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as like something you know? How does the lesson's insistence that likeness is only conceivable for existents challenge you to purify your conception of Him?
?
The objection in this lesson arose from a desire for precision. In your own understanding of your faith, where have you been satisfied with vague or imprecise descriptions of Allah? Will you commit to studying with more care?
?
The final answer shows that non-existents are not even candidates for likeness—nothing escapes His uniqueness. Does your heart truly accept that Allah is beyond any comparison, including those you might make in moments of distress or gratitude? What will you do to reinforce this belief?

The Multiple Meanings of 'Nafs' in Arabic
Al-Sanusi enumerates four lexical meanings of the word nafs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic word 'nafs' has multiple lexical meanings beyond 'self', and you will be able to identify each meaning based on context.

Core Idea
Nafs means more than 'self'—it can mean blood, pride, or punishment.

Before delving into theological discussions, Al-Sanusi clarifies the Arabic word 'nafs'. This simple lexical note prevents confusion: when you see 'nafs' in a text, its meaning depends on context. In this passage, he lists four distinct meanings, one of which is the foundation for his theological usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's enumeration come through.
Notice that he uses 'منها' to list each meaning.
2
Identify the four meanings
List the four meanings Sanusi gives: essence, blood, pride, and punishment. Note the context for each.
The first is highlighted as the intended meaning for theology.
3
Understand each meaning in context
For each meaning, consider the example provided: a legal ruling for blood, an expression for pride, a Quranic verse for punishment.
This will help you recognize 'nafs' in different texts.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi start with this lexical clarification? Because theological arguments depend on precise terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النفسَ تُطْلَقُ على معان كثيرة، منها: الذات، وهو المراد هنا، ومنها: الدَّمُ، وهو المراد في قولهم: ما لا نَفْسَ له سائلةٌ لا يُنَجِّسُ الماءَ، ومنها: الأَنْفَةُ، وهي المرادَةُ في قولهم: فلان لا نَفْسَ له، ومنها: العقوبة، قيل: وهي المرادة في قوله تعالى: (وَيُحَذِّرُكُمُ اللَّهُ نَفْسَهُ) أي: عقوبَتَهُ إلى غير ذلك.
And know that 'al-nafs' is used for many meanings, among them: the essence (al-dhāt), and that is what is intended here; and among them: blood (al-dam), and that is intended in their saying: 'what has no flowing blood does not make water impure'; and among them: pride (al-anafa), and that is intended in their saying: 'so-and-so has no nafs'; and among them: punishment (al-'uqūba), and it is said that it is intended in His, exalted's, saying: 'And God warns you of His nafs' (Quran 3:30), i.e., His punishment, and so on.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a lexical enumeration. Al-Sanusi states that the word نَفْس has multiple meanings in the Arabic language. He lists four: firstly, الذات (essence/self), which is the meaning intended in theological contexts. Secondly, الدم (blood), as in the legal ruling that an animal with no flowing blood does not render water impure. Thirdly, الأَنَفَة (pride/dignity), as in the expression 'he has no nafs' meaning he lacks self-respect. Fourthly, العقوبة (punishment), an interpretation of the Quranic verse 'God warns you of His nafs' meaning His punishment. He indicates that there are still other meanings beyond these.

Key Terms

الذات (al-dhāt)
الدم (al-dam)
الأَنَفَة (al-anafa)
العقوبة (al-'uqūba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think نَفْس only means 'self' or 'soul'.
Correction: In Arabic, نَفْس has multiple meanings including blood, pride, and punishment, depending on context.

Checkpoint

B) Blood

Key Takeaway

Four Categories of Existents: Place and Specifier
Commentator classifies existents into four categories regarding need for place and specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians classify existents into four categories based on their need for a place (mahall) and a specifier (mukhasis), and how this classification highlights the uniqueness of Allah and His attributes.

Core Idea
Existents are either self-subsistent, contingent, or attributes — only Allah is self-sufficient.

In Islamic theology, understanding the nature of existence is foundational. This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin provides a crisp classification of all existents into four kinds based on two criteria: whether they require a locus (mahall) and whether they require a specifier (mukhasis). By working through this classification, you will see how theologians distinguish the Creator from creation and how divine attributes fit into the picture.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two criteria
Learn what 'mahall' (place/locus) and 'mukhasis' (specifier) mean. These are the two factors used to categorize existents.
Mahall refers to the substrate in which something inheres, like body for accidents. Mukhasis is the agent that specifies a thing's existence.
2
Read the passage and translate
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation. Notice the four categories enumerated by the author.
The passage uses 'qism' for each category.
3
Examine each category
Go through each of the four categories: 1) no need for either (God's essence), 2) need both (accidents), 3) need specifier but not place (essences of contingents), 4) need place but not specifier (God's attributes).
Category 4 might be surprising: God's attributes subsist in His essence but do not need a specifier.
4
Understand the theological point
Reflect on why this classification matters. It establishes God's self-sufficiency and the eternal nature of His attributes, distinguishing them from created attributes.
This guards against the misconception that God's attributes are created or separate entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الموجودات بالنسبة إلى المحل والمخصص أربعة أقسام كما ذكره المصنف في المقدّمات: قِسْمٌ لا يَفْتَقِرُ إليهما، وهو ذاتُ الله تعالى، وقسم يفتقر إليهما، وهو أعراض الحوادث، وقسم لا يفتقر إلى المَحَلّ ويفتقر إلى المخصص، وهو ذاتُ الحوادث، وقسم يقومُ بالمحلّ ولا يفتقر إلى المخصص وهو صفاتُ الله تعالى
And know that existents with respect to place (al-mahall) and specifier (al-mukhasis) are four categories as the author mentioned in the preliminaries: a category that does not need either of them, and that is the essence (dhat) of God Most High; a category that needs both, and that is the accidents (a'rad) of contingent things; a category that does not need a place but needs a specifier, and that is the essence of contingent things; and a category that subsists in a place and does not need a specifier, and that is the attributes (sifat) of God Most High.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a fourfold classification of existents based on their relationship to two concepts: 'mahall' (place/locus) and 'mukhasis' (specifier). The author states that existents fall into four categories. First, that which does not depend on either: this is the essence of God, who is self-subsistent and uncaused. Second, that which depends on both: the accidents of contingent beings, which inhere in a substance and require a cause for their specification. Third, that which depends on a specifier but not on a place: the essences of contingent beings (substances), which are not in a subject but need a cause to bring them into existence. Fourth, that which subsists in a place (i.e., inheres in an essence) but does not need a specifier: the attributes of God, which inhere in His essence but are eternal and not caused by an external specifier. This classification underscores the unique ontological status of God and His attributes.

Key Terms
محل
مخصص
ذات
أعراض
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's attributes are separate entities needing a specifier, but they are eternal and not caused by an external agent.
Correction: The classification shows that God's attributes subsist in His essence (mahall) but do not need a specifier, confirming their eternal and uncaused nature.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which category includes the attributes of God?
A) That which depends on both place and specifier
B) That which depends on neither
C) That which depends on place but not specifier
D) That which depends on specifier but not place

Key Takeaway
The fourfold classification of existents shows that only Allah's essence is absolutely independent, while His attributes inhere in Him eternally without external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only Allah's essence is absolutely independent of any place or specifier, how does your daily sense of self-sufficiency deceive you? In what specific area of your life do you need to consciously acknowledge your total dependency on the One who needs nothing?
?
The attributes of Allah reside in His essence without being caused. Contrast this with your own changing traits—your knowledge, power, or mood. In which of your qualities do you most need to stop relying on your own 'specifiers' (effort, people, circumstances) and instead turn to the Self-Sufficient? What would it look like to attribute your entire existence to Him alone?
?
Every contingent being—including you—needs both a place (your body, your environment) and a specifier (Allah's will). When you feel secure in your current 'place' (health, wealth, relationships), do you forget the Specifier who sustains you? What concrete step can you take today to live in constant awareness that your very existence is held by the One who needs nothing?
