Existence as Mental Consideration
Al-Anbabi glosses 'الأمر الاعتباري' and its application to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that existence is a mental consideration (أمر اعتباري غير نفسي) and how this resolves the apparent paradox of saying 'existence has no existence'.

Core Idea
Existence is a mental consideration, not a self-subsistent entity.

Al-Anbabi glosses 'الأمر الاعتباري' and explains that existence falls under this category. He introduces a crucial distinction between truth (صدق) and attribution (اتصاف) to avoid contradiction, concluding that existence is a mental consideration other than the self-subsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on 'الأمر الاعتباري'
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the claim that existence is described by non-existence.
The phrase 'الوجود لا وجود له' is a starting point.
2
Distinguish between صدق and اتصاف
Al-Anbabi says the intended meaning is truth, not attribution. So 'existence has no existence' is not literally true as an attribution.
3
Understand 'غير النفسي'
The mental consideration here is not self-subsistent (غير النفسي). This allows existence to be included without contradiction.
Otherwise, the contradictory would be true of existence.

Source Text

(قوله: غَايَةُ الْخِلَافِ المراد بها التنافي بينهما، فقوله: (لا يجتمعان) تفسير لها، وخرج بهذا القيد الخلافان كالقيام والضحك، فإن بينهما أصل الخلاف، لا غايته لإمكان اجتماعهما.

?

The Opposition Between Origination and Eternity
Defines two types of opposition between origination and eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of logical opposition between origination and eternity depending on the definition of origination.

Core Idea
The logical relation between origination and eternity shifts with the definition of origination.

In this lesson, we examine the precise logical relationship between two fundamental attributes: origination (الحدوث) and eternity (القدم). The passage explains that the type of opposition depends on whether 'origination' is taken in its metaphorical sense (renewal after non-existence) or its real sense (existence after non-existence). This nuanced distinction is crucial for correct kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two definitions of origination and the corresponding types of opposition.
2
Understand the first definition
When origination is metaphorical (renewal after non-existence), its opposition to eternity is like that of a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory, because there is no intermediary.
The contradictory of eternity is 'not eternal', which equals origination in this sense.
3
Understand the second definition
When origination is real (existence after non-existence), its opposition is like that of a thing and the more specific of its contradictory, because 'not eternal' also includes renewal after non-existence.
Thus origination is more specific than 'not eternal'.
4
Study the gloss on equivalence
The gloss clarifies that 'equivalent' (مساوٍ) does not mean identical; it means they always occur together but are distinct in reality.
This applies to both definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْحُدُوثُ) معطوف على (العدم)، والتقابل بينه وبين القدم من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه؛ لأن نقيض القدم لا قدم، وهو عين الحدوث؛ لأنه لا واسِطَةً بينهما. هذا إن فُسّر الحدوث بمعناه المجازي: وهو التجدُّدُ بعد عَدَمٍ، وأما إن فُسِّرَ بمعناه الحقيقي وهو الوجود بعد عَدَمٍ؛ فالتقابل بينهما من التقابل بين الشيء والأخص من نقيضه؛ لأن نقيض القِدَم: لا قِدَمَ كما علمتَ، وهو يشمل الحدوث بالمعنى المذكور والتجدد بعد عدم، فعلى هذا؛ الحدوث أخصُّ من لا قِدَم الذي هو نقيضُ القدم. (قوله: وَطُرُقُ الْعَدَمِ) أي حصوله بعد أن لم يكن، وهو الفناء، والتقابل بينه وبين البقاء من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه؛ لأن نقيض البقاء: لا بَقَاءَ، وهو عين طرق العدم الذي هو الفناء.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَهُوَ عَيْنُ الْحُدُوثِ المراد: أنه مساءٍ له وليس المراد: أنهما متحدان حقيقة، بل حقيقة انتفاء القدم غير حقيقة الحدوث، سواء كان الحدوث بمعنى التجدد بعد العدم، أو بمعنى الوجود بعد العدم، والمراد بتساويهما أنه كلما تحقق أحدهما تحقق الآخر وإن تباينا حقيقة وهكذا يقال في كل متساويين. (قوله: لَا وَاسِطَةَ بَيْنَهُمَا :أي بين القدم والحدوث، والحدوث بمعنى التجدد بعد العدم، وعلى تقدير الواسطة تكون محققة في القدم ) ، فيكون أعم من الحدوث، وهو التجدد بعد العدم، أي: الحصول بعد العدم، وليس المراد بالتجدد هنا: التكرر وإن كان يطلق عليه أيضاً. (قوله: وَهُوَ التَّجَدُّدُ بَعْدَ عَدَمٍ) أي: الحصول بعد العدم، وليس المراد بالتجدد هنا: التكرر وإن كان يطلق على ذلك أيضاً كما مر. (قوله: وَالتَّجَدُّدُ بَعْدَ عَدَمٍ) أي: المتحقق في الأحوال الحادثة والاعتبارات الحادثة. (قوله: وَهُوَ عَيْنُ طُرُقِ الْعَدَمِ) أي: مساءٍ له لا متحد معه بحيث تكون حقيقتهما واحدة كما تقدم التنبيه عليه.
(1) في المطبوعة: "لا قدم".
His saying: 'And origination' is coordinated with 'non-existence'. And the opposition between it and eternity is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory, because the contradictory of eternity is 'not eternal', which is exactly origination, because there is no intermediary between them. This is if origination is interpreted in its metaphorical sense, which is renewal after non-existence. But if it is interpreted in its real sense, which is existence after non-existence, then the opposition between them is of the type between a thing and the more specific of its contradictory, because the contradictory of eternity is 'not eternal' as you know, and it includes origination in the mentioned sense and renewal after non-existence. So on this, origination is more specific than 'not eternal' which is the contradictory of eternity.
His saying: 'And ways of non-existence' – meaning its occurrence after it was not, which is annihilation. And the opposition between it and permanence is of the type between a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory, because the contradictory of permanence is 'not permanent', which is exactly ways of non-existence, which is annihilation.
Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri:
His saying: 'And it is exactly origination' – meaning it is equivalent to it, and not meaning that they are identical in reality, but rather the reality of the non-existence of eternity is not the reality of origination, whether origination is in the sense of renewal after non-existence or in the sense of existence after non-existence. And what is meant by their equivalence is that whenever one is realized the other is realized, even if they are distinct in reality. And such is said for any two equivalents.
His saying: 'There is no intermediary between them' – i.e., between eternity and origination, and origination in the sense of renewal after non-existence. And if an intermediary is hypothesized, it would be realized in eternity, so it would be more general than origination, which is renewal after non-existence, i.e., coming into existence after non-existence. And 'renewal' here does not mean repetition, although it is also used for that.
His saying: 'And it is renewal after non-existence' – i.e., coming into existence after non-existence, and 'renewal' here does not mean repetition, although it is also used for that, as previously.
His saying: 'And renewal after non-existence' – i.e., realized in originated states and originated considerations.
His saying: 'And it is exactly ways of non-existence' – i.e., equivalent to it, not identical with it such that their reality is one, as previously pointed out.
(1) In the printed edition: 'not eternal'.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines the relationships between the attributes of origination (الحدوث) and eternity (القدم), and between annihilation (طرق العدم) and permanence (البقاء). It begins by noting that 'الحدوث' is coordinated with 'العدم' in the sentence. The opposition between الحدوث and القدم is of a special type: it is the opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory. This is because the contradictory of القدم is 'لا قدم' (not eternal), which is exactly الحدوث when interpreted in its metaphorical sense as 'renewal after non-existence', since there is no intermediary between them. However, if الحدوث is taken in its real sense as 'existence after non-existence', then the opposition is of a different type: between a thing and a more specific instance of its contradictory, because 'لا قدم' includes both الحدوث in the real sense and renewal after non-existence. So in that case, الحدوث is more specific than 'لا قدم'. The passage then turns to 'طرق العدم', which means 'its occurrence after it was not', i.e., annihilation (الفناء). The opposition between it and البقاء is again of the type between a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory, because the contradictory of البقاء is 'لا بقاء', which is exactly طرق العدم. The glosses from al-Ajhuri then clarify several points. First, he explains that 'عين الحدوث' (it is exactly origination) means they are equivalent (مساوٍ), not identical in reality; the reality of the non-existence of eternity is not the same as the reality of origination. Equivalence here means that whenever one is realized, the other is realized, even if their essences are distinct. Second, he explains that there is no intermediary between القدم and الحدوث (when الحدوث is taken as renewal after non-existence). If an intermediary were hypothesized, it would be realized in eternity, making it more general than الحدوث. Third, he confirms that 'التجدد' (renewal) means 'coming into existence after non-existence', not repetition, though the word is also used for repetition. Finally, he repeats that 'عين طرق العدم' means equivalent, not identical in reality.

Key Terms
حدوث
قدم
نقيض
مساءٍ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'حدوث' and 'قدم' are direct contradictories regardless of definition.
Correction: The passage shows the relationship depends on the definition of 'حدوث'; it can be either equivalent to 'لا قدم' or more specific than it.

Checkpoint
If 'حدوث' is defined as 'renewal after non-existence', what type of opposition exists between it and 'قدم'?
A) Thing vs the more specific of its contradictory
B) Thing vs the equivalent of its contradictory
C) Direct contradiction
D) Identity

Key Takeaway
The logical relation between origination and eternity is not fixed; it varies based on the definition of origination, showing the importance of precise terminology in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a simple opposition (like 'thing vs its contradictory') captures the full truth? In your own beliefs, where might you be oversimplifying a relationship between two concepts because you haven't paused to define each term precisely?
?
The passage shows that the meaning of 'huduth' changes the logical relationship. When you say 'The world is created'—are you using 'created' in a metaphorical or real sense? Does your heart truly feel that your own existence came from nothing by God's command, or do you treat it as a mere fact without awe?
?
The gloss emphasizes that 'renewal' means coming into existence after non-existence, not repetition. Is there a routine in your life (like daily prayer or repentance) that you have reduced to a repeated action without truly renewing it from non-existence each time? How would your practice change if you approached it as a fresh origination each moment?

The Opposition Between Annihilation and Permanence
Defines the opposition between annihilation and permanence as equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'طرق العدم' (annihilation) is equivalent to the negation of 'بقاء' (permanence) but not identical in reality.

Core Idea
Annihilation and the negation of permanence are logically equivalent but not identical in essence.

This lesson examines the second pair of attributes: annihilation (طرق العدم) and permanence (البقاء). The passage states that they are equivalent in extension but distinct in reality, a distinction that is critical for avoiding confusion in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short passage on annihilation and permanence, including the gloss.
2
Understand the equivalence
The passage says that the contradictory of permanence is 'not permanent', which is exactly annihilation (طرق العدم).
This makes them equivalent (مساوٍ).
3
Distinguish equivalence from identity
The gloss emphasizes that 'equivalent' does not mean 'identical in reality'; they are distinct in essence but always occur together.
This is a key logical distinction.

The Meaning of 'Kadha' and Its Contrast with Qiyam bi-Nafs
Al-Bajuri clarifies 'kadha' and its logical opposition to self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Bajuri clarifies the term 'kadha' as referring to the list of negated attributes and its logical opposition to self-subsistence.

Core Idea
The word 'kadha' ties together all negated attributes, standing in direct contrast to qiyam bi-nafs.

In this short but crucial passage, Al-Bajuri unpacks the meaning of the word 'kadha' (thus) in Sanusi's text. He explains that it refers back to the previously mentioned negated attributes—non-existence, origination, and what follows. Then he highlights the logical contrast between these negated attributes and the divine attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafs). This sets up a foundational opposition in kalam thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Al-Bajuri defines 'kadha' and then states the contrast.
2
Understand the reference of 'kadha'
'Kadha' means 'like this' and refers to the previously mentioned things: non-existence, origination, etc. This includes all similar negations.
Al-Bajuri says 'and likewise it is said in what comes' meaning the pattern continues.
3
Grasp the logical contrast
The contrast between these negated attributes and qiyam bi-nafs is one of opposition—they cannot coexist. This is a basic logical principle.
The phrase 'as is apparent' shows the self-evident nature of this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذا أي مثل ذا يعني: مثل المذكور من العدم والحدوث وما بعدهما، وكذا يقال فيما يأتي. والتقابل بين ذلك وبين القيام بالنفس من التقابل بين الشيء ونقيضه كما هو ظاهر.
Thus, i.e., like this, meaning: like the mentioned things—non-existence, origination, and what follows them—and likewise it is said in what comes. And the contrast between that and self-subsistence is a contrast between a thing and its opposite, as is apparent.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a brief explanatory note. Al-Bajuri clarifies that the word 'kadha' (thus) in Sanusi's text means 'like this', referring back to previously mentioned concepts of non-existence, origination, and the like. He then states that the relationship between these negated attributes (like non-existence, origination, etc.) and the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafs) is one of opposition. This is a logical contrast between a thing and its opposite, meaning they cannot coexist. The phrasing 'as is apparent' indicates the self-evident nature of this logical relationship.

Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'kadha' includes all similar negations, both those mentioned and those to come.

Key Takeaway
The negated attributes of non-existence and origination are directly opposed to the divine attribute of self-subsistence.

Al-Bajuri says the opposition is 'apparent'. Is it apparent to you? When you look at your life and the world, do you see the evidence of your own origin and potential non-existence? How does that urgency move you to rely on Allah alone, right now?

Why Sanusi Defined Karaha as Not Willing
Bajuri explains and defends Sanusi's definition of karaha, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi explicitly defined karaha as 'not willing' and how this corrects the Mu'tazila misunderstanding about God's will and command.

Core Idea
Karaha is the absence of divine will, not a legal command—this corrects the Mu'tazila error.

Bajuri explains that Sanusi's apparently unusual inclusion of a definition serves a double purpose: to prevent a common misunderstanding about the term 'karaha' and to refute the Mu'tazila claim that God's will always aligns with His commands. This passage shows how careful theological precision guards against major doctrinal errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that Bajuri is explaining why Sanusi included a definition that is not normally part of such texts.
Pay attention to the terms: كراهة, إرادة, أمر.
2
Identify the two main purposes
Bajuri gives two reasons for Sanusi's explicit definition: caution and refutation. Find these in the text.
The refutation is directed at the Mu'tazila.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Notice the phrase 'لا يُقال' where an objection is raised. Then see how Bajuri responds with 'لأنا نقول'. Understand the objection and the reasoning behind the response.
The objection claims the definition is obvious; the answer says caution is needed.
4
Examine the Mu'tazila error
Read the section starting with 'وأيضاً قصد التنبية' to understand the Mu'tazila claim and the refutation. Note the four logical possibilities of command and will.
The fourfold breakdown is key to seeing why there is no necessary connection.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves God's absolute freedom and prevents limiting His will to His commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أَيْ: عَدَمِ إِرَادَتِهِ لَهُ تَعَالَى إنما أتى المصنف بذلك ـ مع أن التفسير ليس من وظيفة المتون ; لئلا يُتَوَهَّمَ أن المراد بالكراهة معناها الشرعي: وهو طَلَبُ تَرْكِ الشيء طلباً غير جازم. لا يُقال: إن المقامَ يقتضي تفسيرها بما ذُكِرَ فلا حاجة للتنصيص عليه; لأنا نقول : المصنف لاحَظَ الاحتياط، وأيضاً قصد التنبية على خطأ المعتزلة في قولهم: إن الإرادة على وفق الأمر وبناؤهم على ذلك أن المكروة شرعاً ليس بمراد، ووجه خطئهم في ذلك: أنه لا ملازَمَةَ بين الأمر ،والإرادة، فقد يأمُرُ ولا يُرِيدُ، وقد يُرِيدُ ولا يأمُرُ، كما أنه قد يريدُ ويأمرُ ، وقد لا يريد ولا يأمرُ كما تقدم توضيحه.
His saying: That is, His not willing it, Exalted is He. The author only mentioned that—even though interpretation is not the function of texts—so that it not be thought that what is meant by 'abhorrence' (karaha) is its legal meaning, which is: commanding the leaving of a thing in a non-decisive manner. It cannot be said that the context necessitates its interpretation as mentioned, so there is no need to specify it; because we say: the author observed caution, and also intended to alert to the error of the Mu'tazila in their statement that will (irada) is in accordance with command (amr), and their building upon that that what is legally abhorrent is not willed. And the aspect of their error is that there is no necessary connection between command and will, for He may command and not will, and He may will and not command, just as He may will and command, and may not will and not command, as has been clarified previously.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of Sanusi's definition of 'abhorrence' (karaha) as 'not willing'. Bajuri begins by noting that Sanusi included this definition even though interpreting terms is not normally the function of didactic texts. The reason is to prevent misunderstanding: the reader might otherwise think karaha means its legal sense—a non-decisive command to leave something. Sanusi wanted to clarify that it means absence of divine will, not a legal command. Bajuri then addresses a potential objection: why specify this when the context makes it clear? He gives two reasons: first, the author was being cautious; second, he aimed to point out the error of the Mu'tazila, who hold that God's will always aligns with His command and thus what is legally abhorrent is not willed. Bajuri refutes them by asserting that there is no necessary correlation between command and will—God may command something He does not will (e.g., testing His servants) and will something He does not command (e.g., permitting some actions). The four possible combinations are listed, emphasizing that the Mu'tazila's position is a mistake.

Key Terms
كراهة
إرادة
أمر
مكروه
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think karaha in this context means the legal category (makruh), i.e., a command to leave something.
Correction: Sanusi defines karaha as absence of divine will, not a legal command. This is a metaphysical attribute of God, not a legal ruling.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi explicitly define karaha as 'not willing' even though interpretation is not normally part of matn texts?
A) To show his mastery of legal terminology
B) To prevent misunderstanding that karaha means its legal sense (command to leave), and to refute the Mu'tazila error
C) Because he was forced by his teacher
D) To make the text longer

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's explicit definition of karaha as 'not willing' is a deliberate theological safeguard against the Mu'tazila error of conflating God's command with His will.

Why Specific Cases Are Mentioned Under General Terms in Kalam
Bajuri defends Sanusi's detailed enumeration and clarifies the distinction between dhuhul and ghafala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi enumerated specific states like heedlessness and forgetfulness under the general category of abhorrence, and how the distinction between dhuhul and ghafala is understood according to different scholarly opinions.

Core Idea
In kalam, details matter because ignorance is dangerous.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary addresses a common objection: if certain states are already included under a general term, why mention them separately? Sanusi, the author of the matn, lists heedlessness (dhuhul), forgetfulness (ghafala), causality (ta'lil), and nature (tab' ) as specific cases of abhorrence (karaha). Bajuri defends this practice, explaining the methodological necessity of detailed exposition in theology. He then clarifies the nuanced difference between dhuhul and ghafala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and answer
Read the passage and look for the objection that Sanusi's enumeration is redundant. Notice how Bajuri responds by invoking the purpose of 'ilm al-kalam: detailed mention of beliefs because of the great danger of ignorance.
This is a classic objection-answer structure (فإن قيل... أجيب).
2
Understand the grammatical structure
Bajuri notes that the terms are conjoined to 'with His abhorrence' using 'or'. This is an example of 'atf al-khas 'ala al-'amm (conjunction of the specific to the general). See how that fits the Arabic grammar.
3
Compare the two opinions on dhuhul and ghafala
The passage ends with a dispute: are dhuhul and ghafala synonyms, or is ghafala more general? Understand the defining nuance: dhuhul is absence of knowledge after prior knowledge, while ghafala is absolute absence.
Sanusi apparently favored the more general definition of ghafala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أَوْ مَعَ الدُّهُولِ أَوِ الْغَفْلَةِ معطوف على قوله: (مع كراهته، وكذا قوله: (أو بالتعليل أو بالطبع، وعَطْفُ ذلك على الكراهة بالمعنى المذكور من عطف الخاص على العام لدخوله فيها. فإن قيل: إذا كانت هذه الأمور داخلةً في الكراهة بذلك المعنى; كان مستغنى عنها، فلا حاجة إلى ذكرها أجيب بأنه إنما ذكرها المصنف ـ مع كونها مستغنى عنها ; لأن المقصود في هذا العلم: ذِكْرُ العقائد على وجه التفصيل; لأن خَطَرَ الجهل فيه عظيم، فلا يُكْتَفَى فيه بعامّ عن خاص، ولا بملزوم عن لازم. واعلم أنه اختلف فقيل : الذُّهُولُ والغَفْلَةُ متساويان. وقيل: الغفلة أعم من الذهول; لأن الذهول: هو عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء مع تقدم العلم به، والغفلة: عدم العلم بالشيء مطلقاً، وهذا هو ما ظهر للمؤلف.
His saying: 'or with heedlessness (dhuhul) or with forgetfulness (ghafala)' is conjoined to his saying: 'with His abhorrence', and likewise his saying: 'or with causality (ta'lil) or with nature (tab')', and the conjunction of that to abhorrence in the mentioned sense is of the type of conjunction of the specific to the general due to their inclusion in it. If it is said: If these matters are included in abhorrence in that sense, then they are dispensed with, so there is no need to mention them. The answer is that the author only mentioned them—even though they are dispensed with—because the purpose in this science is the mention of beliefs in detail, because the danger of ignorance in it is great, so one is not satisfied with the general over the specific, nor with the necessary over the necessary concomitant. And know that there is disagreement: it is said that heedlessness and forgetfulness are equivalent. And it is said that forgetfulness is more general than heedlessness, because heedlessness is absence of knowledge of something with prior knowledge of it, and forgetfulness is absence of knowledge of something absolutely. And this is what appeared to the author.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of Sanusi's inclusion of specific cases (dhuhul, ghafala, ta'lil, tab') under the general term karaha. Bajuri first notes the grammatical structure: these terms are conjoined by 'or' to the phrase 'with His abhorrence', and this conjunction is of the specific to the general—since these states are all instances of the general meaning of karaha (absence of will). He then raises an objection: if they are already included in the general term, why mention them separately? The answer is that in 'ilm al-kalam, the subject matter (beliefs) is so important that the danger of ignorance is great; therefore, scholars do not rely on general expressions when specific ones are needed, nor do they substitute a necessary condition (lazim) for what it entails (malzum). Thus, Sanusi's detailed enumeration is pedagogically necessary. Finally, Bajuri discusses the definitions of dhuhul and ghafala. There are two opinions: one holds they are synonymous; the other—which the author (Sanusi) apparently favors—holds that ghafala is broader, because dhuhul is absence of knowledge after prior knowledge, while ghafala is absolute absence of knowledge.

ذُهُول
غَفْلَة
تعليل
طبع
عطف الخاص على العام
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mentioning specific cases under a general term is redundant and unnecessary.
Correction: In kalam, detailed exposition is required because ignorance of beliefs is severely dangerous; general terms alone do not suffice.

Checkpoint
According to Bajuri, why did Sanusi mention specific states like dhuhul and ghafala even though they are included under the general term karaha?
A) For rhetorical effect
B) Because in kalam, beliefs must be mentioned in detail due to the great danger of ignorance
C) Because the Mu'tazila denied them
D) To confuse students

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's detailed enumeration under karaha reflects the kalam principle that the gravity of ignorance demands explicit, specific exposition, and the distinction between dhuhul and ghafala shows the precision required in defining theological terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a detailed theological explanation like Sanusi's enumeration, do you feel annoyed at the precision or grateful that someone took the danger of ignorance seriously? Which response most accurately reflects your heart?
?
The passage says that in creed, one cannot substitute a general term for a specific one. What is one area of your belief about Allah where you have contented yourself with a vague or general understanding, avoiding the uncomfortable details? Will you commit to investigating it with the same seriousness as Sanusi?
?
If you truly believed that ignorance in matters of 'aqeedah is 'great in danger,' would you be reading this lesson with the same casual attention you give to a social media post? What would change in your attitude if you were convinced of that danger?

Definitions of Dhuhul, Ghafala, Sahw, Nisyan and Their Relation to Will
Bajuri defines terms and explains opposition to will via knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of dhuhul, ghafala, sahw, and nisyan and how they oppose divine will indirectly through their opposition to knowledge.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness and heedlessness oppose will by opposing knowledge, its necessary condition.

In this passage, Bajuri clarifies the technical meanings of these key terms. He then explains that they oppose divine will indirectly because they first oppose knowledge, which is a necessary condition for will. An objection is raised and answered by distinguishing simple ignorance from compound ignorance and other states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the definitions and the logical argument.
The passage includes marginal glosses from al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi.
2
Identify the definitions and relationships
Note how ghafala, dhuhul, sahw, and nisyan are defined, and how they relate (more general, more specific, synonymous).
For example, dhuhul is more general than ghafala, and nisyan is more specific than dhuhul.
3
Understand the opposition to will
The author argues that these states oppose will because they oppose knowledge, and everything that opposes knowledge opposes will. This is because knowledge is a necessary condition (lazim) for will.
This is a key logical principle in kalam.
4
Examine the objection and answer
An objection says the author should have mentioned ignorance (jahl) among opposites of will, and should have listed dhuhul/ghafala among opposites of knowledge. The answer clarifies that only simple ignorance opposes will, while compound ignorance, doubt, etc., do not.
This resolves the objection by showing the author's consistency.
5
Reflect on the marginal glosses
The glosses explain the location of the perceptual faculty (mudrika) and the nature of simple ignorance. Use them to deepen understanding.
Al-Anbabi's gloss explains why simple ignorance opposes knowledge and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: الذهول أعم من الغفلة; لأن الغفلة زوالُ الشيء من المُدْرِكَةِ مع بقائه في الحافظة، والذهول زواله من المُدركة مطلقاً، وعلى هذا فالسهو مرادف للغفلة كما يُؤْخَذُ من القاموس حيث قال: غَفَلَ عنه : تَرَكَه وسَهَا عنه اهـ. وأما النسيانُ; فهو أخصُّ من الذهول; لأنه زوال الشيء من الحافظة والمُدْرِكة معاً. ووجه منافاة كلّ من الذهول والغفلة للإرادة: أنهما مُنَافِيَان للعلم و كل ما كان منافياً للعِلم; كان مُنافياً للإرادة، فهما منافيان للإرادة بواسطة منافاتهما للعلم. فإن قيل: يلزم على ذلك أن يَذْكُرَ أضداد العلم، وهي الجهل وما في معناه في مُنَافِيات الإرادة، ويلزمُ عليه أيضاً أن يذكر الذهول والغفلة في منافيات العلم; لأنهما منافيان له بلا واسطةٍ بخلاف الإرادة، فإنهما منافيان لها بواسطة، فهما أقرب إليه منها. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: مِنَ الْمُدْرِكَةِ هي الواهمة المجاورة للحافظة لأنهما في التجويف المؤخر من الرأس. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: أَنَّهُمَا مُنَافِيَانِ لِلْعِلْمِ) أي: لأن الذهول: عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء مع تقدم العلم به، فهو جَهْل بسيط، والغَفْلَةُ: عدم العلم بالشيء مطلقاً، فهي جَهْل بسيط أيضاً، والجهل البسيط ينافي العلم. (قوله: وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ مُنَافِياً لِلْعِلْمِ كَانَ مُنَافِياً لِلْإِرَادَةِ) أي: لأن العلم لازم للإرادة; إذ لا يريد إلا ما يَعْلَمُ، وكلُّ ما نافى اللازم نافى الملزوم. هذا توجيه كلامه وأصله للشكتاني، وفيه أن إرادة الشيء تتحقق مع الجهل المركب ومع الظنّ والشك والوهم، فهذه الأمور لا تنافي الإرادة مع كونها مُنَافِيَّةً للعلم. نعم; الجهل البسيط مُنَافٍ للإرادة; لأن الشيء إذا جُهِلَ جهلاً بسيطاً; لا يُعْقَلُ تعلق الإرادة به، وبهذا تَعْلَمُ ما في السؤال والجواب المشار إليهما بقوله: (فإن قيل: يلزم على ذلك... إلخ)، ويمكن تكلف تصحيحه. تأمل.
And it is said: heedlessness is more general than forgetfulness, because forgetfulness is the removal of something from the perceptual faculty (al-mudrika) while it remains in memory (al-hafiza), and heedlessness is its removal from the perceptual faculty absolutely. And on this, inadvertence (sahw) is synonymous with forgetfulness, as taken from al-Qamus where he said: He forgot (ghafala) about it: he left it and was inadvertent about it. As for forgetting (nisyan), it is more specific than heedlessness, because it is the removal of the thing from both memory and the perceptual faculty together. And the aspect of opposition of both heedlessness and forgetfulness to will (irada) is that they are opposed to knowledge (ilm), and everything that is opposed to knowledge is opposed to will, so they are opposed to will indirectly through their opposition to knowledge. If it is said: This would require that one mention the opposites of knowledge, which are ignorance (jahl) and what is in its meaning, among the opposites of will, and it would also require that one mention heedlessness and forgetfulness among the opposites of knowledge, because they are opposed to it directly, unlike will, for they are opposed to it indirectly, so they are closer to it [than to will]. Gloss of al-‘Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: from the perceptual faculty [al-mudrika]) is the estimative faculty (al-wahima) adjacent to memory because they are in the posterior cavity of the head. Gloss of al-‘Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: that they are opposed to knowledge) i.e., because heedlessness is absence of knowledge of something with prior knowledge of it, so it is simple ignorance, and forgetfulness is absence of knowledge of something absolutely, so it is also simple ignorance, and simple ignorance opposes knowledge. (His saying: And everything that is opposed to knowledge is opposed to will) i.e., because knowledge is a necessary condition for will, for one only wills what one knows, and everything that opposes the necessary condition opposes the conditioned. This is a justification of his statement, and its origin is from al-Shinkiti, and it contains that the will of a thing is realized with compound ignorance and with supposition, doubt, and illusion, for these matters do not oppose will despite being opposed to knowledge. Indeed, simple ignorance is opposed to will, because if a thing is known with simple ignorance, the attachment of will to it is inconceivable. And by this you know what is in the question and answer referred to by his saying: (If it is said: This would require... etc.), and it is possible to strain to correct it. Reflect.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of technical terms and their relation to divine will. Bajuri first presents a third opinion (credited to some) that dhuhul is more general than ghafala, with specific definitions: ghafala is removal from the perceptual faculty (mudrika) while something remains in memory; dhuhul is removal from the perceptual faculty absolutely, regardless of memory. Sahw (inadvertence) is synonymous with ghafala according to al-Qamus. Nisyan (forgetting) is more specific than dhuhul because it involves removal from both memory and the perceptual faculty. Then Bajuri explains how these states oppose divine will: they oppose knowledge (ilm), and since knowledge is a necessary condition (lazim) for will, anything opposing the necessary condition opposes the conditioned (malzum). Thus, dhuhul and ghafala oppose will indirectly. An objection is raised: if that is the case, then the author should have mentioned direct opposites of knowledge (like jahl) among the opposites of will, and should have listed dhuhul and ghafala among opposites of knowledge (since they oppose it directly). Bajuri does not fully reproduce the answer here but indicates that the objection and answer are present in the text. The marginal glosses clarify: al-Ajhuri identifies mudrika as the estimative faculty (wahima) adjacent to memory, located in the posterior cavity of the head. Al-Anbabi explains that dhuhul and ghafala are simple ignorance (jahl basit), which opposes knowledge. The justification from al-Shinkiti is then given: will can coexist with compound ignorance, supposition, doubt, and illusion, but not with simple ignorance. Therefore, only simple ignorance truly negates will. This resolves the objection: dhuhul and ghafala are simple ignorance, so they oppose knowledge directly and will indirectly; other forms of ignorance (compound) do not oppose will. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of ignorance equally negate divine will.
Correction: Only simple ignorance (jahl basit) actively opposes will; compound ignorance, supposition, doubt, and illusion do not prevent will from attaching to something.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do heedlessness (dhuhul) and forgetfulness (ghafala) oppose divine will indirectly rather than directly?

B) Because they oppose knowledge, and knowledge is a necessary condition for will, so opposing knowledge indirectly opposes will
C) Because God is not affected by human states
D) Because they are not mentioned in the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Simple ignorance opposes will by opposing knowledge, its necessary condition, while other cognitive states do not.

The passage defines heedlessness as absence of knowledge with prior knowledge. In your daily worship, do you ever find yourself knowing the rulings but acting heedlessly? How does that affect the sincerity (ikhlas) of your deeds? What will you do to overcome this heedfulness?

Compound ignorance (thinking you know when you don't) is said to not prevent will, but it can lead to actions based on false knowledge. Definitions of Dhuhul, Ghaflah, and Nisyan in Relation to Will
Al-Bajuri defines three mental states and explains their relation to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise distinctions between forgetfulness (dhuhul), heedlessness (ghaflah), and forgetting (nisyan), and why they are contrary to will.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness, heedlessness, and forgetting differ in what they remove from mind and memory.

Al-Bajuri provides careful definitions of three related mental states that can impede human actions. Before discussing their effect on will, he first distinguishes them from each other. This precision is typical of the kalam tradition, where subtle differences in terms carry significant theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Pay attention to how each term is defined in relation to the perceptual faculty and memory.
Note that dhuhul and ghaflah are compared first, then nisyan.
2
Compare dhuhul and ghaflah
Identify that ghaflah is removal from the perceptual faculty while the thing remains in memory, whereas dhuhul is removal from the perceptual faculty absolutely. This makes dhuhul more general.
3
Understand nisyan
Observe that nisyan is more specific than dhuhul because it involves removal from both memory and the perceptual faculty together.
This clarifies that nisyan is a stronger form of forgetfulness.
4
Grasp the causal chain
Al-Bajuri explains that dhuhul and ghaflah are contrary to knowledge, and whatever is contrary to knowledge is contrary to will. Therefore, they are contrary to will indirectly.
This connection links cognitive states to volitional acts.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why these distinctions matter in theology: human responsibility depends on knowledge and will. States that remove knowledge affect moral accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: الذهول أعم من الغفلة؛ لأن الغفلة زوالُ الشيء من المُدْرِكَةِ مع بقائه في الحافظة، والذهول زواله من المُدركة مطلقاً، وعلى هذا فالسهو مرادف للغفلة كما يُؤْخَذُ من "القاموس" حيث قال: غَفَلَ عنه : تَرَكَه وسَهَا عنه اهـ. وأما النسيانُ؛ فهو أخصُّ من الذهول؛ لأنه زوال الشيء من الحافظة والمُدْرِكة معاً.

ووجه منافاة كلّ من الذهول والغفلة للإرادة: أنهما مُنَافِيَانِ للعلم وكل ما كان منافياً للعلم كان منافياً للإرادة، فهما منافيان للإرادة بواسطة منافاتهما للعلم.
It is said: forgetfulness (dhuhul) is more general than heedlessness (ghaflah); because heedlessness is the removal of a thing from the perceptual faculty while it remains in the memory, and forgetfulness is its removal from the perceptual faculty absolutely. On this basis, inadvertence (sahw) is synonymous with heedlessness, as taken from al-Qamus, where he said: 'He was heedless of it: he neglected it and was inadvertent about it.' As for forgetting (nisyan), it is more specific than forgetfulness; because it is the removal of a thing from the memory and the perceptual faculty together.

And the reason each of forgetfulness and heedlessness is contrary to will (irada): they are contrary to knowledge ('ilm), and everything that is contrary to knowledge is contrary to will; so they are contrary to will by virtue of their being contrary to knowledge.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory passage by al-Bajuri, clarifying three related but distinct psychological terms: dhuhul, ghaflah, and nisyan. He begins by comparing dhuhul and ghaflah, stating that dhuhul is more general. The reason: ghaflah is the removal of something from the perceptual faculty (al-mudrikah) while it remains in the memory (al-hafizah), whereas dhuhul is removal from the perceptual faculty regardless of whether it remains in memory. He then notes that sahw (inadvertence) is a synonym of ghaflah, citing the Qamus. Next, he defines nisyan as more specific than dhuhul, because nisyan involves removal from both the memory and the perceptual faculty together. Having clarified the terms, al-Bajuri explains why dhuhul and ghaflah are contrary to will (irada): they are contrary to knowledge, and whatever is contrary to knowledge is contrary to will. Thus, they oppose will indirectly, through their opposition to knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think dhuhul and nisyan are synonyms or that both imply complete forgetfulness.
Correction: Dhuhul is removal from perception only (memory may remain), while nisyan is removal from both perception and memory, making nisyan more specific.

Checkpoint

B) Ghaflah is removal from perception while the thing remains in memory; dhuhul is removal from perception absolutely.
C) They are synonymous.
D) Ghaflah is more general than dhuhul.

Key Takeaway
Dhuhul, ghaflah, and nisyan are distinct cognitive states; they oppose will by opposing knowledge.

Objection and Answer on the Placement of Opposites of Will
Al-Bajuri answers an objection regarding the placement of dhuhul and ghaflah as opposites of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why linguistic and legal usage, not logical proximity, determines the placement of dhuhul and ghaflah as opposites of will rather than knowledge in the context of kalam.

Core Idea
Linguistic convention overrides logical strictness in defining opposites.

Al-Bajuri tackles a subtle objection about the classification of the opposites of will. The criticism seems logical: since dhuhul and ghaflah directly oppose knowledge, they should be listed there. But the author listed them under will. In his answer, Al-Bajuri concedes the logical point but reveals that the classification follows the conventional usage of language and law. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how kalam thinkers integrate linguistic norms into theological definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. Why does the critic think dhuhul and ghaflah should be opposites of knowledge? Notice the argument about direct vs. indirect opposition.
The critic says they are contrary to knowledge 'without intermediary' (bila wasitah).
2
Examine the answer's concession
Al-Bajuri begins with 'taslim dhalik' (granting that). Why does he concede? What is he accepting?
He accepts the logical point but then introduces a different criterion.
3
Identify the two reasons given
The answer gives two reasons: one about ignorance and knowledge, another about dhuhul/ghaflah and will. What is the common factor in both reasons?
Both rely on 'istimal al-lughah wa al-shar'' (usage of language and law).
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
How does this show that kalam definitions are not purely abstract but engage with ordinary language? Think of why this matters for theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: يلزم على ذلك أن يَذْكُرَ أضداد العلم، وهي الجهل وما في معناه في مُنَافِيات الإرادة، ويلزمُ عليه أيضاً أن يذكر الذهول والغفلة في منافيات العلم؛ لأنهما منافيان له بلا واسطةٍ بخلاف الإرادة، فإنهما منافيان لها بواسطة، فهما أقرب إليه منها. أجيب بتسليم ذلك، لكن لما كان الجهل وما في معناه يقابل العلم لغةً وشرعاً ـ حتى إنه لا يُذْكَرُ في مقابلته غيرُه من الذهول والغفلة ـ؛ خُصَّ بمضادة العلم، ولما كان الذهول والغفلة كثيراً ما يقابلان بالإرادة ـ حتى إنه إذا قيل: فَعَلَ فلان كذا مريداً له يَعْتَذِرُ بأنه حصل له ذهول أو غفلةٌ ـ؛ خُصًا بمضادة الإرادة، فالسبب في ما صَنَعَهُ المصنف : استعمال اللغة والشرع الجهل وما في معناه في مقابلة العِلم، والذهول والغفلة في مقابلة الإرادة.
If it is said: that necessitates that he should mention the opposites of knowledge, which are ignorance and what is of its meaning, among the opposites of will; and also necessitates that he should mention forgetfulness and heedlessness among the opposites of knowledge, because they are contrary to it without intermediary, unlike will, for they are contrary to it by intermediary, so they are closer to it. The answer is: by granting that, but because ignorance and what is of its meaning are opposite of knowledge in language and law — to the extent that no other than them from forgetfulness and heedlessness is mentioned as its opposite — they were specified as contrary to knowledge; and because forgetfulness and heedlessness are often mentioned as opposites of will — to the extent that if it is said: 'So-and-so did such-and-such willingly,' he excuses himself by saying that he experienced forgetfulness or heedlessness — they were specified as contrary to will. So the reason for what the author did is the usage of language and law: ignorance and its like are contrasted with knowledge, and forgetfulness and heedlessness are contrasted with will.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format (qila-ujiba). Al-Bajuri presents a potential objection: if dhuhul and ghaflah are contrary to will only indirectly (through being contrary to knowledge), then the author should have listed the direct opposites of knowledge (like ignorance) among the opposites of will, and also listed dhuhul and ghaflah among the opposites of knowledge since they are directly contrary to it. The objection argues that dhuhul and ghaflah are closer to knowledge than to will. The answer begins by conceding the logical point (taslim dhalik), but explains that the author's placement is based on linguistic and customary usage (istimal al-lughah wa al-shar'). In language and law, ignorance (jahl) and its synonyms are treated as the opposite of knowledge; no other terms like dhuhul or ghaflah are used as opposites of knowledge. Conversely, in everyday speech, when someone is said to have acted willingly (muridan), any excuse for the action is often phrased as 'I experienced forgetfulness or heedlessness,' showing that these terms are customarily contrasted with will. Therefore, the author's classification follows common usage rather than strict logical proximity.

Key Terms
جهل
ذهول
غفلة
علم
إرادة
لغة
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The classification of opposites in kalam is based solely on logical proximity and any deviation is an error.
Correction: Al-Bajuri shows that linguistic and legal convention play a decisive role. The author's classification is not a mistake but follows how these terms are used in language and law.

Checkpoint
Why, according to the answer, does the author place dhuhul and ghaflah as opposites of will rather than of knowledge?
A) Because they are logically more opposed to will than to knowledge.
B) Because in language and custom, ignorance is the opposite of knowledge, while dhuhul and ghaflah are used as excuses for willful acts, so they are contrasted with will.
C) Because the author made an error in logical classification.
D) Because dhuhul and ghaflah are not really contrary to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, the classification of opposites is guided by the actual usage of language and law, not solely by abstract logical relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone gives you a weak excuse like 'I forgot,' do you instantly dismiss them, or do you consider that their forgetfulness might reveal something about their will? How can you apply the lesson's insight about linguistic convention to judge more mercifully? Commit to one change in how you respond to excuses this week.
?
Think of a time you attributed your own mistake to 'heedlessness' or 'forgetfulness.' Were you really being honest about your will? In light of this lesson, will you now examine your intentions more carefully before making excuses? Write a short pact with yourself about using language truthfully.
?
The author prioritizes linguistic convention over strict logic. Is there an area of your faith where you prefer rational consistency over the established usage of the Quran and Sunnah? Are you willing to submit to the linguistic and legal convention of revelation, even when it doesn't seem logically neat? Pause and make a concrete intention to align your understanding with the Shari'ah's usage.

Why Ignorance Opposes Knowledge and Forgetfulness Opposes Will
Sanusi explains the linguistic and legal basis for pairing ignorance with knowledge and forgetfulness with will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical theologians specifically pair ignorance with knowledge and forgetfulness with will, based on linguistic and legal usage.

Core Idea
Ignorance is the opposite of knowledge; forgetfulness is the opposite of will—by usage, not accident.

In the previous lesson, you learned the pairs of opposites. Here, Sanusi responds to an objection: why not pair forgetfulness with knowledge? The answer lies in how language and law actually use these terms. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a feel for the author's argument.
Notice the objection is granted before the explanation.
2
Identify the two pairings and their justifications
The passage gives two separate justifications: one for ignorance vs. knowledge, and one for heedlessness/giddiness vs. will. Identify each and note the reasoning.
Both justifications rely on actual usage in language and law.
3
Understand the principle
Ask: Why does the author appeal to 'language and law'? Because theological categories are not arbitrary; they reflect how terms are actually used in revelation and everyday speech.
This is a key method in kalam: grounding definitions in linguistic and legal reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجيب بتسليم ذلك، لكن لما كان الجهل وما في معناه يقابل العلم لغةً وشرعاً ـ حتى إنه لا يُذْكَرُ في مقابلته غيرُه من الذهول والغفلة ـ؛ خُصَّ بمضادة العلم، ولما كان الذهول والغفلة كثيراً ما يقابلان بالإرادة ـ حتى إنه إذا قيل: فَعَلَ فلان كذا مريداً له يَعْتَذِرُ بأنه حصل له ذهول أو غفلةٌ ـ؛ خُصًا بمضادة الإرادة، فالسبب في ما صَنَعَهُ المصنف : استعمال اللغة والشرع الجهل وما في معناه في مقابلة العِلم، والذهول والغفلة في مقابلة الإرادة.
It was answered by granting that, but since ignorance and what is in its meaning correspond to knowledge in language and law—so much so that nothing other than it (ignorance) is mentioned in opposition to knowledge from among heedlessness and giddiness—it was specified as the opposite of knowledge. And since heedlessness and giddiness often correspond to will—so much so that if it is said: 'So-and-so did such-and-such willing it,' he apologizes that he had heedlessness or giddiness—they were specified as the opposite of will. Thus the reason for what the author did is the usage of language and law: ignorance and what is in its meaning in opposition to knowledge, and heedlessness and giddiness in opposition to will.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection regarding the pairing of opposites. It explains that ignorance is specifically the opposite of knowledge because in both language and law, only ignorance is mentioned as the contrary of knowledge, not heedlessness or giddiness. Similarly, heedlessness and giddiness are paired as opposites of will because one typically uses them as excuses for not acting willingly. Thus the author's pairing follows actual usage, not arbitrary convention.

Key Terms
جهل
علم
ذهول
غفلة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that forgetfulness is the opposite of knowledge, or that all opposites are purely logical.
Correction: Classical usage restricts 'forgetfulness' as opposite of will, not knowledge. Pairings are based on linguistic and legal convention.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is ignorance specifically the opposite of knowledge rather than forgetfulness or giddiness?
A) Because forgetfulness and giddiness are not real opposites.
B) Because in language and law, only ignorance is used as the opposite of knowledge.
C) Because ignorance is more common.
D) Because the author arbitrarily chose it.

Key Takeaway
The pairing of opposites in kalam is based on actual linguistic and legal usage, not arbitrary or purely logical assignment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Grammatical Analysis of 'بمعلوم ما' in the Matn
Bajuri explains the phrase 'بمعلوم ما' and discusses the syntactic attachment of the prepositional phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical function of the phrase 'بمعلوم ما' and the syntactic issue of separation between a verbal noun and its object.

Core Idea
A prepositional phrase can separate a verbal noun from its object, an exception to the usual rule.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary focuses on the phrase 'بمعلوم ما' in the matn. He explains that 'ما' is a nominal adjective indicating generality, then highlights a grammatical issue: the prepositional phrase attaches to 'al-jahl' (ignorance), creating a separation between the verbal noun and its object. This lesson walks through that analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the phrase 'بمعلوم ما'
Read the explanation of the phrase: 'ما' is a nominal adjective meaning 'any', attached to 'معلوم' to indicate generality. This includes all known things.
2
Identify the attachment of the prepositional phrase
Note that the prepositional phrase (الجار والمجرور) is attached to 'al-jahl' (the verbal noun), not directly to the verb. This causes a separation between the verbal noun and its object.
The object is 'معلوم' which is the object of 'al-jahl'.
3
Recognize the grammatical exception
The separation is normally prohibited, but the commentator notes it is excused for prepositional phrases. Reflect on why this might be an exception.
This is a specific rule in Arabic grammar: prepositional phrases have some flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بِمَعْلُومٍ (ما) أي بأي معلوم كان (ف(ما) اسمية صفة لمعلوم، أتى بها للدلالة على العموم في المعلوم، فيشمل جميع المعلومات كما تقدَّم نظيره، ولا يخفى أن الجار والمجرور متعلّق بالجهل، لكن يلزم على ذلك الفصل بين المصدر ومعموله بأجنبي، إلا أن يُقال: إنه يُغْتَفَرُ في الجار والمجرور ما لا يُغْتَفَرُ في غيره.
His statement: 'بمعلوم (ما)' means 'by any known thing whatsoever' — so (ما) is a nominal adjective modifying 'معلوم', brought to indicate generality in the known, so it includes all known things as its parallel preceded. It is not hidden that the prepositional phrase is attached to 'al-jahl' (ignorance), but that requires separation between the verbal noun and its object by a foreign element, unless it is said that what is excused in the prepositional phrase is not excused in others.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical gloss on the matn's phrase 'بمعلوم ما'. The commentator explains that 'ما' is a nominal adjective meaning 'any', indicating generality. He then discusses the syntactic attachment of the prepositional phrase: it is attached to 'al-jahl' (ignorance) which is a verbal noun, but that creates a separation between the verbal noun and its object (معلوم) by an intervening element (the prepositional phrase itself). The commentator notes that such separation is sometimes excused for prepositional phrases.

Key Terms
الجار والمجرور
المصدر
معمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the prepositional phrase attaches directly to the verb or that the separation is not allowed at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the phrase attaches to the verbal noun, and the separation, though usually prohibited, is excused specifically for prepositional phrases.

Checkpoint
What is the grammatical issue discussed in this passage?
A) The use of 'ما' as a negating particle.
B) The attachment of a prepositional phrase causing separation between a verbal noun and its object.
C) The definition of ignorance.
D) The difference between literal and metaphorical speech.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'بمعلوم ما' indicates generality, and its attachment to the verbal noun 'al-jahl' demonstrates a grammatical exception where separation between a verbal noun and its object is permitted for prepositional phrases.

Objection on al-bakam and its answer
Al-Bajuri answers an objection about al-bakam using literal and metaphorical senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri resolves the objection that al-bakam only opposes verbal speech by distinguishing literal and metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
Al-bakam can metaphorically oppose inner speech.

Al-Bajuri addresses a potential challenge: if al-bakam means muteness, how can it oppose inner speech? The answer lies in the distinction between literal and metaphorical application, showing the flexibility of theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection followed by answer.
The objector assumes a single meaning for 'bkam'.
2
Identify the two senses
Identify the literal sense (affliction preventing verbal speech) and the metaphorical sense (affliction preventing inner speech).
The metaphorical extension is crucial for the argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the discussion of divine attributes. It shows that terms can be used flexibly in kalam.
This answers the objection and allows the attribute of speech to be discussed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ على المصنف بأن البَكَم إنما يضادُّ الكلام اللفظي، لا الكلام النفسي الذي كلامنا فيه، وأجيب بأن البكم كما يطلق حقيقةً على: آفَةٍ تمنع من الكلام اللفظي؛ يُطلَقُ مجازاً على آفةٍ تمنعُ من الكلام النفسي، وذلك هو المراد هنا. (قوله: وَأَضْدَادُ الصَّفَاتِ الْمَعْنَويَّةِ وَاضِحَةٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ) أي: لأنك إذا علمت أن ضد القدرة العجزُ؛ علمتَ أن ضدَّ كونه قادراً كونه عاجزاً، وإذا علمت أن ضدَّ الإرادة الكراهة؛ علمتَ أن ضدَّ كونه مريداً كونه كارهاً، وهكذا. وعُلِمَ مما تقرر أن اسم الإشارة في كلام المصنف راجع لأضداد صفات المعاني، وهو ما يُؤْخَذُ من كلام السكتاني؛ وإن كان كلام بعضهم صريحاً في أنه راجع لصفات المعاني؛ لأنه يُخْوجُ إلى تقدير مضاف بأن يقال: واضحة من أضداد هذه، مع كونه خلاف المتبادر من كلام المصنف فتدبر.
An objection was raised against the author that al-bakam (muteness) only opposes verbal speech, not the inner speech which is our subject here. It was answered that al-bakam, just as it is applied literally to an affliction that prevents verbal speech, is applied metaphorically to an affliction that prevents inner speech, and that is what is intended here. (His saying: 'And the opposites of the essential attributes are clear from these') meaning: because if you know that the opposite of power is incapacity, you know that the opposite of His being Powerful is His being Incapable, and if you know that the opposite of will is aversion, you know that the opposite of His being Willing is His being Averse, and so on. And it is understood from what has been established that the demonstrative pronoun in the author's speech refers to the opposites of the essential attributes, which is taken from the words of al-Saktani; although the words of some are explicit that it refers to the essential attributes themselves, because it necessitates an implied genitive construction by saying 'clear from the opposites of these', despite that being contrary to the apparent meaning of the author's speech, so reflect.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit followed by a grammatical clarification. First, an objection is raised that the term 'al-bakam' (muteness) only opposes verbal speech, whereas the discussion is about inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). The answer distinguishes between literal and metaphorical usage: literally, 'bkam' means an affliction preventing verbal speech; metaphorically, it can mean an affliction preventing inner speech, which is the intended meaning. Next, al-Bajuri explains the author's statement about the opposites of the essential attributes being clear from what preceded. He gives examples: if you know the opposite of power is incapacity, then you know the opposite of 'being Powerful' is 'being Incapable'; similarly for will and aversion. He then clarifies that the demonstrative pronoun in the author's phrase 'clear from these' refers to the opposites of the essential attributes, not the attributes themselves, as understood from al-Saktani. Although some explicitly take it as referring to the attributes, that would require an implied genitive construction, contrary to the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
al-bakam (البكم)
al-kalam al-lafzi (الكلام اللفظي)
al-kalam al-nafsi (الكلام النفسي)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-bakam can only refer to a physical condition preventing speech.
Correction: It also has a metaphorical meaning referring to an affliction preventing inner speech.

Checkpoint
How does al-Bajuri answer the objection that al-bakam only opposes verbal speech?
A) He denies that al-bakam can apply to inner speech.
B) He says al-bakam is used metaphorically for an affliction preventing inner speech.
C) He argues that the objection is irrelevant.
D) He redefines al-bakam as a physical condition.

Key Takeaway
Al-bakam can metaphorically refer to an affliction preventing inner speech, resolving the objection.

The pronoun refers to opposites, not attributes themselves.

The Permissible in God's Right and the Objection of Circularity
Al-Bajuri explains the wording of the third division and presents a circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author phrased the third section as 'As for the permissible in His right' rather than 'from what is permissible,' and the objection of circularity raised by the synonymy of 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin'.

Core Idea
The permissible in God's right is a limited category, leading to a circularity objection.

In this section, al-Bajuri explains the author's wording choice for the third division of what is obligatory to know about God. He then presents an objection that if 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms, the author's definition would be circular. This sets the stage for the answers that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the phrasing difference
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author says 'As for the permissible in His right' not 'From what is permissible in His right'.
This phrasing is deliberate and indicates a confinement.
2
Understand why the phrasing is different
The permissible in God's right is confined to what he mentioned (doing or leaving every permissible), unlike the obligatory and impossible which are not confined.
3
Grasp the circularity objection
Since 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms, defining the permissible in God's right as doing every permissible or leaving it would be defining a thing by itself, causing circularity.
This objection motivates the answers in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا الْجَائِزُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى... إلخ) هذا هو القسم الثالث مما يجب على المكلَّف معرفته، وإنما لم يقل المصنف ومما يجوز في حقه تعالى كما قال: (فمما يجب في حقه (تعالى) ومما يستحيل في حقه تعالى)؛ لأن الجائز في حقه تعالى مُنْحَصِرٌ فيما ذكره، بخلاف كلّ من الواجب والمستحيل في حقه تعالى، فإن كُلاً منهما غيرُ منحصر فيما ذُكِرَ كما علم مما تقدم. واعترض على المصنف بأن الجائز والمُمْكِنَ مترادفان عند المتكلمين وحينئذٍ يكون في كلامه أَخْذُ الشيء في تعريف نفسه، فكأنه قال: وأما الجائز في حقه تعالى، ففِعْلُ كلّ جائز أو تَرْكُهُ، أو: وأما الممكنُ في حقه تعالى ففِعْلُ كلّ ممكن أو تركه، وذلك مُوجِبٌ لِلدَّوْرِ لتوقف كلّ من المعرَّف والتعريف على الآخر حينئذ. وأجيب عن ذلك بأجوبة، أحسنُها أن كلاً من الجائز والممكن يُطلَقُ ويراد به: (قوله: أَحْسَنُهَا ... إلخ) حاصله أن الجائز المعرف معناه: الشيء الموصوف بالجواز والممكن الواقع في التعريف معناه الشيء الموصوف بالإمكان الذي هو الجواز، فيجعل الشيء الأول على تعلق القدرة، والشيء الثاني على المقدور، فيحل كلام المتن إلى قولنا وأما تعلق القدرة الموصوف بالجواز في حقه تعالى؛ ففعل كل مقدور موصوف بالإمكان، فصار الجائز المعرف غير الممكن الواقع في هذا التعريف لاختلاف الموصوف فيهما. ويرد على هذا الجواب أن الجواز موجود في كل من المعرف والتعريف لما علمت أن الإمكان الذي تضمنه الممكن هو الجواز الذي تضمنه الجائز، وهذا كاف في حصول الدور. فالأولى ما أجاب به بعضهم من أن هذا ليس من باب التعريف، بل المقصود به بيان أفراد الجائز في حقه تعالى، وأما تعريف الجائز؛ فقد تقدم في قوله: (والجائز: ما يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه، ومما يدل على صحة هذا الجواب: أن قوله: فمما يجب لمولانا جل وعز عشرون صفة لا يقصد به إلا بيان أفراد الواجب، وكذا قوله: (ومما يستحيل في حقه تعالى عشرون صفة لم يقصد به إلا بيان أفراد المستحيل فتعين أن قوله: (وأما الجائز... إلخ) لبيان أفراد الجائز في حقه. وما اعترض به على هذا الجواب من يصير المعنى عليه وأما أفراد الجائز؛ ففعل أفراد الجائز، وهو لا يستقيم؛ لا يرد إلا لو أريد بيان أفراد الجائز مطلقاً مع أن ذلك غير مراد، بل المراد بيان أفراد الجائز في حقه تعالى والإخبار عنها بأنها فعل أفراد الجائز من حيث هو مستقيم، لكن المراد بأفراد الجائز التي أضيف إليها الفعل: المقدورات التي تفعل تارة وتترك أخرى، كذات زيد وذات عمرو وبياض خالد وغير ذلك من الذوات والصفات، وأفراد الجائز في حقه تعالى: فعل تلك الأفراد وتركها. والحاصل أن تعريف الجائز بقولنا: ما يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه شامل للذوات والصفات وللأفعال والتروك المتعلقة بتلك الذوات والصفات، والجائز في حقه تعالى هو القسم الثاني، وهو الأفعال والتروك المتعلقة بتلك الذوات والصفات، فالجائز في حقه تعالى أخص من مطلق الجائز؛ إذ لا معنى لكون ذات زيد وبياضه مثلاً جائزين في حقه تعالى. وأجاب بعضهم أيضاً بأن المراد بالممكن الواقع في التعريف: الشيء بأن يجرد الممكن عن صفة الإمكان، فتحصل أن الأجوبة ثلاثة، وأبعدها ما ذكره المحشي.
(His saying: 'As for the permissible in His right, exalted be He... etc.') This is the third division of what is obligatory for the responsible person to know. The author did not say 'And from what is permissible in His right, exalted be He' as he said: 'From what is obligatory in His right (exalted be He) and from what is impossible in His right, exalted be He' because the permissible in His right, exalted be He, is confined to what he mentioned, unlike each of the obligatory and the impossible in His right, exalted be He, for each of them is not confined to what was mentioned, as is known from what preceded. An objection was raised against the author that 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' are synonymous among the theologians, and then his speech involves taking a thing in its own definition, as if he said: 'As for the permissible in His right, exalted be He, it is the doing of every permissible or leaving it,' or 'As for the possible in His right, exalted be He, it is the doing of every possible or leaving it,' and that necessitates circularity because each of the defined and the definition depend on the other. That was answered with several answers, the best of which is that each of 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' is used and intended to mean: (His saying: 'The best of them... etc.') Its summary is that the defined 'al-ja'iz' means the thing described with permissibility, and 'al-mumkin' occurring in the definition means the thing described with possibility, which is permissibility. So the first thing is made to apply to the connection of power, and the second thing to the object of power. Then the text is resolved to our saying: 'As for the connection of power described with permissibility in His right, exalted be He, it is the doing of every object of power described with possibility.' Thus the defined 'al-ja'iz' is not the same as the 'al-mumkin' occurring in this definition due to the difference in what is described between them. It is objected to this answer that permissibility is present in both the defined and the definition, because you know that the possibility contained in 'al-mumkin' is the permissibility contained in 'al-ja'iz', and this suffices for the occurrence of circularity. So better is what some answered: that this is not a matter of definition, rather the intent is to list the instances of 'al-ja'iz' in His right, exalted be He. As for the definition of 'al-ja'iz', it has preceded in his saying: 'Al-ja'iz is what is intellectually sound in its existence and non-existence.' Evidence for the correctness of this answer is that his saying: 'From what is obligatory for our Master, majestic and mighty, are twenty attributes' intends only to list the instances of the obligatory, and similarly his saying: 'From what is impossible in His right, exalted be He, are twenty attributes' intends only to list the instances of the impossible. So it is established that his saying: 'As for the permissible... etc.' is for listing the instances of the permissible in His right. The objection raised against this answer that the meaning would become 'As for the instances of the permissible, they are the doing of the instances of the permissible,' which is not sound, is not valid unless it intended to list the instances of the permissible absolutely, but that is not intended. Rather, the intent is to list the instances of the permissible in His right, exalted be He, and to inform about them that they are the doing of the instances of the permissible in that it is sound. But the intended meaning of 'the instances of the permissible' to which the act is added are the potentialities (maqdurat) that are sometimes done and sometimes left, like the essence of Zayd, the essence of 'Amr, the whiteness of Khalid, and other essences and attributes. And the instances of the permissible in His right, exalted be He, are the doing and leaving of those instances. The conclusion is that the definition of 'al-ja'iz' by our saying: 'What is intellectually sound in its existence and non-existence' includes essences, attributes, acts, and omissions related to those essences and attributes. The permissible in His right, exalted be He, is the second type, i.e., the acts and omissions related to those essences and attributes. So the permissible in His right, exalted be He, is more specific than absolute permissibility, since there is no meaning to Zayd's essence or his whiteness, for example, being permissible in His right, exalted be He. Some also answered that what is meant by 'al-mumkin' occurring in the definition is the thing by stripping 'al-mumkin' of the attribute of possibility. Thus the answers become three, and the most remote is what the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical discussion of the definition of 'al-ja'iz' (the permissible) in relation to God. It begins by explaining why the author phrased the third section differently: he said 'As for the permissible in His right' rather than 'from what is permissible', because the permissible in God's right is confined to what he mentioned (acts and their omissions), whereas the obligatory and impossible are not confined. Then an objection of circularity is raised: since 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' are synonyms among theologians, the author's statement 'the permissible in His right is doing every permissible or leaving it' would define a thing by itself. Several answers are given. The first answer (considered best) distinguishes between the thing described with permissibility (al-ja'iz) and the thing described with possibility (al-mumkin), making the former refer to the connection of power and the latter to the object of power, thus they differ in what is described. However, this answer is refuted because permissibility is common to both terms, so circularity persists. The second answer (better) is that the passage is not a definition but an enumeration of instances; the definition of 'al-ja'iz' was already given earlier. Evidence from similar passages on the obligatory and impossible supports this. An objection to this answer is rebutted by clarifying that the intention is to enumerate instances of the permissible in God's right, not absolute permissibility. The passage then summarizes that the general definition of 'al-ja'iz' includes essences, attributes, acts, and omissions, but the permissible in God's right is limited to acts and omissions, making it more specific. A third answer is briefly mentioned: stripping 'al-mumkin' of the attribute of possibility. The passage concludes that there are three answers, the last being the weakest.

Key Terms
al-ja'iz
al-mumkin
al-dawr

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's statement 'As for the permissible in His right...' is a formal definition of the permissible.
Correction: The author is enumerating the instances of the permissible in God's right, not defining it; the definition was given earlier.

Checkpoint
Why did the author say 'As for the permissible in His right' instead of 'From what is permissible in His right'?
A) Because the permissible in His right is not confined.
B) Because the permissible in His right is confined to what he mentioned.
C) Because he wanted to avoid circularity.
D) Because the permissible is synonymous with the possible.

Key Takeaway
The author's wording for the third division reflects the confinement of the permissible in God's right, but it opens the door to an objection of circularity due to the synonymy of ja'iz and mumkin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power, does the idea that His permissible actions are limited to 'doing or leaving every permissible' feel restrictive to you? How does this definition affect your understanding of God's will and freedom? What will you do to trust His wisdom even within these categories?
?
The objection of circularity shows how easily our definitions can trap us. Consider a belief you hold about God or predestination. Are you using terms that are undefined or circular? What concrete step can you take this week to examine and clarify that belief?

Logic and Technical Terms

7 min
Answers to the Circularity Objection
Al-Bajuri presents three answers to the circularity objection, advocating for the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three answers to the circularity objection, and why the second answer—that the passage is an enumeration not a definition—is considered the best.

Core Idea
The passage is an enumeration of instances, not a definition, thus avoiding circularity.

Al-Bajuri now presents the responses to the circularity objection. He examines three answers, arguing that the best is to recognize that the passage is not a definition but a listing of instances. This resolves the problem and aligns with similar passages on the obligatory and impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the three answers
Read the Arabic and translation for the three answers: the first distinguishes between described subjects, the second reinterprets as enumeration, the third strips possibility from mumkin.
Note that the second answer is presented as better.
2
Understand why the first answer is refuted
Despite distinguishing by referent, both terms still contain the notion of permissibility, so circularity remains.
3
Grasp the strength of the second answer
It cites parallel passages on the obligatory and impossible to show that the author's intent is to enumerate instances, not define. This avoids circularity completely.
The third answer is mentioned but deemed weak.

Commentary

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circularity objection is unanswerable.

What is the primary reason the second answer is considered better than the first?
A) It distinguishes between ja'iz and mumkin.

C) It strips possibility from mumkin.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about what God 'can' do, do you often include things like creating a second universe or changing the past? Al-Bajuri says that the permissible in God's right is specifically acts and omissions, not essences. Have you ever considered that some of what you call 'possible for God' might actually be outside the scope of what He has made permissible for Himself? How does this realization confront your assumptions about divine power? Will you now examine your beliefs about God's actions more carefully?
?
Al-Bajuri argues that the best answer to the circularity objection is to see the passage as an enumeration, not a definition. In your own life, do you sometimes mistake a list of examples for a complete definition? For instance, do you define God's mercy only by the examples you know? How does this passage challenge you to be more precise in your understanding of theological terms? What will you do to ensure your definitions are accurate?
