Definition of Burhan and Its Relation to Dalil
Al-Bajuri defines burhan, gives its etymology, and distinguishes it from dalil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meaning of 'burhan' in Islamic logic and how it is distinguished from the broader term 'dalil'.

Core Idea
Burhan is a demonstrative proof that cuts through doubt and whitens the heart with certainty.

Al-Bajuri begins by exploring the word 'burhan' itself, tracing its Arabic roots and then clarifying its precise technical usage. This lesson unpacks those etymological insights and shows why a burhan is more specific than a general dalil—it must be built from two certain premises.

Guided Path
1
Explore the etymology of burhan
Read the two proposed origins: from 'barh' (cutting) and 'barah' (whiteness). Notice how each connects to the function of proof.
The first etymology emphasizes cutting off the opponent; the second emphasizes purifying the heart.
2
Understand the two views on burhan and dalil
Identify the two opinions: that they are synonymous, and that burhan is more specific. Note which one al-Bajuri considers correct.
3
Analyze the correct view with the poem
Examine the line from al-Sullam that defines burhan as composed of certain premises. See how this restricts burhan compared to dalil.
4
Distinguish burhan from dalil in your own words
Write a one-sentence summary explaining the key difference based on the passage.
Burhan: compound of two certain premises. Dalil: can be compound/simple, certain/probable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبُرْهَانُ مأخوذ من: البَزه: وهو القطعُ، يقال: بَرَهْتُ العود، أي: قطعته؛ لأنه يقطع الخصم عن المحاجة. وقيل: من البره: وهو البياضُ، ويقال: امرأةٌ بَرْهَاءُ، أي: بيضاءُ؛ لأنه يبيّضُ القلب ويصفّيه من الجهل. وهو والدليل مترادفان. وقيل: هو أخصُّ من الدليل؛ لأنه يختص بالمركب من مقدمتين يقينيتين كما قال صاحب "السلم" أَجَلُهَا الْبُرْهَانُ مَا أُلِفَ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ بِالْيَقِينِ تَقْتَرِنُ بخلاف الدليل، فإنه يكون مركباً وغير مركب، وقطعياً وظنياً، وهذا هو الصحيح.
And 'burhan' is derived from 'barh', which is cutting. It is said: 'barah-tu al-'ood', i.e., I cut it; because it cuts off the opponent from argumentation. And it is said it is from 'barah', which is whiteness; and it is said: a woman is 'barhaa', i.e., white; because it whitens the heart and purifies it from ignorance. And it [burhan] and 'dalil' are synonymous. And it is said it is more specific than 'dalil'; because it is restricted to what is composed of two certain premises, as the author of 'al-Sullam' said: 'The greatest of them is burhan, which is composed of premises that are certain in conjunction.' Unlike 'dalil', for it can be compound or non-compound, and certain or probable. And this is the correct view.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a definition and linguistic analysis of the term 'burhan' (demonstrative proof). Al-Bajuri first gives two etymologies: one connecting it to 'barh' (cutting), as a burhan cuts off the opponent's argument; the other to 'barah' (whiteness), as it whitens the heart from ignorance. He then discusses the relationship between 'burhan' and 'dalil' (evidence). There are two views: one that they are synonymous, and another—which al-Bajuri identifies as correct—that 'burhan' is more specific than 'dalil'. Specifically, 'burhan' is restricted to a compound argument consisting of two certain premises, forming a syllogism that yields certain knowledge. In contrast, 'dalil' is broader: it can be compound or simple, and it can be certain or probabilistic. Al-Bajuri supports this second view by citing a line from the didactic poem 'al-Sullam' which defines burhan as what is composed of certain premises.

دليل
مقدمتين يقينيتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might confuse burhan with any argument or evidence.
Correction: Burhan is specifically a demonstrative proof with two certain premises, unlike dalil which can be simpler or probabilistic.

Checkpoint

B) Burhan is derived from Arabic etymology, while dalil is not.
C) Burhan is specifically composed of two certain premises, while dalil can be compound or simple, certain or probable.

Key Takeaway
Burhan is a specific type of evidence composed of two certain premises, unlike dalil which can be simpler or probabilistic.

?
You now know that a burhan requires two certain premises. Are there beliefs you hold that rest on only one shaky premise? Will you examine them today and replace them with a solid foundation?

Al-Ajhuri on Looseness of Expression and Transference of Accidents
Al-Ajhuri clarifies three phrases from al-Bajuri's text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ajhuri clarifies three key phrases from al-Bajuri's text: the leniency in attributing observation, the referent of the pronoun in 'to another', and the meaning of an accident's concealment.

Core Idea
Technical terms in kalam can be lenient for brevity, but must be precisely interpreted.

Al-Ajhuri's marginal notes are a classic example of how later scholars unpack ambiguous or compressed expressions in earlier texts. In this passage, he addresses three specific points: why the author said they were 'lenient' in attributing observation to accidents, what 'another' refers to in the claim of accident transference, and what it means for an accident to be concealed. These clarifications prevent misunderstandings that could lead to theological errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read al-Ajhuri's Arabic text and its English translation to get the overall flow of his clarifications.
Notice that he explains three phrases in sequence.
2
Identify the three clarifications
The passage covers 'tasāhul' (leniency), 'li-ghayrihi' (to another), and 'kawnuhu' (its concealment). For each, note what he says.
These correspond to common points of confusion in kalam discussions.
3
Understand the rationale
Think about why each clarification matters for the broader argument about accidents and observation.
Al-Ajhuri's glosses ensure the technical terms are correctly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: تَسَاهلُوا فِي ذَلِكَ أي في إسناد المشاهدة إلى الأعراض الشاملة للحركة والسكون مع أنهما لا يشاهدان، والمراد بضيق العبارة: عسر تفصيلها عليهم؛ لما فيه من التطويل. (قوله: لِغَيْرِهِ الضمير عائد على الجرم المعلوم من المقام، أي لغير الجرم الذي كان قائماً به. (قوله: وإبْطَالِ (كونه أي استتاره في الجرم عند ظهور ضده.
Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'they were lenient in that' meaning in attributing observation to accidents that include motion and rest even though they are not observed. And what is meant by 'restrictive expression' is the difficulty of detailing it for them due to the length. His saying: 'to another' the pronoun refers to the body known from the context, meaning to other than the body in which it subsisted. His saying: 'and refuting its concealment' meaning its hiding in the body at the appearance of its opposite.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This marginal note by al-Ajhuri explains three phrases from al-Bajuri's main text. First, 'tasāhalu fī dhālika' (they were lenient in that) refers to the earlier point about attributing observation to accidents like motion and rest even though they are not directly observed. The reason for this looseness is the difficulty of fully expressing the detailed truth without prolonged explanation. Second, the pronoun in 'li-ghayrihi' (to another) in the phrase 'refuting its transference to another' refers to a body other than the one in which the accident originally existed. Third, 'kawnuhu' (its concealment) means the accident's hiding within the body when its opposite appears, such as blackness being concealed when whiteness appears. These clarifications ensure the technical terms are correctly understood.

Key Terms
تساهل
ضيق العبارة
كون
استتار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The looseness of expression might be seen as inaccuracy or error.
Correction: Al-Ajhuri clarifies that the leniency is a practical necessity for brevity, not a mistake.

Checkpoint

B) It is a concession for the sake of brevity.
C) It is a poetic license.
D) It is an intentional error.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ajhuri's glosses show that technical terms in kalam must be interpreted with attention to context and authorial intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Seven Demands and Their Mnemonic Poem
Bajuri enumerates the seven demands and presents a mnemonic poem with marginal commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven demands (al-matalib al-sab'a) that secure salvation from Hell and the mnemonic poem that encodes them.

Core Idea
Seven rational demands summarize the proof for creation, saving believers from the seven gates of Hell.

After establishing the necessity of accidents, bodies, and their temporal origination, the text now names these lines of refutation as the 'seven demands.' Knowing them is not merely intellectual—it is salvific, protecting one from the seven gates of Hell. The author then provides a compact poetic summary that encapsulates each demand, with later scholars clarifying the meaning line by line.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the shift from prose to poetry.
The poem is a mnemonic device—each short phrase stands for a key claim.
2
Identify the seven demands
The poem lists seven elements: Zayd, ma qama, ma intaqal, ma kamana, ma infakka, la 'udmu qadimin, la hana. Match each to the explanation in the marginal commentary.
Each phrase negates or affirms a specific doctrine about accidents and bodies.
3
Understand the significance
Notice why these are called 'demands'—they are required conclusions to prove creation. Also note the link to the seven gates of Hell and the statement about the firmly grounded.
This elevates the material from mere argument to a matter of faith and salvation.
4
Reflect on the poetic form
Consider why scholars used poetry: memorization and encapsulation. The marginalia explain grammatical elisions for meter.
Think about how meter forced changes like dropping the alif of 'ma'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأمور تسمَّى المطالب السَّبْعَة، وبمعرفتها ينجو المكلف من أبواب جهنم السبعة كما قال المصنف قال: ولا يعرفها إلا الراسخون في العلم اهـ. وقد أشار لها بعضهم بقوله:
زَيْدٌ مَ قَامَ مَا انْتَقَلْ مَا كَمَنَا مَا انْفَكَ لَا عُدْمُ قَدِيمٍ لَا حَنَا
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: زَيْدٌ) أي: للأجرام شيء زائد عليها، فهو مبتدأ محذوف، حذف خيره. (وقوله: مَ قَامَ) (م) نافية حذفت ألفها لضرورة الوزن والضمير عائد على الزيد بمعنى الزائد، وكذا الضمائر بعد.
(وقوله: لَا حَنَا مُنْتَحَتْ من قولهم: لا حوادث لا أول لها.

(قوله: زيد مصدر زَادَ، وهو إشارة لإثبات زائد على الأجرام.

(وقوله: مَا انْتَقَلْ) بسكون اللام للوزن، وهو إشارة إلى نفي انتقال العرض. :(وقوله: مَا كَمَنَا) قيل: إنه من باب نَصَرَ وسَمِعَ، وهو إشارةً إلى نفي كُمُون العرض. وقوله: مَا انْفَكَ) إشارة إلى ملازمة الأجرام للأعراض.
(وقوله: لَا عُدْمُ قَدِيمٍ) بضم العين وسكون الدال، مُرَكَّب إضافي اسم (لا)، خبره محذوف.
(وقوله: لَا حَنَا) (لا) نافيةً، و(حنا) رَمَزَ بالحاء إلى حوادث لا أول لها، أي: لا حوادث لا أوّلَ لها كائنة لنا.
These matters are called the seven demands (al-matalib al-sab'a). By knowing them, the morally responsible person is saved from the seven gates of Hell, as the author said: "And none know them but the firmly grounded in knowledge." End. And someone alluded to them by his saying:
Zayd: what stood, what transferred not, what hid not, what never separated, no annihilation of the eternal, no hana.
Margin by al-Ajhuri: (His saying: Zayd) meaning: for bodies there is something additional to them, so it is a deleted subject, its predicate deleted. (His saying: ma qama) 'ma' is negative, its alif omitted for meter, and the pronoun refers to 'al-zaid' meaning the additional thing, and likewise the pronouns after. (His saying: la hana) is derived from their saying: no events without beginning.
Annotations by al-Anbabi: (His saying: Zayd) is the masdar of 'zada', and it is an indication of establishing something additional to bodies. (His saying: ma qama) with the alif of the negative 'ma' deleted for meter; 'qam' is a past tense verb indicating the negation of the accident's subsistence by itself. (His saying: ma intaqal) with the lam sukun for meter, and it is an indication of negating the transfer of the accident. (His saying: ma kamana) it is said that it is from the verbs of nasara and sami'a, and it is an indication of negating the concealment of the accident. His saying: ma infakka is an indication of the inseparability of bodies from accidents. (His saying: la 'udmu qadimin) with damm on 'ayn and sukun on dal, it is a genitive compound, the noun of 'la', its predicate deleted. (His saying: la hana) 'la' is negative, and 'hana' symbolizes by the ha' events without beginning, i.e., no events without beginning for us.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage concludes the preceding arguments by naming the key refutations as the 'seven demands' (al-matalib al-sab'a). The author emphasizes that knowing these saves the believer from the seven gates of Hell, citing the original author's (Sanusi's) statement that only the firmly grounded in knowledge comprehend them. Then a poetic summary is given, encoding each of the seven elements: 'Zayd' refers to the additional existence of accidents; 'ma qama' negates the accident's self-subsistence; 'ma intaqal' negates its transfer; 'ma kamana' negates its concealment; 'ma infakka' affirms the inseparability of bodies and accidents; 'la 'udmu qadimin' denies the annihilation of the eternal; 'la hana' negates events without beginning. The marginalia from al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi provide grammatical and conceptual clarifications, such as that 'Zayd' is a subject with elided predicate, and 'ma' is a negative particle with shortened form for metrical necessity. The final line 'la hana' is derived from 'la hawaditha la awwala laha' (no events without beginning). This mnemonic helps students memorize the essential components of the cosmological proof.

أبواب جهنم السبعة
الراسخون في العلم
زَيْدٌ مَ قَامَ مَا انْتَقَلْ مَا كَمَنَا مَا انْفَكَ لَا عُدْمُ قَدِيمٍ لَا حَنَا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'Zayd' in the poem refers to a person named Zayd.
Correction: In this context, 'Zayd' is derived from the root z-y-d meaning 'increase' and refers to the additional existence of accidents over and above bodies.

Key Takeaway
The seven demands are rational refutations that lead to salvation, and the mnemonic poem helps memorize them, each phrase encoding a specific theological negation or affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Types of Possibility: Absolute and Restricted
Bajuri defines 'possible things' and distinguishes two types of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between absolute possibility (imkān) of a thing and the permissibility (jawāz) of God's action regarding it.

Core Idea
A thing's possibility is not the same as God's permission to create it — one is absolute, the other is restricted.

In this passage, Bajuri clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between a thing being possible in itself (its contingency) and the permissibility of God's action of creating or leaving it. This distinction prevents confusion when discussing divine power and will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'possible things'
Read the opening where Bajuri defines possible things as those whose existence and non-existence are valid in themselves, disregarding their relation to God. Note that these include essences and attributes.
2
Grasp absolute possibility (imkān)
Identify the description of absolute possibility: it is unrestricted, meaning the act or omission is valid in itself without considering God. This is the possibility known from attributing the act to the possible thing.
3
Contrast with permissibility (jawāz) in relation to God
Notice that permissibility is ruled with respect to God: each of action and omission is valid from God. This permissibility is restricted because it is tied to God's relation.
4
Examine the linguistic nuance of 'or' (aw)
Bajuri notes that 'or' in the author's phrase can mean a disjunction of two absolute things or can mean 'and'. Consider how this affects interpretation.
This shows the author's careful use of language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فِعْل الْمُمْكِنَاتِ) المراد بالممكنات كما تقدم المقدورات التي يصح وجودها وعدمها في نفسها بقطع النظر عن ارتباطها بالله تعالى، وتلك المقدورات هي الذوات والصفات كذات زيد وبياضه والمحكوم عليه بالجواز في حقه تعالى فعلها أو تركها، أي: إيجادها وإبقاؤها على عدمها والجواز المذكور غير إمكان الفعل والترك المعلوم من إضافتهما إلى الممكنات؛ لأن الإمكان المعلوم من تلك الإضافة مطلق غير منظور فيه إلى الله تعالى، بل المراد به أن كلاً من الفعل والترك يصح ثبوته وعدم ثبوته من غير تقييد الثبوت وعدمه بارتباطهما بالله تعالى بخلاف الجواز المحكوم به عليهما، فالمراد به أن كلاً منهما يصح ثبوته وعدم ثبوته من الله تعالى فالمحكوم به مقيد والمعلوم من الإضافة مطلق، والمقيد غير المطلق، و(أو) في كلام المصنف يحتمل كما تقدم أن تكون لأحد الشيئين المطلق الصادق بكل من الفعل والترك، وأن تكون بمعنى الواو.
His saying: 'The doing of possible things' — by 'possible things' is meant, as has preceded, the things that are power-able [such that] their existence and non-existence are valid in themselves, disregarding their relation to God, exalted is He. And those power-able things are the essences and attributes, like the essence of Zayd and his whiteness. And that which is ruled to be permissible with respect to Him, exalted is He, is their doing or their omission, i.e., bringing them into existence or leaving them in non-existence. And the mentioned permissibility (jawāz) is other than the possibility (imkān) of the doing and the omission known from their attribution to the possible things; because the possibility known from that attribution is absolute, not considered in relation to God, exalted is He. Rather, by it is meant that each of the doing and the omission is valid in its being established and not being established, without restricting being established or not by their relation to God, exalted is He. In contrast to the permissibility ruled concerning them, for by it is meant that each of them is valid in its being established and not being established from God, exalted is He. So the ruled [permissibility] is restricted, and the known from the attribution is absolute, and the restricted is not the absolute. And the 'or' (aw) in the author's speech, as has preceded, can be for one of two absolute things, true of each of the doing and the omission, and it can be in the meaning of 'and' (wāw).
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a terminological clarification. The commentator defines 'possible things' (al-mumkināt) as those things whose existence and non-existence are intrinsically valid, regardless of their relation to God. These include essences and attributes. Then he distinguishes between two types of possibility: (1) the absolute possibility (imkān) that is known from attributing the act or omission to the possible thing itself — this is unrestricted, not considering God. (2) the permissibility (jawāz) that is judged with respect to God — this is restricted, meaning that both the act and its omission are valid from God. The permissibility is narrower because it is related to God's power and will. The commentator also notes that the word 'or' in the author's original phrase can mean either a disjunction that includes both possibilities, or it can be equivalent to 'and'.

Key Terms
ممكنات
جواز
إمكان
فعل
ترك
ذوات
صفات
قيد
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the possibility of a thing (imkān) is the same as God's permissibility to act upon it.
Correction: Imkān is absolute and intrinsic to the thing, while jawāz is restricted by its relation to God's action.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between 'imkān' (absolute possibility) and 'jawāz' (permissibility) in this passage?
A) Imkān is about God, jawāz is about the possible thing
B) Imkān is absolute and unrestricted, while jawāz is restricted to God's relation
C) They are synonyms
D) Imkān is used only for necessary things

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a thing (imkān) is absolute and intrinsic, while the permissibility of God's action (jawāz) is restricted by its relation to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence and non-existence are equally possible in themselves. If God had not permitted your existence, you would be nothing. Are you living today as though every breath is a gift sustained by His permissibility? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
The passage teaches that God's permissibility is restricted—He could have omitted you. Does this truth humble you to the point of surrender? How would your daily choices look if you acted as though His permission were the only reason you are here?
?
You often claim 'I can do this' or 'I can do that,' but your very ability to act depends on God's permissibility. Can you honestly say you acknowledge that in your heart? What one area of your life would you need to realign with this reality?

The Synonymy of Prophet and Messenger
Definition of taraaduf as synonymy between nabi and rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'taraaduf' as applied to the terms nabi and rasul in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Nabi and rasul are synonyms meaning the same: a messenger of Allah.

In Islamic theology, there is a debate whether the terms 'nabi' (prophet) and 'rasul' (messenger) are synonyms or have distinct meanings. This passage presents the view that they are synonymous, defining 'taraaduf' as the two words sharing one meaning, specifically that of a messenger. The commentator cites the scholar al-Shanashuri in support.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Note the key term 'taraaduf' and how it is explained.
The Arabic is a brief gloss on a previous statement.
2
Understand the term 'taraaduf'
The term means 'synonymy'. The author specifies it refers to the synonymy of nabi and rasul with the meaning of rasul.
3
Note the citation of al-Shanashuri
The commentator relies on al-Shanashuri as an authority for this view. This shows the tradition of scholarly reference.
Al-Shanashuri is a known commentator on Umm al-Barahin.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this distinction matters: if they are synonyms, then every prophet is a messenger, and vice versa, simplifying the category of prophethood.
This impacts discussions on the roles and ranks of prophets.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you understood the definition.

The Qiyas Proof for Permissibility of Human Accidents
Al-Bajuri presents and explains the logical proof for the permissibility of human accidents in prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri uses a syllogism (qiyas) to prove that human accidents are possible for prophets, including both logical forms and the resolution of the observation objection via tawatur.

Core Idea
Occurrence implies possibility: the logical bedrock of proving human accidents in prophets.

Al-Bajuri unpacks Sanusi's argument for the permissibility of human accidents in prophets using a formal syllogism. He shows how observation of their occurrence serves as evidence, and clarifies how we, who did not witness the prophets, can rely on tawatur. This lesson walks through the logical structure and the resolution of a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises of the conjunctive syllogism
Read the passage and locate the minor premise (observation of accidents in prophets) and the major premise (everything observed to occur is possible). Understand that the conclusion is that accidents are possible.
The minor premise comes from 'فَمُشَاهَدَةُ وُقُوعِهَا بِهِمْ' and the major from 'وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ كَانَ جَائِزاً'.
2
Recognize the exceptional (conditional) syllogism form
Notice that al-Bajuri also presents the argument as a conditional: if accidents were not possible, they would not have occurred; but they did occur, so they are possible. This is a modus tollens form.
The phrase 'وَيُحْتَمَلُ تَقْرِيرُهُ اسْتِثْنَائِيًّا' introduces this alternative form.
3
Address the objection about observation
Consider the objection: we did not observe the prophets ourselves. Al-Bajuri answers that observation by contemporaries suffices, and for us, it is like observation through tawatur (mass transmission).
The objection is phrased as 'كَيْفَ يَقُولُ الْمُصَنِّفُ: فَمُشَاهَدَةُ وُقُوعِهَا بِهِمْ مَعَ أَنَّا لَمْ نُشَاهِدْ ذَلِكَ؟'
4
Refine understanding with al-Anbabi's gloss
Al-Anbabi clarifies that the major premise's 'like that' refers specifically to observed occurrence in prophets, because even obligatory matters are also observed in them. This prevents misunderstanding.
The gloss: 'الْمُرَادُ كُلُّ مَا شُوهِدَ وُقُوعُهُ بِهِمْ عَلَى وَجْهٍ مَخْصُوصٍ'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فَمُشَاهَدَةُ وُقُوعِهَا بِهِمْ يُؤْخَذُ من ذلك مقدمةً صغرى قائلة: الأعراض البشرية شُوهِدَ وقوعها بهم، ويُضَمُّ إليها مقدّمة كبرى قائلة: وكلُّ ما كان كذلك كان جائزاً؛ لأن الوقوع يستلزم الجواز، ومجموع هاتين المقدمتين قياس اقتراني، ويحتمل تقريره استثنائياً بأن تقول: لو لم تَجُزِ الأعراض البشرية في حقهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام لما وَقَعَتْ بهم، لكن التالي باطل لمشاهدة وقوعها بهم، ولا يخفى أن مشاهَدَةَ ذلك إنما وقعت ممن عاصرهم فاندفع ما قد يقال: كيف يقول المصنف: (فمشاهدة وقوعها بهم مع أنا لم نشاهذ ذلك؟ ويمكن أن يكون المراد بالمشاهدة : ما يشمل المشاهدةَ حُكْماً كبلوغ ذلك لنا بالتواتر. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي: (قوله: وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ كَانَ جَائِزاً) المراد كلُّ ما شُوهِدَ وقوعه بهم على وجه مخصوص، وإلا فالواجبُ شُوهِدَ وقوعه بهم.
His saying: 'So observing their occurrence in them' is taken from that as a minor premise saying: 'Human accidents have been observed to occur in them', and to it is joined a major premise saying: 'And everything that is like that is possible'; because occurrence necessitates possibility. The combination of these two premises is a conjunctive syllogism. It is possible to formulate it as an exceptional syllogism by saying: If human accidents were not possible for them—peace and blessings be upon them—they would not have occurred in them, but the consequent is false due to the observation of their occurrence in them. And it is not hidden that the observation of that only occurred from those who were their contemporaries, thus refuting what might be said: How can the author say 'so observing their occurrence in them' when we have not observed that? It is possible that what is meant by 'observation' includes observation in effect, like that reaching us through tawatur. The gloss of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'And everything that is like that is possible') means everything that has been observed to occur in them in a specific manner; otherwise, the obligatory has been observed to occur in them.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (qiyas) demonstrating that human accidents (a'rad) are possible (ja'iz) for prophets. Al-Bajuri explains Sanusi's argument: the minor premise states that human accidents have been observed to occur in prophets; the major premise states that whatever is observed to occur is possible (because occurrence implies possibility). This forms a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). He then notes it can also be presented as an exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): if accidents were not possible for prophets, they would not have occurred; but they did occur, so the conditional is false. The objection that we do not observe these accidents ourselves is answered: observation by contemporaries suffices, and for us, it is like observation through tawatur (mass transmission). The gloss by Muhammad al-Anbabi clarifies the major premise: the phrase 'like that' refers specifically to observed occurrence in prophets, because even obligatory matters are also observed in them.

قياس استثنائي
جواز
وقوع
مشاهدة
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must personally observe an event to consider it evidence for possibility.
Correction: The passage explains that observation by contemporaries is sufficient, and for later generations, tawatur (mass transmission) serves as observation in effect.

Checkpoint

D) Observation by contemporaries is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of human accidents in prophets is proven through a syllogism where observed occurrence implies possibility, and this observation is valid through tawatur.

The passage tackles an objection about observation. When you encounter a doubt about Islam, do you quickly dismiss it, or do you engage with it rationally? The scholars did not ignore objections; they answered them. What objection about Islam lurks in your heart that you have not honestly addressed?

The Kalima Encompasses All Beliefs and Its Predicate Dispute
Al-Bajuri explains the kalima's comprehensiveness and the predicate dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah' encompasses all necessary beliefs and why scholars debated the nature of its omitted predicate.

Core Idea
The kalima entails all beliefs, but its predicate is disputed between existence and possibility.

Al-Bajuri now turns from listing the required beliefs to the virtue of the word of tawḥīd. He explains that the meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah' encompasses all those beliefs because it entails them. Then he highlights a subtle point: the omitted predicate of 'lā' has two interpretations, affecting whether the kalima affirms God's existence. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing grammar. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is a continuous commentary; note the shift from grammatical explanation to theological dispute.
2
Analyze the grammatical construction
Look at how 'encompasses' (yajmaʿu) is parsed: its subject is the meaning of the kalima, not the wording. The phrase 'all of them' emphasizes the beliefs. Understand why the kalima is said to encompass all beliefs.
The key point: the meaning entails the beliefs, so it's valid to say it encompasses them.
3
Identify the two views on the predicate
Find where al-Bajuri states that scholars agree the predicate is omitted, but differ on whether it is estimated from existence (wujūd) or possibility (imkān). Which view is preferred?
The preferred view is from possibility.
4
Understand the objection
Read the objection to the preferred view: if the predicate is 'possible', then the kalima only denies the possibility of other gods, but does not affirm Allah's existence. Think about why this is a problem.
This objection shows the importance of the predicate choice.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: does the kalima itself prove God's existence? According to the preferred view, not directly. How might this affect how we use the kalima in daʿwah or theology?
This connects the lesson to broader theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَيَجْمَعُ مَعَانِي هَذِهِ الْعَقَائِدِ... إلخ) لما أنهى الكلام على ما يجب على المكلف معرفتُهُ؛ تَمَّمَ الفائدة ببيان فضل الكلمة المشرفة التي هي كلمة التوحيد، فقال: (ويجمع معاني هذه العقائد... إلخ)، وإضافة (معاني) لما بعده للبيان، أي: عقيدة، بمعنى: مُعْتَقَدَةٍ، فعيلة بمعنى مفتعلة. جمع (وقوله: كُلّها إما بالنصب على أنه توكيد لـ (معاني)، وإما بالجرّ على أنه توكيد لـ هذه العقائد، معاني هي (وقوله: قَوْلُ: لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا الله ... إلخ) فاعل لقوله : (يجمع)، لكن على تقدير مضاف، أي: معنى قول لا إله إلا الله... إلخ؛ لأن الجامع لما ذُكِرَ إنما هو المعنى لا اللفظ، فالقول بمعنى: المَقُولِ، وإضافته لما بعده للبيان، أي: مقول هو لا إله إلا الله... إلخ، ووجهُ جَمْع معنى ذلك لمعاني هذه العقائد: أنه يستلزمها كما سيوضّحه المصنف، والمستلزم للوازم متعددةٍ يصحُ وَصْفُه بجمعه لها. واعلم أنه لم يختلف في أن خبر (لا) في الكلمة المشرفة محذوف، وإنما اختلف هل يُقَدَّرُ من مادة الوجود أو من مادة الإمكان؟ والمختار الثاني، لكن استشكل : بأنه لا يُستفاد من الكلمة المشرفة حينئذٍ ثبوتُ الوجود له تعالى؛ لأنه يَصِيرُ المعنى: لا إله ممكن إلا الله، فإنه ممكن ، وهل هو موجود؟ لا يُستفاد ذلك.
His saying: 'And it encompasses the meanings of these beliefs... etc.' — When he finished the discussion of what the obligated person must know, he completed the benefit by explaining the virtue of the noble word which is the word of tawḥīd. So he said: 'And it encompasses the meanings of these beliefs... etc.' And the annexation of 'meanings' to what follows is for clarification, i.e., beliefs (ʿaqīda), meaning that which is believed, the form faʿīla meaning mufʿala. The plural. (And his saying: 'all of them' — either in the accusative as an emphasis of 'meanings', or in the genitive as an emphasis of 'these beliefs', they are meanings. And his saying: 'the saying: Lā ilāha illa Allāh... etc.' is the subject of his saying 'encompasses', but with an implied genitive construction, i.e., the meaning of the saying Lā ilāha illa Allāh... etc. For what encompasses what was mentioned is only the meaning, not the wording. So 'saying' is in the sense of 'what is said', and its annexation to what follows is for clarification, i.e., what is said is Lā ilāha illa Allāh... etc. And the way the meaning of that encompasses the meanings of these beliefs is that it entails them, as the author will make clear. And that which entails multiple entailments is correctly described as encompassing them. And know that there is no disagreement that the predicate of 'lā' in the noble word is omitted; rather, they disagree whether it is estimated from the root of existence (wujūd) or from the root of possibility (imkān). And the preferred view is the second. But an objection is raised: that in that case the affirmation of existence for Him, exalted, is not derived from the noble word, because the meaning becomes: 'There is no possible god except Allah', for He is possible. And is He existent? That is not derived.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is al-Bajuri's commentary on Sanusi's discussion of the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah'. It begins by explaining that the author, having finished the required beliefs, now shows the virtue of the word of tawḥīd. The phrase 'it encompasses the meanings of these beliefs' is analyzed grammatically: 'meanings' (maʿānī) is annexed to 'these beliefs' for clarification, and 'all of them' (kulluhā) can be either accusative or genitive as an emphasis. The subject of 'encompasses' is 'the saying: Lā ilāha illa Allāh', but with an implied word 'meaning' because it is the meaning, not the wording, that encompasses the beliefs. The kalima entails all the necessary beliefs, and what entails multiple things is validly described as encompassing them. Then al-Bajuri moves to a theological-linguistic point: scholars agree that the predicate (khabar) of 'lā' in the kalima is omitted, but they differ on whether it is estimated from the root of existence (wujūd) or from the root of possibility (imkān). The preferred view (al-mukhtār) is the second (i.e., from imkān). However, an objection is raised: if the predicate is 'possible', then the kalima only says 'there is no possible god except Allah', which affirms that other possible gods do not exist, but it does not explicitly affirm the existence of Allah; it only says He is possible. Thus, the objection claims that the kalima does not prove God's existence. This objection is left open in the passage, though it is implied that a response exists.

Key Terms
الكلمة المشرفة
خبر لا
مادة الوجود
مادة الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah' directly proves God's existence.
Correction: According to the preferred view, the predicate is estimated from possibility, so the kalima only denies other possible gods, not affirming existence. Existence must be proven by other arguments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred view (al-mukhtār) regarding the predicate of 'lā' in 'La ilaha illa Allah'?
A) It is estimated from the root of existence (māddat al-wujūd).
B) It is estimated from the root of possibility (māddat al-imkān).
C) The predicate is explicitly mentioned.
D) There is no difference of opinion.

Key Takeaway
The kalima encompasses all beliefs by entailing them, and the preferred view estimates its predicate from possibility, leaving existence to be proven by other arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The preferred view in this lesson is that the kalima says God is 'possible', not 'existent'. Does that disturb you? Have you ever reflected on the logical foundation of your belief in God, or have you simply assumed His existence without proof?
?
If the kalima does not explicitly affirm God's existence, how have you been affirming His existence in your heart? Are you relying on proofs outside the kalima? If not, what is the state of your certainty?

The Three Opinions on the Exception in 'La Ilaha Illa Allah'
Bajuri explains the three scholarly opinions on the type of exception in the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on whether the exception in the testimony of faith is continuous, discontinuous, or neither.

Core Idea
The logical type of exception in the shahada affects its theological meaning.

The shahada, 'There is no god but Allah,' contains an exception. Scholars debate whether this exception is continuous (muttasil), connecting the negation directly to the affirmation, or discontinuous (munqati'), or something else entirely. This lesson presents the three classical opinions and their logical underpinnings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Note the key terms: istithna', muttasil, munqati', mustathna minhu.
The passage begins by clarifying the purpose of the shahada before listing the opinions.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage lists three opinions. Identify each one and the reasoning given for it.
The first opinion is called 'well-known' (mashhur).
3
Understand the reasoning
For each opinion, understand why the scholars consider the exception continuous, discontinuous, or neither. Focus on how the term 'ilah' is understood.
The key is whether 'ilah' includes both the true God and false gods.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this logical distinction matters for the meaning of the shahada. The passage says the purpose is to negate the possibility of other deities, not to affirm God's existence.
This protects against misunderstanding the shahada as merely affirming God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأن القصد من الجملة إنما هو نَفْي إمكان غيره، لا إثبات الوجود له تعالى؛ لأن وجوده تعالى مسلم الثبوت والمشهور أن الاستثناء مُتَّصِل؛ لأن المستثنى منه كُلّي يشمل المستثنى وغيره. وقيل: إنه منقطع؛ لأنه يجب على المتكلم بهذه الكلمة أن يلاحظ أن النفي متوجّة على ما عداه تعالى، وحينئذ فالمستثنى منه غيرُ شامل للمستثنى. وقيل: إنه لا متصل ولا منقطع، فالخلاف في ذلك على أقوال ثلاثة.
And it is answered that the intent of the sentence is only to negate the possibility of other than Him, not to affirm existence for Him, may He be exalted, because His existence, may He be exalted, is conceded as established. And the well-known (view) is that the exception is continuous (muttasil), because the thing from which the exception is made (mustathna minhu) is a universal that includes the excepted and other than it. And it is said: it is discontinuous (munqati'), because the speaker of this word must observe that the negation is directed at what is other than Him, may He be exalted, and then the thing from which the exception is made does not include the excepted. And it is said: it is neither continuous nor discontinuous. So the disagreement on that is three opinions.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents a discussion about the type of exception (istithna') in the phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah'. It begins by answering a previous objection: the purpose of the sentence is to negate the possibility of any other deity, not to affirm God's existence, since His existence is already conceded. The text then lists three opinions about the exception. The first opinion holds that the exception is continuous (muttasil), meaning that the term 'ilah' is a universal that includes both the true God and false gods, and the exception removes the false gods. The second opinion considers it discontinuous (munqati'), because the negation is focused on everything other than God, so 'ilah' here does not include God. The third opinion denies both classifications. The passage concludes that there are three opinions on this matter.

مستثنى منه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exception type in the shahada is irrelevant to its meaning.
Correction: The type of exception affects how negation and affirmation relate, impacting the theological interpretation of monotheism.

B) Because the term 'ilah' is a universal that includes both the true God and false gods
C) Because the exception is made from a specific individual
D) Because the exception is only verbal

Key Takeaway
The exception in the shahada is analyzed in three ways—continuous, discontinuous, or neither—each reflecting a different logical interpretation of the phrase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your life: is there any 'god' you have excluded from the negation, such as your own ego or wealth? What would it take to include it in the general category and then truly negate it? Why Explicit Negation is Better: Objection and Answer
Bajuri presents an objection about the predicate from possibility and answers that explicit negation is better.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical reasoning behind choosing an explicit predicate over an implicit one derived from possibility in the Shahada.

Core Idea
Explicit speech is superior when addressing those who miss allusions.

In the study of 'La ilaha illa Allah', scholars debate whether the implicit predicate should be derived from the concept of possibility (imkan). An objection is raised that such a derivation provides a demonstration refuting polytheists. However, the answer prioritizes clarity: explicit negation is better because the audience is stubborn and requires direct language. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim carefully: estimating the predicate from imkan provides a demonstrative refutation, so it is better.
Notice the use of 'burhani' (demonstration).
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that if the predicate is from possibility, the statement logically refutes those who claim necessary existence of other gods. This is a strong rational proof.
3
Read the answer
The author counters by saying explicit address is better, because opponents are extremely dull and do not understand allusions.
Observe the strong language: 'ghayat min al-balada' (utmost stupidity).
4
Reflect on the pedagogical principle
Consider why explicit speech is more effective: it leaves no room for misinterpretation and directly confronts the falsehood.
This connects to broader kalam methodology of audience consideration.
5
Apply the principle
Think of a modern scenario where direct speech is better than indirect reasoning when dealing with a stubborn audience.
Optional: write a short reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت: تقدير الخبر من مادة الإمكان فيه الردُّ على من ادَّعى وجود غيره لزوماً، فهو مُسْتَفَادٌ من الجملة بطريق برهاني، فهو أولى. قلت الأَوْلى مُخَاطَبَةُ الخصوم في هذا المقام بالصريح للإشارة إلى أنهم في غاية من البلادة لا يفهمون بالكناية ونحوها.
If you say: Estimating the predicate from the material of possibility (imkan) contains a refutation of those who claim the existence of other than Him by necessity, so it is derived from the sentence by a demonstration (burhani), so it is better. I say: What is better is addressing the opponents in this context explicitly to indicate that they are in the utmost stupidity, not understanding by allusion (kinaya) and the like.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that if the implicit predicate of 'La ilaha illa Allah' is estimated to be from the root of possibility (imkan), i.e., 'There is no possible deity except Allah,' then the sentence logically refutes those who claim that other gods necessarily exist. This derivation is by way of demonstration, so it is better. The answer is that it is better to address the opponents explicitly, because they are extremely dull and do not understand allusions and indirect speech. Thus, the explicit negation of any other god is more effective.

Key Terms
تقدير الخبر
الإمكان
بطريق برهاني
الكناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an implicit predicate derived from possibility is the most logically sound way to phrase the Shahada.
Correction: The answer shows that explicit negation is better because the audience (the opponents) lacks the subtlety to understand implicit logical arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the answer given to the objection that the predicate from possibility (imkan) is better?
A) It is indeed better
B) Explicit address is better because the opponents are dull and do not understand allusions
C) The predicate should be from existence
D) The objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
When addressing stubborn opponents, explicit speech is more effective than indirect logical inference.

Grammatical Analysis of 'La Mustaghniya' in the Shahada
Bajuri discusses declension of 'la mustaghniya' and related phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different grammatical treatments of 'mustaghniya' in the shahada and the implications of the Baghdad school's rules.

Core Idea
The grammatical case of 'mustaghniya' is flexible, reflecting different interpretive traditions.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary examines a specific grammatical point in the shahada: the declension of 'la mustaghniya'. It shows how different ways of understanding the phrase's syntactic structure lead to different case endings, and how the Baghdad school treats such phrases. Let's follow along as Bajuri unpacks these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key grammatical issue
The passage discusses the phrase 'la mustaghniya' and its declension. Notice the mention of two alternatives: with tanwin and without.
The phrase 'shibh al-mudaf' is central.
2
Understand the Baghdad school's rule
The text says some follow the Baghdad school that treats shibh al-mudaf like a simple noun, leaving off tanwin. Recognize this as a grammatical principle.
3
Explore the alternative interpretation
Another possibility is that the genitive phrase is not directly attached, so 'mustaghniya' is not shibh al-mudaf. See how this changes the analysis.
This involves a deleted predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لَا مُسْتَغْنِيَ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ... إلخ) هكذا في كثير من النسخ بفتح الياء من غير تنوين، وفيه أن ذلك شبية بالمضاف فحقه النصب مع التنوين كما في بعض النسخ، إلا أن يقال: إنه جارٍ على طريقة البغداديين الذين يُجرون الشبية بالمضاف مَجْرَى المفرد في ترك تنوينه أو يقال: إن قوله عن كل ما سواه ليس متعلّقاً بذلك حتى يكون شبيهاً بالمضاف بل متعلّق بمحذوف، والتقدير: لا مستغني يستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ. (قوله: وَمُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَيْهِ... إلخ) بالرفع أو بالنصب، لا البناء لعدم تكرار (لا)، فهو على حد قولهم: لا رَجُلَ في الدار وامرأة بخلاف ما إذا تكرّرَتْ كما في: لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم. (قوله: كُلُّ مَا عَدَاهُ) عَدَلَ عن كلّ ما سواه - مع اتحاد المعنى - لمجرد التفنن، وقد تقدم تفسيره (قوله: أَمَّا اسْتِغْنَاؤُهُ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ... إلخ) لما ذَكَرَ أن معنى الألوهية التي انفرد بها المولى استغناؤه عن كل ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما عداه
(His saying: 'There is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc.') — thus in many copies with fatha on the ya and without tanwin, and it is that it resembles an annexed noun (shibh al-mudaf), so its right is nasb with tanwin as in some copies, except that it is said: it follows the way of the Baghdadis, who treat what resembles an annexed noun like the simple noun in leaving off its tanwin, or it is said: that his saying 'from all other than Him' is not related to it so that it becomes like an annexed noun, but rather it is related to a deleted (phrase), and the estimation is: 'there is no self-sufficient one who is self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc.' (His saying: 'And in need of Him... etc.') — in the nominative or accusative, not built (mabni) because of the non-repetition of 'la', so it is like their saying: 'There is no man in the house and a woman' contrary to when it is repeated as in: 'There is no power and no strength except by Allah, the High, the Great.' (His saying: 'all other than him') — he turned from 'all other than Him' — with unity of meaning — merely for stylistic variation, and its explanation has preceded. (His saying: 'As for His self-sufficiency, He is mighty and majestic, from all other than Him... etc.') — because he mentioned that the meaning of uluhiyya which the Lord alone possesses is His self-sufficiency from all other than Him and the dependence of all other than Him.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical analysis of the phrase 'la mustaghniya' (there is no self-sufficient) in the interpretation of the shahada. It notes that in many copies, it is read with fatha on the ya without tanwin, treating 'mustaghniya' as resembling an annexed noun (shibh al-mudaf) because of the following genitive phrase 'min kulli ma siwah'. The proper rule would be nasb with tanwin, but some follow the Baghdad school who treat it as a simple noun without tanwin. Another possibility is that the phrase 'an kulli ma siwah' is not directly attached but is related to a deleted predicate, so the estimation is: 'there is no self-sufficient one who is self-sufficient from all other than Him.' Then the text discusses 'wa muftaqirun ilayhi' (and in need of Him), saying it can be nominative or accusative, but not built (mabni) because 'la' is not repeated before it. It cites the example 'la rajula fi al-dari wa imra'a' where the second noun is not built. The author also notes the stylistic variation between 'kullu ma siwah' and 'kullu ma 'adahu' with the same meaning. Finally, the text returns to the meaning of uluhiyya as self-sufficiency and dependence.

Key Terms
لا النافية للجنس
شبية بالمضاف
البغداديون
التنوين
المفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the case ending of 'mustaghniya' is fixed and there is only one correct reading.
Correction: The passage shows that there are valid alternative readings based on different grammatical schools and syntactic analyses. Both the reading with tanwin and without tanwin (following the Baghdadis) are acceptable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why might 'mustaghniya' be written without tanwin in some copies?
A) Because it is a proper noun
B) Following the Baghdad school who treat it as a simple noun
C) Because it is diptote
D) Due to a copying error

Key Takeaway
The case ending of 'mustaghniya' is not fixed; it depends on grammatical school and syntactic analysis, with valid alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Grammatical Analysis of the Shahada Wording
Three grammatical annotations on the Shahada wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical rationale behind the varying readings of the Shahada wording and how different Arabic grammatical schools influence these choices.

Core Idea
The Shahada's precise wording reflects subtle grammatical rules and school-specific conventions.

Al-Bajuri provides three grammatical clarifications on the Shahada. The first concerns the reading of 'lā mustaghniya', the second the case of 'wa-muftaqirun', and the third the synonymous variation between 'siwāhu' and 'adāhu'. This lesson unpacks these annotations to reveal the reasoning behind each grammatical choice.

Guided Path
1
Examine the issue with 'lā mustaghniya'
Notice that the common reading has no tanwīn, but the phrase resembles a genitive construct (shabīh bi-al-muḍāf), which normally requires tanwīn. Understand why this tension exists.
Al-Bajuri offers two justifications for the reading without tanwīn.
2
Evaluate the two justifications
First, the Baghdad school treats shabīh bi-al-muḍāf like a simple noun. Second, the attached phrase may not be directly attached, so it is not truly shabīh. Contrast these explanations.
3
Analyze the case of 'wa-muftaqirun'
Al-Bajuri says it can be in raf‘ or naṣb, but not binā', because 'lā' is not repeated. Compare with examples like 'lā rajula fī al-dāri wa-imra'atu'.
The repetition of 'lā' would require binā' on the second noun.
4
Appreciate the stylistic variation
The shift from 'siwāhu' to 'adāhu' is purely stylistic with no change in meaning. Note that this is a common rhetorical device.
This point is straightforward and reinforces the unity of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: لَا مُسْتَغْنِيَ عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سِوَاهُ... إلخ) هكذا في كثير من النسخ بفتح الياء من غير تنوين، وفيه أن ذلك شبيه بالمضاف فحقه النصب مع التنوين كما في بعض النسخ، إلا أن يقال: إنه جارٍ على طريقة البغداديين الذين يُجرون الشبية بالمضاف مجرى المفرد في ترك تنوينه أو يقال: إن قوله عن كل ما سواه ليس متعلّقاً بذلك حتى يكون شبيهاً بالمضاف بل متعلّق بمحذوف، والتقدير: لا مستغني يستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ. (قوله: وَمُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَيْهِ... إلخ) بالرفع أو بالنصب، لا البناء لعدم تكرار (لا)، فهو على حد قولهم: لا رَجُلَ في الدار وامرأة بخلاف ما إذا تكرّرَتْ كما في: لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم. (قوله: كُلُّ مَا عَدَاهُ) عَدَلَ عن كلّ ما سواه - مع اتحاد المعنى - لمجرد التفنن، وقد تقدم تفسيره
(His saying: 'Lā mustaghniya 'an kulli mā siwāhu... etc.') Thus in many copies with fatḥa on the yā' and without tanwīn, and there is (the issue) that it is similar to a genitive construct (shabīh bi-al-muḍāf) so it deserves naṣb with tanwīn as in some copies, except that it is said: it is following the method of the Baghdadis who treat the shabīh bi-al-muḍāf like the simple noun in leaving off its tanwīn, or it is said: that his saying 'an kulli mā siwāhu is not attached to it in order for it to be shabīh bi-al-muḍāf, but rather it is attached to something deleted, and the estimation is: lā mustaghniya yastaghni 'an kulli mā siwāhu... etc. (His saying: 'Wa-muftaqirun ilayhi... etc.') with raf' or naṣb, not binā' because lā is not repeated, so it is like their saying: 'lā rajula fī al-dāri wa-imra'atu' contrary to when it is repeated as in 'lā ḥawla wa-lā quwwata illā bi-Allāh al-'aliyy al-'aẓīm'. (His saying: 'Kullu mā 'adāhu') he turned from 'kullu mā siwāhu' - while the meaning is identical - for mere stylistic variation, and its explanation has preceded.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage provides three successive grammatical annotations on the wording of the Shahada. First, on 'la mustaghniya', al-Bajuri notes that most copies read it with a fatḥa on the yā' and without tanwīn, but since it resembles a genitive construct (shabīh bi-al-muḍāf), it should theoretically take naṣb with tanwīn. However, two justifications are given: either it follows the Baghdad school which treats such phrases as simple nouns, or the phrase 'an kulli mā siwāhu' is not directly attached, so it is not truly shabīh bi-al-muḍāf. Second, on 'wa-muftaqirun ilayhi', he explains that it can be in raf' or naṣb, but not binā', because 'la' is not repeated; this is analogous to 'la rajula fī al-dāri wa-imra'atu' where the second noun takes raf' or naṣb. Third, on 'kullu mā 'adāhu', he notes that it is simply a stylistic variation of 'kullu mā siwāhu' with the same meaning.

Key Terms
لا مستغني
ومفتقر
كل ما عداه
شبيه بالمضاف
التنوين
طريقة البغداديين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different readings of the Shahada (e.g., with or without tanwīn) are random or due to scribal error.
Correction: Al-Bajuri shows that each reading has a valid grammatical justification, following either a school of grammar (Baghdadis) or a different parsing of the sentence structure.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, why can 'wa-muftaqirun ilayhi' not be built (binā')?
A) Because it is indefinite.
B) Because 'la' is not repeated.
C) Because it is similar to a genitive construct.
D) Because it is a proper noun.

Key Takeaway
The seemingly subtle variations in the Shahada wording are upheld by distinct grammatical principles from different schools, demonstrating the depth and precision of classical Arabic grammar.

Division of Gharad into Two Impossible Kinds
Sanusi divides gharad into two kinds and states both are impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God acts without any purpose that implies benefit or need, as both kinds of purpose (benefit to Himself or to creation) are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
God has no purpose that benefits Himself or others.

Sanusi now addresses the concept of 'gharad' (purpose) in divine actions. He divides it into two types and declares both impossible. This passage refines our understanding of God's transcendence and self-sufficiency, building on earlier principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two kinds of gharad
Find the two kinds: benefit returning to God, and benefit returning to creation. Understand why both are impossible.
3
Ask: why does this matter?
Ask yourself why these two categories are both impossible. The commentary will help you connect to earlier teachings on God's transcendence and the non-obligation upon Him.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيانُ ذلك أن الغرض على قسمين أحدهما مصلحة تعود عليه تعالى، والآخرُ مصلحةً تعود على خلقه، وكلاهما محال. وقد تقدم إبطال الأول في قوله: ويؤخذ منه أيضاً: تنزهه تعالى عن الأغراض... إلخ)، والقرينة على أنه أراد خصوص الأغراض العائدة عليه تعالى: الإضافة إلى الضمير في قوله: (إلى ما يحصل غرضه، وكذا أشار إلى إبطال الثاني بقوله: (ويؤخذ منه أيضاً: أنه لا يجب... إلخ) على ما صرح به المصنف في شرحه
And the explanation of that is that the purpose (al-gharad) is of two kinds: one is a benefit (maslaha) returning to Him, exalted, and the other is a benefit returning to His creation, and both are impossible. The refutation of the first has already been mentioned in His statement: 'It is also derived from it: His transcendence, exalted, from purposes... etc.' And the indication that He intended specifically the purposes returning to Him, exalted, is the addition to the pronoun in His statement: 'to that which fulfills His purpose.' Likewise, He indicated the refutation of the second with His statement: 'And from it is also derived: that it is not obligatory upon Him... etc.' as the author stated in his commentary.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the concept of 'gharad' (purpose or objective) in relation to God. It begins by stating that gharad is of two kinds: (1) a benefit that returns to God Himself, and (2) a benefit that returns to His creation. Both are impossible (muhal) for God. The text then refers back to earlier statements: the first kind is refuted by the principle that God is transcendent (tanzih) above any purposes, as indicated by the phrase 'to that which fulfills His purpose' (the pronoun refers to God). The second kind is refuted by the derived principle that nothing is obligatory upon God, as expressed in 'and that it is not obligatory upon Him...' The commentator notes that the author (Sanusi) clarified this in his own commentary. The passage thus establishes a key theological point: God acts without any purpose that benefits Himself or others in the sense of necessity, preserving His absolute independence and self-sufficiency.

Key Terms
غرض
مصلحة
محال
تنزه
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God acts for a purpose (gharad) like humans do, to achieve some benefit.
Correction: Sanusi shows that gharad is divided into two types, both of which are impossible for God. God acts without any such purpose.

Checkpoint

A) One kind, and it is possible.
B) Two kinds, both possible.
C) Two kinds, both impossible.
D) Three kinds, two possible and one impossible.

Key Takeaway
God is transcendent above any purpose that implies benefit, whether to Himself or to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Resolving the Thawab Objection
Objection about thawab and its resolution via distinction between reward and rewarding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection regarding thawab is resolved by distinguishing between reward (thawab) and the act of rewarding (ithaba), and how this clarifies the concept of purpose (gharad) and the distinction between rational and legal obligation.

Core Idea
Reward is not the act of rewarding; distinguishing them resolves the theological puzzle.

Sanusi's mention of thawab raised an objection: where is the purpose and the thing from which it is sought? The answer hinges on a crucial distinction between thawab as the reward itself and ithaba as God's act of rewarding. This distinction not only answers the objection but also reinforces the difference between what is rationally necessary and what is legally obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the logical demand: the definition of gharad requires two things—the purpose and that from which the purpose is sought. The text only mentions thawab, so the objection asks where the purpose and the source are.
Notice that the objection is based on a precise definition of gharad.
2
Examine the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes thawab (reward as recompense) from ithaba (the act of rewarding). Thawab is the purpose, and ithaba is the action from which the purpose is sought, thus fulfilling the two requirements.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
If thawab is interpreted as ithaba, then it can be the purpose, and the obedience that brings reward becomes the source. This also works.
Both interpretations show the flexibility of the term.
4
Understand the obligation clarification
The passage concludes that rational obligation is negated, but legal obligation is affirmed. Reward is rationally permissible but legally obligatory due to divine promise.
This is a key distinction in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى فَرْضِ أن قصده ذلك؛ يكون الكلامُ مُشْكِلاً؛ لأن الغَرَضَ - كما تقدم - : هو المصلحة المترتبة على الفعل أو الحكم من حيث كونها مقصودةً منه، وحينئذ فلا بد من شيئين: الغَرَضُ، وما قُصِدَ منه ذلك الغرضُ، ولم يَذْكُرِ المصنف إلا الثواب فيُسأل ويقال: أين الغرضُ؟ وما قصد منه ذلك الغرض؟ : وأجيب بأن المراد من الثواب مقدار من الجزاء، وهو غيرُ الفعل الذي هو تعلق القدرة به المسمّى بـ: الإثابة، فالغَرَضُ هو الأول، وما قُصِدَ منه ذلك الغرضُ هو الثاني، وعلى تقدير أن يُراد بالثواب الإثابةُ؛ فلا مانع من كونه غَرَضاً مقصوداً تعالى الطاعة التي ترتب عليها الثوابُ؛ إذ لا يمتنع ترتُبُ فعل من الفعل، وهو خَلْقُه تعالى ذلك كله؛ فهو غيرُ مناسب لظاهر صنيع المتن كما علمت، على فعلٍ آخر، ومع والمنفى إنما هو الوجوب المستفاد من العقل أخذاً من قوله: (إذ لو وجب... إلخ)، وأما الوجوبُ المستفاد من الشرع؛ فهو ثابت لا منفي، فالثوابُ مثلاً جائز في حقه تعالى عقلاً، لكنه واجب شرعاً؛ لأنه قد وَرَدَ الوعد به في الكتاب والسنة.
And on the assumption that He intended that, the speech becomes problematic; because the purpose (al-gharad) - as previously mentioned - is the benefit (maslaha) resulting from the action or judgment from the aspect of being intended from it. In that case, two things are necessary: the purpose, and that from which that purpose is intended. The author mentioned only the reward (al-thawab), so it is asked: 'Where is the purpose? And what is that from which that purpose is intended?' And it was answered: that what is meant by thawab is a measure of recompense, and it is other than the action which is the attachment of power to it, called 'ithaba' (the act of rewarding). So the purpose is the first, and that from which that purpose is intended is the second. And on the supposition that by thawab is meant ithaba, then there is no hindrance to it being a purpose intended by God, the obedience that results in reward; because it is not impossible for one action to follow from another action, and that is His creation of all that. So it is not appropriate for the apparent structure of the text, as you know, with respect to another action. And what is negated is only the obligation derived from reason, taken from His statement: 'For if it were obligatory... etc.' As for the obligation derived from the Law (shar'), it is established, not negated. So reward, for example, is rationally permissible in His right, exalted, but legally obligatory because the promise has come in the Book and the Sunnah.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the mention of thawab (reward) in the earlier text. The objection is raised on the assumption that the author intended to discuss the second kind of gharad (purpose benefiting creation). The problem is: the definition of gharad requires two things—the purpose itself and the thing from which the purpose is sought. The text only mentions thawab, so where is the purpose and what is the thing from which it is sought? The answer distinguishes between thawab (reward) as the recompense and ithaba (the act of rewarding) as the action of God that attaches power to the reward. Thus the purpose is the reward itself, and the action from which it is sought is the act of rewarding. Alternatively, if thawab is interpreted as ithaba, then it can be the purpose, and the obedience that brings reward is the thing from which the purpose is sought. The passage then clarifies that what is negated is only rational (aqli) obligation, not legal (shar'i) obligation. Reward is rationally permissible for God but legally obligatory due to His promise in revelation.

إثابة
غرض
وجوب عقلي
وجوب شرعي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if reward is obligatory by reason, then it is necessary for God to reward.
Correction: The passage clarifies that reward is rationally permissible (God is not compelled by reason), but legally obligatory due to His promise.

Checkpoint
How does the answer resolve the objection about thawab?
A) It says thawab is the purpose and ithaba is the action from which it is sought.
B) It says thawab is both the purpose and the action.
C) It says the text is unclear and cannot be resolved.
D) It says thawab is irrelevant to the discussion of gharad.

Key Takeaway
The resolution of the thawab objection hinges on distinguishing reward from the act of rewarding, and on separating rational obligation from legal obligation.

The Secret of His Revelation: Grammatical Analysis
Al-Bajuri explains the phrase 'upon the secret of His revelation'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Bajuri's two grammatical interpretations of the phrase 'the secret of His revelation' and the precise meaning of 'revelation' in this context.

Core Idea
A single phrase can carry two valid grammatical meanings in theological texts.

Al-Bajuri, in his commentary on Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin, pauses on the phrase 'upon the secret of His revelation' to clarify its precise meaning. He offers two possible grammatical analyses and specifies what 'revelation' refers to here. This short gloss demonstrates the importance of linguistic precision in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic phrase and its English translation. Notice the two grammatical possibilities Al-Bajuri presents.
The phrase is 'عَلَى سِرَ وَحْيِهِ'.
2
Understand the two grammatical analyses
Al-Bajuri says it could be an attributive genitive (إضافة الصفة للموصوف) meaning 'the secret revelation', or an explicative genitive (إضافة للبيان) meaning 'a secret that is His revelation'. Reflect on the difference.
The first treats 'secret' as an adjective, the second as an appositive.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'revelation'
Note that Al-Bajuri specifies that 'revelation' here refers to the content revealed, i.e., the legal rulings brought by the messengers.
This avoids confusion with the act of revelation or the angel.
4
Consider why this matters
Understanding these linguistic nuances is crucial in kalam because a small change in interpretation can affect theological conclusions.
Precision in language preserves doctrinal accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: عَلَى سِرَ وَحْيِهِ) أي: على وَحْيه السرّ، فهو من إضافة الصفة للموصوف، ويحتمل أن الإضافة للبيان، أي: على سِرّ هو وحيه، والمراد بالوحي هنا: المُوحَى به، وهو الأحكام التي جاءَتْ بها الرسل.
His saying: "Upon the secret of His revelation" i.e., upon His secret revelation; it is a genitive construction of the adjective to the described (iḍāfat al-ṣifa ilā l-mawṣūf). And it is possible that the genitive is for explanation, i.e., upon a secret that is His revelation. And what is meant by "revelation" (al-waḥy) here is the revealed thing (al-mūḥā bihi), i.e., the rulings that the messengers brought.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on Sanusi's phrase "upon the secret of His revelation". Al-Bajuri explains two possible grammatical analyses: either it is an attributive genitive (iḍāfat al-ṣifa li-l-mawṣūf) meaning "the secret revelation", or it is an explicative genitive meaning "a secret which is His revelation". Then he clarifies that "revelation" here refers to the content of revelation, i.e., the legal rulings conveyed by the messengers.

The Meaning of 'al-Shar'' in the Shahada: Law or Lawgiver?
Al-Bajuri discusses the meaning of 'al-Shar'' in the context of the Shahada being legislated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced interpretation of the term 'al-shar'' when it is said that 'the Law made the Shahada the test of faith,' and how it can refer to the Lawgiver rather than the law itself.

Core Idea
The Law cannot make anything; only the Lawgiver can — so 'al-Shar'' must mean the Lawgiver.

One of the subtle points in kalam is the precise meaning of key terms. When the matn says 'the Law (al-shar') made the Shahada,' a student might wonder how the law itself can perform an act. This passage addresses that puzzle by exploring the linguistic and theological possibilities. Al-Bajuri presents a detailed analysis that distinguishes between the literal and metaphorical uses of the term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the problem-answer structure.
2
Identify the problem
The phrase 'the Law made it' seems problematic because the law (like shari'ah) is a set of rulings, which cannot act as a maker. Why does the commentator raise this issue?
3
Understand the two answers
The passage offers two ways to resolve the problem: (1) assume an omitted genitive, i.e., 'the possessor of the Law,' or (2) interpret 'al-shar'' as meaning 'al-Shari'' (the Lawgiver). Note that in the second answer, the Lawgiver is literal for Allah and metaphorical for the Prophet.
4
Appreciate the refinement
The older scholars' view that 'al-Shari'' is literal for Allah is correct only if we mean the establisher of the law. But linguistically, 'al-Shari'' can also mean the explainer, in which case it is literal for the Prophet. This distinction shows the depth of kalam terminology.
This refinement is endorsed by the commentator.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The commentator concludes that this detailed view is the truth. Think about why such precision matters in theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: جعلها الشرع فيه: أن الشرع كالشريعة بمعنى: الأحكام الشرعية، وليست بجاعلة. ويجاب بأنه على تقدير مضاف، أي: جعلها صاحب الشرع، أو أن المراد بالشرع: الشارع، وهو الله حقيقةً، والنبي مجازاً. هذا ما قاله الأشياخ قديماً وحديثاً، وهو صحيح بالنظر لكون الشارع بمعنى: المثبت للشرع والموجد له، وأما بالنظر لكون معناه: المبين والمبلغ - وهو ما يؤخذ من كتب اللغة وغيرها؛ فهو حقيقة في النبي، وهذا التفصيل هو الحق إن شاء الله تعالى.
His statement: 'the Law (al-shar') made it' — therein is [the issue] that al-shar' is like al-shari'ah meaning the legal rulings, and it (the law) is not a maker (ja'ila). And it is answered that it is on the assumption of an omitted genitive construct, i.e., 'the possessor of the Law made it', or that what is meant by al-shar' is al-Shari' (the Lawgiver), which is Allah literally, and the Prophet metaphorically. This is what the shaykhs have said anciently and recently, and it is correct considering that al-Shari' means the establisher and originator of the Law. As for considering its meaning as the explainer and conveyer — which is what is taken from the books of language and others — then it is literal for the Prophet. And this detailed view is the truth, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle theological-linguistic point. The matn states that 'the Law (al-shar') made the Shahada the test of faith.' The commentator raises a problem: the word 'shar'' (law) is like 'shari'ah' (divine law) and refers to legal rulings; the law itself cannot be an agent that 'makes' something. The answer is that there is an implied construct: 'the possessor of the Law' (i.e., the Lawgiver). Alternatively, 'al-shar'' can mean 'al-Shari'' (the Lawgiver) — that is, Allah in a literal sense, and the Prophet in a metaphorical sense. The commentator then refines this: the older scholars' view that 'al-Shari'' is literal for Allah is correct if by 'Shari'' we mean the establisher and originator of the law. However, if we consider the linguistic definition of 'Shari'' as the explainer and conveyer, then it is literal for the Prophet and metaphorical for Allah. This nuanced distinction shows the sophistication of kalam terminology. The commentator endorses the detailed view as the truth.

Correction: The passage shows that 'al-Shari'' can be literal for the Prophet when understood as explainer, and literal for Allah when understood as originator.
