The Wisdom of the Basmala's Letters and Words
Al-Bajuri explains the wisdom behind the 24 letters and 7 words of the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the symbolic significance of the basmala's 24 letters and 7 words, as explained by al-Bajuri and Fakhr al-Razi.

Core Idea
The basmala's letters and words correspond to hours and organs, expiating sins.

Al-Bajuri presents a beautiful spiritual reflection on the basmala, showing that its structure is not arbitrary. Each letter and word carries a meaning that points to detachment from this world and expiation of sins. This lesson unpacks these symbolic correspondences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claims
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that al-Bajuri makes two main symbolic correspondences: for the letters and for the words.
The passage opens with an observation about the dots being omitted.
2
Understand the significance of the missing dots
Al-Bajuri says the letters are all dotted (mu'jam) but written without dots. This indicates that the one who says the basmala should be detached from everything besides Allah.
This is a subtle spiritual lesson embedded in the script.
3
Connect the 24 letters to the 24 hours
The 24 letters correspond to the 24 hours of the day and night. Each letter expiates the sins of one hour. This emphasizes the comprehensive forgiveness granted through the basmala.
4
Connect the 7 words to the 7 organs of sin
Fakhr al-Razi is cited: sin comes from seven organs—ears, eyes, hands, feet, tongue, stomach, and private part. Each word of the basmala expiates the sin of one organ. Additionally, the seven words allude to the seven gates of Hell being closed for the utterer by Allah's grace.
This shows the basmala's power to erase sins from all limbs.
5
Reflect on the overarching message
The basmala is not just a phrase but a means of spiritual purification and protection. Its structure is divinely ordained to cover all times and all aspects of human action.
This lesson deepens one's appreciation for the basmala recited daily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حَرْفٌ مُعْجَمٌ، بل كلُّها مُجَرَّدَةٌ عن النُّقَطِ إشارة إلى أنه ينبغي لمن نَطَقَ بها أن يتجرَّدَ عن كل ما عداه تعالى. وكانت أربعة وعشرين حرفاً؛ لأن الليل والنهار أربع وعشرون ساعةً، فكل حرف يُكَفِّرُ ذنوبَ ساعةٍ. وكانت سَبْعَ كلماتٍ، قال الفَخْرُ الرَّازِيُّ: لأن المعصية لا تكون إلا من الأعضاء السبعة الأذنان والعينان واليدان والرجلان واللسان، والبطن، والفرج فكل كلمة تُكَفِّرُ معصية عُضو، وأيضاً في ذلك إشارة إلى أن أبواب جهنم السبعة عن قائلها بفضل الله ورحمته.
A dotted letter, but all of them are free of dots, indicating that the one who utters them should detach from everything other than Him, exalted. And they were twenty-four letters because the night and day are twenty-four hours, so each letter expiates the sins of an hour. And they were seven words. Fakhr al-Razi said: Because sin only occurs from the seven organs: the two ears, the two eyes, the two hands, the two feet, the tongue, the stomach, and the private part. So each word expiates the sin of an organ. Also in that is an indication that the seven gates of Hell are [closed] for its utterer by the grace of Allah and His mercy.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the wisdom behind the specific number of letters and words in the basmala. The commentator first notes that the letters of the basmala are all dotted letters (mu'jam) but they are written without dots, which is a sign that the one pronouncing them should be detached from everything other than Allah. Then he explains the twenty-four letters correspond to the twenty-four hours of the day and night, each letter expiating the sins of one hour. Then he mentions the seven words correspond to the seven organs through which sin is committed, as reported from Fakhr al-Razi, each word expiating the sin of one organ. Additionally, the seven words allude to the seven gates of Hell being closed for the one who says the basmala by Allah's grace.

Correction: They are deliberately omitted to signal detachment from everything besides Allah.

The Meaning of 'al-Shar' in 'Ja'alaha al-Shar'
Al-Bajuri explains the meaning of 'al-shar' in the hadith phrase 'ja'alaha al-shar'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'al-shar' can refer to the Lawgiver rather than the Law itself, and the distinction between its literal and metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
The term 'al-shar' in theological discourse often points to the Lawgiver, not the Law.

This lesson examines a key linguistic nuance in kalam. When we say 'the Law made it', we must ask: what does 'Law' mean? Al-Bajuri resolves the apparent problem by showing that 'al-shar' can refer to the Lawgiver. We will follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Read the passage and note the apparent contradiction: the phrase 'ja'alaha al-shar' seems to attribute making to 'al-shar' as a set of rulings, which cannot be an agent.
The rulings themselves do not make anything.
2
Understand the Solution
Al-Bajuri explains that the phrase implies an omitted word (تقدير مضاف), so it means 'the Lawgiver made it'. The term 'al-shar' is used metonymically for the Lawgiver.
This interpretation resolves the issue.
3
Distinguish Two Meanings of Lawgiver
Al-Bajuri then distinguishes two meanings of 'al-shari': the establisher of the law (Allah, literally; Prophet, metaphorically) and the explainer/conveyer (Prophet, literally). He endorses this nuanced view.
This detail is considered the correct one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: جَعَلَهَا الشَّرْعُ فيه: أن الشرعَ كالشريعة بمعنى: الأحكام الشرعية، وليست بجاعِلَةٍ. ويجاب بأنه على تقدير مضاف، أي: جَعَلَها صاحب الشرع، أو أن المراد بالشرع: الشارع، وهو الله حقيقةً، والنبي مجازاً. هذا ما قاله الأشياخ قديماً وحديثاً، وهو صحيح بالنظر لكون الشارع بمعنى: المُثْبِت للشرع والموجد له، وأما بالنظر لكون معناه: المبين والمبلّغ - وهو ما يُؤخَذُ من كتب اللغة وغيرها ؛ فهو حقيقة في النبي ، وهذا التفصيل هو الحق إن شاء الله تعالى.
His saying: 'The Law made it' - regarding it: that 'al-shar' (the Law) is like 'al-shari'ah' (the revealed law) in the sense of legal rulings, and it is not a maker. And it is answered that it is on the assumption of an omitted word, i.e., 'the Lawgiver made it', or that what is meant by 'al-shar' is 'al-shari'' (the Lawgiver), which is Allah in reality and the Prophet metaphorically. This is what the shaykhs have said, ancient and modern, and it is correct with regard to the meaning of 'al-shari' as the establisher of the law and its originator. But with regard to its meaning as the explainer and conveyer - and this is what is taken from the books of language and others - then it is literal for the Prophet. And this detail is the truth, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage analyzes the phrase 'ja'alaha al-shar' (the Law made it). The commentator first notes that 'al-shar' (the Law) could be interpreted as the set of legal rulings, which would not be an agent that makes something. To resolve this, he explains that the phrase implies an omitted word: 'the Lawgiver made it'. He then distinguishes between two meanings of 'al-shari' (the Lawgiver): as the establisher of the law (which applies to Allah literally and to the Prophet metaphorically) and as the explainer and conveyer (which applies to the Prophet literally). The commentator endorses this nuanced distinction.

D) The Quran

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ja'alaha al-shar' is correctly interpreted as referring to the Lawgiver, with a nuanced distinction between Allah as establisher (literal) and the Prophet as explainer (literal).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith like 'ja'alaha al-shar', do you automatically assume it refers to Allah, or do you pause to consider the precise meaning? How can you cultivate that habit of careful theological thinking in your daily life?
?
The Prophet is the conveyor and explainer of the law, not its originator. Do you ever speak about the Prophet in a way that blurs this distinction? What can you do to ensure your speech remains precise and reverent?
?
This lesson relies on a subtle linguistic nuance. Are you willing to engage with such details even when they challenge your initial understanding? What would it cost you to ignore these distinctions?

The Mixing of Dhikr: Spiritual Permeation and Miraculous Signs
Al-Bajuri explains the meaning of dhikr mixing with one's being and cites miraculous examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of dhikr 'mixing' with one's flesh and blood – as a metaphor for ingrained habit and as a literal spiritual permeation – and the miraculous examples that support it.

Core Idea
Dhikr can permeate one's entire being, even manifesting on blood and tongue involuntarily.

This passage delves into the profound state of dhikr reaching such intensity that it becomes inseparable from the practitioner. Al-Bajuri offers both metaphorical and literal interpretations, citing extraordinary stories from saints. These narratives illustrate the reality of spiritual permeation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key phrase 'حَتَّى تَمْتَزِجَ' and how it is unpacked.
The passage is dense with meaning; take your time.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
Identify the two senses of 'mixing': first as a kinaya (metaphor) for the dhikr running involuntarily, second as literal intermingling (ikhtilat) and inward permeation (saryan batini).
The second interpretation is supported by the miraculous stories.
3
Examine the miraculous examples
Read the three stories: blood uttering tahlil, tongue uttering in sleep, and blood forming 'Allah'. Reflect on how they demonstrate the reality of dhikr permeating one's being.
These are karamat (miraculous signs) that validate the literal interpretation.
4
Understand 'tawājada'
Note the marginal explanation of 'tawājada' as a spiritual attraction (jadhba) that makes one unconscious of everything but Allah.
This connects to the state of ecstasy in dhikr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: حَتَّى تَمْتَزِجَ مَعَ مَعْنَاهَا بِلَحْمِهِ وَدَمِهِ) غاية في الكثرة السابقة، وهي كناية عن شِدَّةِ التمكَّن بحيث إذا تَرَكَهُ؛ جَرَى على لسانه وقلبه بغير اختياره، ويحتمل أن المراد بذلك الاختلاط والسَّرَيَانُ الباطني؛ لأنه إذا أَكْثَرَ من ذِكْرِها اختلطت بلحمه ودمه، وسَرَتْ في ذلك؛ إذ الإكثار من إجراء الشيء على اللسان يستلزم حضورة في الجنان الذي هو رئيس الأعضاء. ويدلُّ لذلك ما حكي عن بعضهم من تهليل دَمِه حين قطعت رأسه، وعن بعضهم من تهليل لسانه حالة نومه، وقد كان بعضهم يقول: الله دائماً، فتواجَدَ، فأصاب رأسه حَجَرٌ، فَشَجَّهُ وسال دَمُهُ على ض فكتب الله الله فهو امتزاجُ سَريَانِ کسريان الماء في العود الأخضر حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: فَتَواجَدَ :أي حصل له جذبة، وصار لا شعور له مما سوى الله.
His saying: 'until it mixes with its meaning with his flesh and his blood' is a goal for the previously mentioned abundance, and it is a metaphor for extreme firmness such that if he abandons it, it runs on his tongue and heart involuntarily. And it is possible that the intended meaning is the intermingling and inward permeation; because if he increases in remembering it, it becomes mixed with his flesh and blood and permeates therein, since abundance of uttering something on the tongue necessitates presence in the heart, which is the chief of the limbs. And that is supported by what is related about some of them: the tahīl (uttering 'lā ilāha illa llāh') of his blood when his head was cut off, and about some of them: the tahīl of his tongue while sleeping. And one of them used to say 'Allāh' constantly and then became ecstatic (tawājada), and a stone hit his head, wounded him, and his blood flowed, and it wrote 'Allāh, Allāh'. So it is the intermingling of permeation like the permeation of water in a fresh branch. Marginal note by the scholar Aḥmad al-Ajhūrī. His saying: 'fatawājada' means he experienced a spiritual attraction (jadhba) and became unaware of anything other than Allah.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the phrase 'حَتَّى تَمْتَزِجَ مَعَ مَعْنَاهَا بِلَحْمِهِ وَدَمِهِ' from the original text, which describes the goal of abundant dhikr. The commentator clarifies that this is a metaphor (kināya) for the state where dhikr becomes so deeply ingrained that even if one stops consciously, the tongue and heart continue involuntarily. Another interpretation is literal intermingling and inward permeation: abundant utterance of the dhikr causes it to blend with one's flesh and blood, as the tongue's frequent action brings presence to the heart. The commentator supports this with miraculous stories (karāmāt): one saint's blood uttered 'lā ilāha illa llāh' when his head was cut; another's tongue did so in sleep; a third, while constantly saying 'Allāh', was struck by a stone and his blood flowed forming the word 'Allāh'. These are evidence of the dhikr's permeation. A marginal note by al-Ajhūrī explains 'tawājada' as a spiritual ecstasy (jadhba) that makes one unconscious of everything besides Allah.

Key Terms
تمتزج
السريان الباطني
التهليل
التواجد
الجذبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mixing of dhikr with flesh and blood is purely physical like food mixing with blood.
Correction: It is a spiritual reality that may manifest in physical signs (karāmāt), not a biological process. The passage emphasizes inward permeation and presence of the heart.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the phrase 'until it mixes with its meaning with his flesh and his blood' primarily indicate?
A) The dhikr becomes physically part of the body like food.
B) A metaphor for extreme firmness such that dhikr runs on tongue and heart involuntarily.
C) The dhikr must be recited until the person bleeds.
D) The meaning of the dhikr is forgotten once mixed.

Key Takeaway
The mixing of dhikr with one's flesh and blood signifies a profound spiritual state where remembrance permeates one's entire being, exemplified by miraculous events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your blood were to spill right now, what would it write? Is the name 'Allah' so deeply mixed with your being that even your body would testify to Him involuntarily, or would it betray a heart still entangled with the world? What are you willing to change so that your very blood becomes a declaration of His oneness?
?
The saint reached a state where even in sleep his tongue continued to utter 'la ilaha illa Allah'. Does your sleep reflect the condition of your heart? When your conscious guard is down, what thoughts and words arise? Are you at peace that your unconscious is filled with remembrance, or does it expose a love for other than Him? Start today by saturating your waking hours with dhikr so that your nights may also be sanctified.
?
The marginal note says that 'tawajada' means he became unaware of anything other than Allah. Have you ever tasted such a state, even for a moment? If not, what is holding you back? Your attachment to this life? Your pride? Your lack of yearning? This lesson shows that such a state is possible and that it leaves a mark even on the body. Will you seek it with sincerity, or are you content with a superficial dhikr that never reaches your heart?

The Definition of Tawfīq: Al-Juwaynī vs. Al-Ashʿarī
Definition of tawfīq and comparison of two scholarly definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of tawfīq (divine facilitation) in Islamic theology and why al-Juwaynī's definition is preferred over al-Ashʿarī's.

Core Idea
Tawfīq is the creation of obedience, not just the capacity for it.

This lesson examines a key theological term: tawfīq, or divine facilitation. The commentator begins by explaining the grammatical restriction in the phrase 'with Allah is tawfīq,' then presents two competing definitions from major Ashʿarī theologians. By comparing them, we see why one definition is considered more sound based on the case of the disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the grammatical point about restriction (ḥaṣr).
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition by al-Juwaynī (خلق الطاعة) and by al-Ashʿarī (خلق قدرة الطاعة). Understand the difference.
3
Understand the objection and reply
The objection is that al-Ashʿarī's definition applies to disbelievers too. The reply refines what 'capacity' means. Follow the logic.
The passage ends mid-reply, but the point is clear.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for understanding Allah's role in human actions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَبِاللهِ التَّوْفِيقُ أي لا بغيره فتقديمُ الجار والمجرور لإفادة الحصر. والتوفيق لغةً: التأليف بين شيئين فأكثر. وشرعاً : خَلْقُ الطاعة في العبد. كذا عَرَّفَه إمامُ الحَرَمَيْنِ، وهو أولى من تعريف الأشعري له: بأنه خَلْقُ قُدْرَةِ الطاعة في العبد؛ لأن خَلْقَ القدرة على الطاعة موجود في الكافر مع أنه غيرُ مُوَفِّقٍ. ودُفعَ ذلك: بأنه ليس المراد بالقدرة: سلامة الآلات حتى يَردَ ما ذُكِرَ، بل المراد بها:
His saying: 'and with Allah is tawfīq (divine facilitation)' means not with other than Him; thus the fronting of the prepositional phrase is for the purpose of restriction (ḥaṣr). Tawfīq linguistically means: bringing together two or more things. And in the Sacred Law (sharʿan): the creation of obedience (khalq al-ṭāʿa) in the servant. Thus defined it by Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), and it is more appropriate than the definition of al-Ashʿarī of it: that it is the creation of the capacity (qudra) for obedience in the servant, because the creation of the capacity for obedience exists in the disbeliever while he is not given tawfīq. And that is refuted: by saying that what is meant by capacity is not the soundness of instruments (salāmat al-ālāt) such that the mentioned objection would apply, but rather what is meant is...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the term tawfīq (divine facilitation). The commentator first explains the grammatical emphasis of restriction in the phrase. Then he gives the linguistic meaning: bringing together. Then the legal (sharʿī) meaning, quoting Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwaynī: it is the creation of obedience in the servant. He notes that this definition is superior to al-Ashʿarī's definition, which defines tawfīq as the creation of the capacity for obedience. The reason is that capacity for obedience is also present in disbelievers, yet they are not given tawfīq. However, this objection is countered by explaining that the capacity meant by al-Ashʿarī is not merely soundness of instruments (which disbelievers also have) but rather the capacity that leads to actual obedience.

Key Terms
التوفيق
خلق الطاعة
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawfīq means giving the capacity to obey.
Correction: Tawfīq is the actual creation of obedience, as per al-Juwaynī's definition, not merely the capacity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn's definition, what is tawfīq?
A) The creation of capacity for obedience
B) The soundness of instruments
C) The creation of obedience in the servant
D) Bringing together two things

Key Takeaway
Tawfīq is specifically the creation of obedience in the servant, as defined by al-Juwaynī, and is distinct from the mere capacity for obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to do good, do you typically blame your circumstances or your own weakness? This lesson teaches that even the capacity to obey is a creation from Allah, and tawfīq is the actual creation of obedience. How will you now beg Allah for tawfīq in every act of worship, starting with your next salah?
?
The disbeliever has the physical capacity to pray, fast, and give charity, yet he is not given tawfīq. Think of one sin or act of disobedience you struggle with. Do you have the ability to stop? Why haven't you? What does this tell you about your need for Allah's tawfīq to actually change?
?
If tawfīq is solely from Allah (as the phrase 'and with Allah is tawfīq' indicates), then where is your arrogance? Right now, reflect on a good deed you did today. Did you thank Allah for creating that obedience in you, or did you attribute it to your own effort? Commit to saying 'al-hamdu lillah' for every act of obedience you perform today.

The Refutation of Mixing as a Bodily Property
Commentator distinguishes juxtaposition from true mixing to refute an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mixing in the context of divine attributes does not imply bodily mixture, and the distinction between true mixing and juxtaposition.

Core Idea
Mixing can be mere juxtaposition without interpenetration.

Sanusi's text raises an objection: mixing is a property of bodies, so how can we speak of mixing in relation to Allah? The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between true mixing (like water and honey) and juxtaposition (like two eyes placed side by side). This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the translation. Notice the contrast between two types of mixing.
The word 'چشم' is Persian for 'eye'.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the statement that is being refuted and the commentator's counterexample.
3
Understand the distinction
Think about why juxtaposition is not the same as true bodily mixing. How does this protect divine transcendence?
This relates to the concept of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا امتزاج مماشةٍ كامتزاج چشم بآخر، فاندفع ما يقال: إن الامتزاج من خواص الأجسام كامتزاج الماء بالعسل.
(قوله: فَإِنَّهُ يَرَى لَهَا ... إلخ) عِلَّةٌ لقوله: (فعلى العاقل أن يكثر من ذكرها ... إلخ).
(قوله: مَا لَا يَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ حَصْرِ أَي تحت عَدَدٍ محصورٍ، وهذا كناية عن المبالغة في الكثرة.
There is no mixing of juxtaposition like the mixing of one eye with another, so what is said is repelled: that mixing is a property of bodies like the mixing of water with honey. (His statement: 'for he sees for them... etc.') is a cause for his statement: 'so it is upon the intelligent person to increase in mentioning them... etc.'. (His statement: 'what does not enter under enumeration') i.e. under a limited number, and this is a metaphor for exaggeration in abundance.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage contains three separate explanatory comments on Sanusi's text. The first comment refutes the claim that mixing (imtizāj) is a property of bodies. The author distinguishes between a true mixing of substances (like water and honey) and a mere juxtaposition (mumāshshah) without interpenetration, using the mixing of one eye with another as an example—a phenomenon that does not involve bodily mixture. Thus, the argument that mixing is inherently bodily is rejected. The second comment explains that the phrase 'for he sees for them' is a causal connection (ʿillah) to the earlier imperative that the intelligent person should frequently remember those secrets and wonders. The third comment clarifies the phrase 'what does not enter under enumeration' (ḥaṣr), meaning it is beyond counting, and that this is a metaphorical expression (kināyah) for abundant blessings.

Key Terms
امتزاج
مماشة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All mixing implies bodily mixture and interpenetration.
Correction: Juxtaposition (mumāshshah) is a type of non-bodily mixing that does not require physical interpenetration, as shown by the example of two eyes.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the mixing of water and honey and the mixing of one eye with another according to the gloss?
A) Water and honey mix by juxtaposition, the example by true mixing
B) Water and honey mix truly, while the example uses juxtaposition without interpenetration
C) Both are examples of bodily mixing
D) The example is about spiritual mixing

Key Takeaway
In theological discourse, mixing can refer to juxtaposition without interpenetration, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes like mercy and justice, do you ever imagine them as physically blended or overlapping? How does the distinction between juxtaposition and interpenetration correct your mental picture of the Divine? What step will you take to ensure you think of God's unity without reducing it to physical categories?
?
In your daily life, you often combine roles—Muslim, professional, family member. Do you mistake mere juxtaposition (doing these side by side) for true integration (uniting them under one purpose)? How can you move from juxtaposition to a genuine fusion of intentions? Will you identify one area today where you will intentionally merge your actions for God?
?
Consider your worship: do you 'mix' your intention with worldly desires, or merely place them side by side? Is your prayer truly a unified act for God, or is it juxtaposed with distractions? What one change can you make before your next prayer to remove the distractions and present a pure heart?
